LITERARY NOTES.
Buckle and His Critics.
Mr. John M. Robertson’s book on “Buckle
and His Critics ” (Swan, Sonnenscliein and
Co.) is his most memorable work to date.
Though his previous essays in literary and
sociological criticism have been of consider-
able volume and noticeable ingenuity, they
are comparatively light artillery, exploding
with a proportionately insignificant “boom.’
Now, for the first time, Mr. Robertson
canvasses for readers’suffrages on the serious
historical plane.
• * * *
Mr. Robertson’s enthusiasm for Buckle
may be likened to that of those lettered
archaeologists who roam through f%ypt, and
Greece, and Asia Minor, disinterring the
noble statues of antiquity, and clearing away
from many a majestic monument the dust
and dfibris collected to the discredit of care-
less or contemptuous ages. His zeal attacks
all the critics of his Master, great or small,
English or foreign—or as many of them as
one man can possibly attack. He gives them
back scorn for scorn, and rending for railing.
Do they jeer at Buckle’s style ? from their
own style he points their barbs anew : do
they challenge Buckle’s matter ? he confutes
them laboriously, point by point, out of their
own mouths, or one another’s mouths, or by
reference to the original authorities. Such
devotion is not rare in literature, but such
capacity to direct such devotion is not
common.
* * * *
“Confutes” —say rather “attempts to
confute.” Sometimes the honors of the
tussle do not seem as well-secured to Mr.
Robertson as they might be. When critic
has attacked critic, rolled critic over, bitten
critic, taken critic’s bone, and sent critic-
howling into the vista with critic’s tail
between critic’s legs, the performance is n >t
always, in Mr. Robertson’s book, so vivid
and lifelike as to settle the query where Mr.
Robertson’s tail would have been in the other
fellow’s book. Not that Mr. Robertson
denies that there are spots on the sun. He
sees many spots ; but complains that what
Buckle’s critic - have alleged o lie spots are
really not sun-spots at alt. but the dark areas
of their own vision, obscured by ignorance
and prejudice. He too is a critic of Buckle.
The differences between himself and those
whom he attacks are generally expressed in
vitally different conclusions : a great man
with demerits —a small man with merits.
• • • •
Yet Buckle was really great. His histori-
cal torso is magnificent, and it is war—war
on the pedants and dryasdusts, the borrowers
and pigeonholers lost in a maze of details
' and disconnected tags of inferences ; war on
•he recorders of battles and sieges, reigns
a Lid treaties, who pin down epochs like
entomological specimens ; war on the mere
s.y lists and-rhetoricians, on the men of one
age, or one language, or one country, on the
writers who stand an inch away from their
subject, and lose all sense of perspective or
proportion. These have their uses ;in a
minor sense they are historians ; but
they do not write the history which Buckle
aimed to write and began to write. It is not
for what he has done, though that is much,
but for what he suggests that Buckle is so
valuable. Every page is luminous. And
his capacity for generalisation—the highest
intellectual capacity—was almost Newtonian
in force and scope. Where other men
floundered among facts he sprang at once to
the law. Where other men’s minds walked
painfully among events from valley to hill
and down to valley again, he flew above
them as a bird and saw the whole configura-
tion of the landscape. He was the first
historian of note to discard the anthropo-
centric myth, and to write with a sense of
man’s relation to the universe continually
guiding his pen. He hated narrow theo-
logians ; he despised narrow schoolmen ; and
schoolmen and theologians have bitterly
reciprocated his scorn and hate. He
was one ; they were many ; and their
voices and pens have out-cried him.
But what historian of to-day has his mental
equipment 1 who could put his hand to the
plough where Buckle’s dropped it ? Errors
Buckle made, and many—it is given to no
man to receive all knowledge for his pro-
vince ; his theories have been altered in the
progress of science ; yet his book has influ-
enced and continues to influence the spirit
of his age as inuch, perhaps, as any other
book of the century. Buckle was a pioneer
in wide fields of thought, and the very critics
who attack him employ weapons which he
was among the first to point and use.
* * * *
Mr. Robertson’s book was a very necessary
book. It is tedious occasionally , occasion-
ally its comment seems trivial; it almost needs
as much enthusiasm to read it straight
through as was needed to write it; but it
finally clears away many misconceptions,
disposes of many ill-founded attacks, and
takes its place with Mr. Huth’s life on the
permanent shelf of Buckle literature. And
in the assurance of this result, Mr. Robert-
son will find repayment for all his labor.

Short, Stories.
The train which five daysa week at 5.25p.m.
leaves Sydney for the Blue Mountains is
known as “the honeymoon train”—so many
newly-wedded couples travel by it. Think
with what a wealth of precious hopes that train
is freighted ! When Driver Cupid touches
the starting lever, how ardently the steam
rushes into the embracing cylinders ! in
what fiery paroxysms the sparks fly from the
heated chimney ! what vistas of poetry and
romance wreathe themselves into the hurry-
ing smoke ! The grimy stoker fires the torch
of Hymen ; the guard, locking with a parental
smile the doors of a dozen engaged compart-
ments, has the air of saying. “Hi , you, my
children ! ” to a family whose potentialities
would stagger the heaviest father of the
melodramatic stage. Then consider ....
(idle rhymesters may enlarge upon the theme
in 24 lines of any metre, divided at pleasure.
The best verses will be printed).
The story of William Gay, whose sonnets
were printed the other day at Bendigo, Vic.,
is a pathetic one. Like the late Francis
Adams, he seems running a race with Time to
get one more book into the light of day be-
fore he is beckoned away to the Shades.
He lies in a private hospital, fed with a
spoon, dictating his verses to a nurse ; and he
has not been out of bed since last October.
His life has been all tragedy. He began if
badly in Glasgow 30 years ago —the odd
child in a family of the severest Scotch type.
As a lad he separated himself from his un-
genial home by winning a bursary at
Glasgow University. Here he lived on next
to nothing ; he was delicate to begin with ;
and the seeds of consumption took root and
flourished. He made his way to London,
always trying to write, and often enduring
semi-starvation. Menaced health drove him
to look to Australia. He undertook to work
his way out on a sailing ship ; fell sick on
board ; and was nursed by friendly pas-
sen ges. Since reaching Melbourne he has
made poor living at journalism—for the
last two years in hospital.
The sadness of Mr. Gay’s circumstances
may be read in the sympathetic criticism
which his sonnets have received ; but, taken
together, they form a strong body of work,
with something pf Wordsworth’s grave sim-
plicity, of Milton’s religious elevation.
Singly, with rare exceptions, they lack
poetry, workmanship, distinction. Mr. Gay
writes easily and not unmelodiously; the
sonnet-form seems to suit him ; but he
has little grace or fervour, and for the most
part he is merely gilding commonplace.
There is so much inaccuracy in the sheet of
eulogies which Mr. Gay issues that it seems
■’necessary to say this, which detracts nothing
from the impression of earnestness, sincerity,
and sober dignity which his Muse leaves
upon the reader.
The essays and reviews of the late C. H.
Pearson, formerly Vic. Minister for Educa-
tion, and author of “ National Life and
Character ” —a very suggestive and valuable
book -are shortly to be published.
In S.M. Herall* of April 4, “L.” brings
a crowd of noted fictional characters to a
conve”sazione. Robert Elsmere talks to
Elsie Venner ; Mr. Potter of Texas to
Princess Napraxine ; and so on. The idea
has been neatly exploited by Landor and
Lang and a score of others ; but “ L.’
crowds all the interest out of her (?) stage,
and her article degenerates to a mere
catalogue.
M‘G. : Uin true = oo; u in cube = yoo;
and yoo , as pronounced, isn’t ee-oo (two
sounds) —it’s simply yoo (one sound). And
even if it were two sounds, then on your
own shoving the u in true (one sound) can-
not possibly have “the same sound” as the
nin cube (alleged two sounds); though the
defective English language gives those differ-
ent sounds the same symbol. Where in
Pitman did you find cube spelt lc-ee-00-b, as
you phonetise it ?
Gordon, dedicating “ Bush Ballads and
Galloping Rhymes” to Whyte Melville, in
lines of which the merit seems rarely to be
recognised, said they came from a land—
Where, tire and fierce drought on her tresses,
Insatiable summer oppresses
Sere woodlands and sad wildernesses
And faint flocks and herds.
Sydney Star, wishing to “quote poetry”
appropriate to R.A. Society’s show, says it
comes at a time when an Australian poet
tells us :
Insatiate autumn oppresses
Sad woodlands and sere wildernesses,
And faint flocks and herds.
Is this ignorance ? or guilt ?
Hood’s “Song of the Shirt” is said to have
tripled the circulation of London Punch.
Litterateur Saintsbury, newly-appointed
Professor of English at Edinburgh Uni-
versity, after speaking of the slovenly
rhymes of Elizabeth Barrett Browning, goes
on to say, “Nay, more, despite her Greek,
she will rhyme ‘idyll’ with ‘middle.’”
Now, Prof. Maccallum, of Sydney Uni-
versity, would defend Mrs. Browning’s (and
his own) pronunciation of this word. Good
dictionaries give both “Idyll and “idyll.'
Probably as many average people pronounce
the word one way as pronounce it the other.
And it is useless pedantry to pit etymology
against custom : how many 20-year-old
words are there in English pronounced
now as when first introduced to the language ?
Further, the slovenly language of Saints-
bury himself is a byword with hostile critics
Kipling’s books, being mostly -nan’s meat
and virile, are not stocked in Melb. Public
Lending Library. Zola is only admitted in
the form of R. H. Sherard s biography.
George Moore’s novels are blacklisted—like-
wise Ouida and A Yellow Aster ; an-
“ The Heavenly Twins ’ weep tears on the
door-mat and are denied.
Fergus Hume’s “Mystery of a Hansom
Cab ” has actually reached' its 377th thou-
sand, and the half-million looms imminent.
This should be a sufficient answer to Marie
Corelli and similar.writers who conceive that
popularity necessarily means merit. It
merely means that, in Lincolnian phrase,
for the people who like this sort of thing
(e.g., ' Sorrows of Satan’) this is plainly
the sort of thing those people like.
W. T. Stead, in the Review ok Reviews,
becomes devil’s advocate for the discredited
Rhodes. He admits, in effect, that the es-
sence of Rhodes’s power is his talent for
corruption, but seems to suggest that s :eh
peccadilloes are a necessary part of ; -v u
men,” who must not be held too closely to
ordinary people’s rule-of-thumb honesty.
Stead would make a very fair Loyola for a,
new order of Nonconformist Jesuits.
Gladstone is writing for the North
American Review a series of articles on
“The Future Life, and the Condition of
Man Therein,” which, in view of their
originality, value, scope, and enlightenment,
might have been penned by a mediaeval
bishop. It is one of the curiosities of modern
science that when a British ex-Premier
founds a scientific theory on a careful induc-
tion of Bible texts the paradox should be
accepted as a commonplace. True, Glad-
stone does not rely wholly on Scriptural
evidence for proof of “man’s future life.’
He refers to the opinions of Barnabas.
Clement of Rome, Ignatius, Hernias, Poly-
carp, and Clement of Alexandria ; and notes
that the attempt to remove Rufinus. Arno
bius, and Athen.igoras from the blofid of
valuable witnesses on liis side is by no means
satisfactory. Any unprejudiced person will
feel glad to have this assurance about
Rufinus. 41

T. O O K!
BEFORE YOU
T. E a 3F» !
When you go out shopping, consider, think
of the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of a pretty show ; there is
B, whose wiles are innumerable; and C,
who says that he is the man for your money ;
and so on, throughout a list as long as
George-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned ; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “There be land-sharks and water-
sharks,” and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round—
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer—for you to pie id : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

A voice front the Lake Height,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
* don’t want no pioneerin’: take me back to
Collin wood,
Where’s the house that holds my heaven, an’ my
heart-strings, an’ my blood !
lake me where the whirring cable-cars slide plea-
sant ways to town,
Where there’s tea that’s tea , and real watered milk
to wash it down !
I arn sittin’ on my reefin’ claim at Day Dawn,
Forrestville,
Watchin’ pet goannas eatin’ flies, an’ gettin’ of
their fill,
Cursin’ that accursed quartz-blow, which I thought
so fine of late,
Cursin’ underlay, an’ shaft, an’ cursin’ quartz an’
cursin’ slate.
This my claim is bought up by the agent, come
from Adelaide :
(Adelaide ! That’s goin’ to Melbourne, where I
learnt my only trade.)
I’ve bin here a blessed twelvemonth, spendin’ grub
an’ sweat an’ fuse
In this hole the Ad’laide agent’s goin’ to win on
or to lose;
Livin’ on tinned dog an’ pudden, an’ preserves of
fruit, an’ such,
Carrots canned, an’ pears in syrup, an’ I hate ’em
overmuch.
Dog and mutton, canned and curried, soon in-
crease the sum of years—
An’ I’Ve got bung eyes, an’ sore lips, an’ Barcoo
rot on my ears.
No more blasted pioneerin—gimme back the job
I ’ad
Makin’ boots in Melbourne fact’ries—I was
strugglin’ then an’ glad !
An’ I got my pay on Fridays—it was just two
quid a week ; '
But my missus ! She can manage—she could
make that two pound speak.
When I left her Friday momin’s for the work I
had to do,
She would say, ‘ ‘ I’ve got a deener—would you like
a carrot stew ? ”
Wouldn’t I just like to hear her say them blessed
words just now :
I’d give half the blessed purchase an’ I’d stay
home, anyhow 1 ,
There’s the kids a-playin’ dollies with a towel and
a stick,
And at night they lisp a prayer for “ dad ” —O
Lord, it makes me sick !
An’ I’m sittin’ here all lonely watchin’ the
goannas’ fun,
And the great salt lakes below me lie a mirage in
the sun.
Lakes for scores of miles and hundreds, salt and
gypsum, fling their sheen
To the rainless sky above them; an’ I crave for
the Yan Yean!
Lurid fires from the condensers where the steam
becomes a tear,
An’ the water’s sold per gallon at the price of
Melbourne beer.
Here the vagrant wind goes moaning ever on
strange tracks at fault,
Sweeping off the seas made by the blue deceitful-
ness of salt;
Gimlet, salmon-gum, and samphire, white-ant-
eaten in the dark—
Oh ! there ain’t a tree in cooee like the gums at
Studley Park.
An’ there’s wide streets East, my oath, yes ! Here
we walk in single file,
And the thirsty land has tragedies to each and
every mile. ,
I don’t want no blasted gnamma-holes—give ine a
Melbourne tap ;
Gimme dust that isn’t greasy; gimme trees that
have some sap;
Gimme tracks where there’s no Afghans an’ no
roarin’ camel cow —
Gimme tracks down to Fremantle an’ I’ll get home
anyhow.
We ain’t got no reefs in Melbourne, but we’ve got
cheap meat and fruit,
Not gold-weighted curried mutton, carried here
per blasted boot;
You can earn and earn like thunder, but the old
stone-broker comes
When you’ve anted five-and-sixpence for a bottle
full of plums. .
You’ve to travel as a swamper, if you am t a
wealthy jay;
For horses drink ten shillings and eat thirty bob
a day.
Come on, Mr. Ad’laide gent.! pay up the balance;
meet the claim ; . .
I am goin’ back to Melbourne, for this isn t worth
the game.
Gimme back my homely heaven in that stinkin’
Melbourne street;
Make me stone-broke near the people who have
poverty made sweet;
I will leave these lakes for ever, never hear a camel
roar,
Never hear the Afghan “ Hooshta ! hauling nose-
pegs at the bore ;
Never work the crazy windlass, never, never,
never more , . . .
Gaze on seas-of salt and lophet which have never
any shore ; , , , ,
Never kid the English Johnny, when lie calls to
see the claim, , . T . „
Tell him ironstone is gold, until bai Jove .
grows mighty tame.
I will sit in some small cottage, with my wife and
children by, ,
And we’ll sit and love for ever till the time shah
come to die; , , , ,
No more shall I have my camp-place fenced by
mountain-range and sky,
No more hear the lake-wind wailing die down in
the samphire sigh ,
No more shall the tinned-meat feed me ; I shall
have a steak that’s good ; _ ...
I don’t want no pioneerin—I am off to Coiling-
wood !
Randolph Bedford.
The Day Dawn, Lake Cowan (W.A.)

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 8000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly unaerstand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, APRIL 18, 1890.
The Insurance Difficulty.
The old question about the advisability or
otherwise of extending the operations of the
A.M.P. Society to England and other coun-
tries outside Australasia is again cropping
up. The A.M.P. Society is a national insti-
tution ; it is the largest life office, save one,
belonging to the British empire ; and the
proposal to extend its business abroad has
excited a lot of interest on several previous
occasions. The final result of the controversy
is hardly a matter of doubt; sooner or later, if
present conditions continue, the A.M.P., and
other societies as well, will have to spread
their operations over Great Britain, and pro-
bably many other places, in their own
defence. Australia as a field for life-insur-
ance business has decayed very much of late
years, through excessive competition, reduc-
tion in rates of interest, depreciation in the
value of securities, lack of prosperity among
the people, diminution in the rate of increase
of population, lack of safe investments for
the enormous accumulation of insurance
funds ; and various other influences. Some of
these troubles arise from natural causes, and
more of them from the deranged policy of
the various Governments. And the result
of them all is that the boom days of large
bonuses and constantly-increasing business
have departed—for the time, at all events.
* * *
There are at present ten local offices doing
life business in Australasia, to say nothing
of three old proprietary institutions which
have practically retired from the field. There
are also one English and three American
offices at work, making fourteen in all. This
means that there are nearly twice as many
offices in proportion to population as in the
United Kingdom, and that there is a great
deal more insurance-office than the popula-
tion can reasonably support. As the popu-
lation increases, it is probable that other
English and American offices will swarm into
the country ; in the absence of any re-
strictive legislation, the possibilities in this
direction are absolutely unlimited. This
excessive competition means that there are far
more different-sots.of"head.offices,and branch
offices to be supported than there need be,
also more different managers, directors,
actuaries, office staffs, sets of agents and
other appurtenances to be maintained than
the business actually requires-, and all these
needless extra charges have to be paid by the
policy-holder in some shape or other.
Further, when 14 different lots of agents are
hunting after the available business of a
small population, which seven of them could
do to the satisfaction of everybody con-
cerned, it means that business is harder to
get, and the agent has to be paid on a larger
scale for getting it to enable him to live on
the game, and this extra expense has also to
be charged to the policy-holder, as the in-
dividual who pays everything in the end.
The natural result of all this pressure is that
expense rates are far higher than they need
be, therefore the societies have either to
charge higher rates or pay smaller bonuses
than would otherwise be necessary, and
either way the insurer suffers.
♦ * * *
If the invasion by British and American
offices and the multiplication of local ones
are allowed to continue, the time will ulti-
mately arrive when the Australian societies
will have to skirmish for business in Britain
and America to make up for what they lose
in their own country. One—-the Colonial
Mutual--has already spread itself over the
United Kingdom and South Africa, and has
done remarkably well by it, but if all the
Australian societies did likewise they would
intensify the competition to such an extent
that there would probably be very little
profit in it—for some of them, at all events.
The shortest and most effective remedy for
the trouble is for the Australian Govern-
ments to license the various offices now doing
business in this country; to prohibit any
office doing business here without a license ;
and to enact that no more licenses shall be
granted—except to a State insurance office —
until the population of Australia and Tas-
mania has reached, say, 6,000,000. After
that figure is attained one new society might
be licensed for each additional million or
more of population. This scheme (which is
altogether apart from any question of State
audit or State supervision in any shape) would
inflict no injury upon any one who has
any serious right to be considered. The
people who start a new insurance society
under present conditions certainly do not do
it to fill a public want, for all the public want
which exists in this direction is already filled
to overflowing ; therefore the assumption is
that they do it solely to supply comfortable
billets for themselves at the expense of the
public, who are already supporting more
managers and directors than are at all neces-
sary. Therefore, this paper declines to re-
gard these—the only possible sufferers—as
persons who deserve any consideration what-
ever.
* * * *
As regards the public, the license system,
on the lines suggested, should be wholly
beneficial. There is sufficient rivalry among
the fourteen live offices at present doing busi-
ness in the country to prevent serious risk
of their becoming fossilised. By the gradual
reduction in the intensity of the competition,
which must follow as population increases, it
would be possible to conduct the business at
much less expense than at present, and this
would mean much more profit for the in-
surers. It would diminish the tendency, which
is beginning to appear in certain. quarters,
to attract business by cutting rates danger-
ously low, and by holding out estimates of
impossible profits in the future ; in other
words, it would reduce the possibilities of
dishonesty by making it possible for all the
existing institutions to live on an honest
basis. And it would enable them to live
honestly on local business alone, whereas,
as things are, it seems increasingly probable
that they will have to spread out into distant
lands to make up for the difficulties incurred
through excessive competition at home. As
a proof that the competition is excessive :
Among the six large life offices doing busi-
ness in Australia, one—a sound and highly-
reputable concern showed a continuous
decline in its premium income every year
from 1886 to 1893—the last year to which
the tabulated statement refers ; another
showed a decline in ’92 and ’93; another
showed a falling-off in ’9O, ’92 and ’93;
a fourth also showed a declining pre-
mium income for three years ; and the re-
maining two showed a considerable drop
for one year, at all events. Moreover,
though the expense rate ought to diminish
as business grows larger, it was actually more
per cent, in 1892 than it was in 1881, though
business had more than doubled. Under
these conditions there is good reason for the
State to shut down on any further aggrava-
tion of the evil. Life assurance is a business
of national importance—not a thing to be
left to the go-as-you-please methods of free
competition.
* * * *
The chief reason why the Australian
societies have shown such excellent results
in the past, despite the high expense rate
caused by too much assurance society in pro-
portion to population, is the remarkably high
rate of interest realised on their invested
funds. But while the competition shows no
signs of diminution, unless the State inter-
feres in some decisive fashion, and conse-
quently the expense rate declines very slowly,
the interest realised has been diminishing
rather rapidly, and seems likely to fall a good
deal lower and thereafter to remain down
permanently. The Australian societies have
been confined by the circumstances of the
country to three principal modes of invest-
ment —freehold property, loans on mortgages,
and loans on the security of their own poli-
cies. Freehold property pays a much smaller
percentage than it did, and through the
steady increase of taxation and other ciicuni-
stances it seems likely to remain that way ,
moreover, by reason of the practical cessa-
tion of the great borrowed-money boom, the
selling-value shows a decided tendency to
fall instead of constantly rising as in the old
financial-drunk days. The interest on mort-
gages has fallen, partly through general stag-
nation, partly through the influx of foreign
money from the glutted markets of Eng ant,
partly through general distrust, partly through
increased competition of banks and builc -
ing societies and mortgage companies, partly
because the insurance societies themselves
have now £*22,000,000 to invest whereas,
fifteen years ago, they had only £4,000,000.
And it seems likely to fall further and still
more violently through a continuation of all
these causes, and also through the spread of
the State Land Bank system, which is now
only in its infancy, and may yet expand in-
definitely, and which is expressly designed
to reduce the rate of mortgage-interest.
The third great resource —loans on the sur-
render values of policies—has hitherto been
a most profitable investment, and one which
the societies had practically all to themselves;
but these securities are so liquid and so re-
liable that, sooner or later, other financial
institutions will begin to compete in this
branch of investment at a low rate of inter-
est, and badly damage this resource also.
Owing to the insane and unpatriotic policy
of selling almost all Government and good
municipal debentures in London, there is
practically nothing else in which the local
societies can invest their money on anything
like a large scale. Therefore, they are con-
fronted with the practical certainty that the
large profits hitherto realised upon their
accumulated funds are, to some extent, a
thing of the past, and, unless the loss can be
made up in the future by a large reduction
in working expenses, bonuses will never
again be paid on the magnificent scale of
former years. The only way in which, so
far as appears at present, working expenses
can be reduced is by a diminution of the pre-
sent excessive competition, and no effectual
method of doing that has yet been suggested.
Therefore The Bulletin proposes that the
State should closely restrict the number of
societies in proportion to population, and this,
it believes, is the only remedy that has the
.slightest prospect of meeting the case. This
proposal has nothing whatever to do with the
necessity for a State insurance office or for a
State audit of private offices; the adoption of
both these reforms would not diminish in
any way the need for the proposed restric-
tion. The said restriction is practically an
adaptation, in a milder form, of the Scotch
banking law, which practically prohibits the
creation of new banks in that country; there-
fore those conservative individuals who desire
at all times to act strictly on British prece-
dent have here a good example to follow.
The South Australian Referendum
The obligatory referendum, involving the
decision of a public question by vote of the
whole body of electors, is not yet included by
Parliamentary enactment among the means of
government of any Australasian province.
Nothing, however, prevents the application of
the referendum principle at any time towards
the settlement of any public matter in dispute ;
and the South Australian Government proposes
to employ it at the general election on April
25. The questions set the electors are :
1. Do you favor the continuance of the present system
of education in the State schools ?
. 2. Do you favor the introduction of religious instruction
in the State schools during school hours?
3. Do you favor the payment of the capitation grant to
denominational schools ?
The local R.C. Archbishop is reported to have
advised his flock to answer “No” to the first
two questions, and “ Yes ” to the third. (The
R.C. church wants the whole hog or none)
I’lie local Anglican Bishop is said to recommend
“ No ” t 0 the first question, and “ Yes ” to the
second and third. (The Anglican church would
prefer the whole animal, but will take half if
it can get no more). The Presbyterian Assembly
considers that the first question should be left
unanswered, the second answered “ Yes ” and
the third answered “No.” (The Presbyterians
don t care for pork all the time, but will take
a little bit if they’re allowed to choose their
own pet morsels.) The Bulletin advises in-
telligent men and women to answer “ Yes ” to
the first question, and “ No ” to the second and
1 iii / 1 , HE Bulleti * thinks the sectarian hog
should be kept penned in the sectarian sty.)
Most Austrahans have convinced themselves
that State aid to religion, in any form, is a
thoroughly undesirable thing. There are
always, however, a number of waverers, and
a number of people whose opinions are in pro-
cess of formation, and for the sake of these it
seems expedient to range anew some of the
reasons which have influenced this conviction
It may be premised that the result of the
coming vote will not directly affect the South
Australian educational system. That system
permits the ministers of any recognised
ft" * 0 , gl ! e religious instruction In
the State schools, out of school hours to
children whose parents desire it. The com
promise is one employed in other Austrian
provinces, and is found practically harmles"
since ministers are nearlv . , ess ,
children too indifferent, o give R effbc

questions are bound to be inconclusive and

» * * •
Nevertheless, the first question should put
every democratic voter on his or her mettle.
The first argument that suggests itself against
State aid to religion in Australia is that every
Australian province has tried the system and
found it injurious. In province after province
the subsidy to religious schools has been with-
drawn, and a system of practically secular
State education substituted. In Westralia
religious bigotry found its last retreat, yet even
Westralia has now withdrawn State aid from
denominational schools. Why? Did these con-
sistent changes from a religious to a secular
system follow' one upon the other without grave
reason ? Is it wise to recur in Australia to a
system that has already in Australia been tried
everywhere, and over and over, and has always
and every where been found wanting?
* * * *
Universal history suggests the answer.
Alliance between Church and State has in
every country, in all ages, proved injurious
to the State. Italy, the scene of a great
temporal sovereignty acquired by the head of a
Christian religion, at last purchased its
release from that sovereignty with the blood of
some of its best patriots, and Austria and the
Papacy fought together to destroy the liberties
of the nascent nation. To this day, so bitter
was the experience of priestcraft gained in the
years preceding 1861, the name of priest is for
educated Italy a symbol of contempt. Yet the
Papacy has never ceased endeavoring to extend
its temporal supremacy ; it would dearly love to
win in Australia an influence to replace that
which it has lost in Europe. The object of
the Catholic Church is its own ascendancy:
its chief means is the enslavement of
the human mind. Hence its zeal in all
countries to undertake the entire education of
youth, and implant a devotion to its interests,
a subservience to its instructions, which can
never be eradicated. Hence its horror of the
Australian State schools, which teach facts and
truths regardless of their bearing on religious
dogma ; hence its continual segregation of the
children of its own flock from the dangerous
influences of free speech, and free thought, and
free light. It is the Catholic Church that keeps
alive denominational education in Australia:
the efforts of the Protestant sects are meagre
and half-hearted by comparison.
» * * *
It is unwise for South Australia to help the
Catholic Church, or any other church, to sow
the seeds of disunion among its citizens. When
the State encourages a system which sets apart
the Catholic boy from the Anglican boy, and
the Presbyterian girl from the girl wdio goes to
the State school, it does itself a grievous wrong.
The only school recognised by the State should
be a National school—a school where children
of all creeds, of all social conditions, can meet
on the same plane, and learn that the State
exacts from them all the same social duty, and
grants to them all the same social privileges.
In this country, at this day, much of the bitter-
ness of old religious feuds has been forgotten.
Why fan them into new flame ?
* * * . *
It is not the business of the State to teach
any variety of religion. The general principles
of social morality are not the property of any
religious body, and are not contained in anyone
religious text-book. They are the crystallised
fruit of uncounted ages of human experience,
and have been expressed and symbolised in a
thousand ways by a thousand philosophers.
They may be taught apart from any hook, or
faith, or fetish. Regarding the comparative
value of books, and faiths, and fetishes, there
are many differences of opinion, and upon
these differences are founded many dogmas.
It is unjust for the State to promulgate at
the expense of the general body of tax-
payers the religious dogma of any section of
the taxpayers. It is inexpedient: the State
should not subsidise instruction concerning
matters which it knows nothing about. Hell is
not a Govt, department.
* * * *
And, if it be urged that not subsidy of
denominational schools, but Biblical instruc-
tion in State schools, is what is wanted, these
considerations lose nothing of their force. Bibli-
cal instruction will not satisfy the chief agitators
against the present system : the Catholic
church will still remain outside. The State is
a Union of citizens of all creeds to secure good
government on earth ; it has no business to tax
un-Christian citizens in order to teach the
children of Christian citizens how affairs are
managed in Heaven. And, if the truth must
be told, the Bible is behind the morality of the
age : it was full time the Chicago churches
bowdlerised it. Every decent church or family
has bowdlerised it long ago. All the purity of
its selected maxims cannot nullify the force of
the sanction which it gives to depraved conduct.
In its totality, as a moral work, the Bible is
riot fit to be read to children.
* * * *
For all these reasons the S.A. democrat is
enjoined to record his (or her) vote on April *25
against any change in the present educational
system. It is vitally necessary that he record
his vote, lest judgment should go by his default.
And, if he cannot defend the boon of unsee-
tarian State education, he is not worthy to
enjoy it.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Kingston’s “Continuous Parliament.”
Ihe programme announced by Premier King-
ston, of S.A., consists of several great demo-
cratic principles and one unaccountable fad.
He proposes, among other things, to introduce
the principle of elective Ministries in lieu of the
old, discredited policy of Government by Con-
tradiction, to establish the Referendum, to
further reduce the franchise of the Upper
House, and to do various other things in which
every true Democrat will back him up to the
u termost. And,.in addition to these reforms,
ie proposes to put S.A. Assembly on an entirely
new basis. Instead of a complete dissolution
every three years and the election of a new


JhnnM P r °P° ses one-half the Assembly
should retire every second year, so that instead
ot a whole election every three years there will
be two half ones in four years. The only
reason advanced in favor of this scheme is that
it will provide a “Continuous Parliamentin
other words, if any great crisis should arise be
tween the dissolution of one-half the Assembly
and its re-election, the other half may rise to the
occasion so far as half an object can rise to
any occasion—and declare war, or float a big
loan, or mobilise the troops, or do whatever is
necessary. This is presumably what Kingston
\ T -?c IlS i’ m faCfc ’ lf lle doesu ’ L mean that, it is
difficult to see where the advantage comes in.
Uu the other side there are sundry serious dis-
advantages. The Assembly will never wholly
reflect the popular sentiment. At the best,
one-half of it will represent how the people
feel, and the other half will represent how they
felt two years ago. At the worst, one-half will
represent how they felt a year and 11 months
ago, and the other half will represent how they
felt three years and 11 months ago, and there
will never be any real accord. If there is a
great revulsion of popular sentiment, such as
that which swept away M'Culloch in Victoria
years ago, only half of it can find expression.
1 here may be in power an almost wholly Tory
or almost wholly Democratic legislature, and
when the customary change of sentiment comes
along the people may want to replace it by
one of an almost entirely different com-
plexion, and simply because they can’t do
so their interest in politics will largely evapo-
rate. A vast amount of the interest in the
Assembly election is based on the fact that it is
a fight for absolute possession of the dominant
House, and the winners “get there” for cer-
tain ; a fight in which the winners may not get
there after all, but may simply, as the result of
a great triumph, reduce the other party’s
majority somewhat and leave it still in posses-
sion, is a very different affair. At present, if
any great crisis arises between the dissolution
and the election, the Ministry acts on its own
responsibility and at its own risk, and this plan
looks about as satisfactory as the proposed
system of intermediate Government by half a
Pirliament. A much more effective plan than
either would be to keep the old Assembly in
existence until the new one was elected, and
• hen there would be a whole House to fall back
upon at an emergency instead of a mere dis-
located fragment. Kingston’s plan looks like
that of a man who feels that he must keep on
doing things to preserve his reputation as a
progressive politician, and who doesn’t know
exactly in what direction to progress, so finds
it necessary to do something comparatively
harmless to fill in time while he looks round for
a new impulse.
A Matter of Economy.
The following item from Premier Kingston’s
>S.A. election programme is interesting princi-
pally because it, or something to the same
etfect, has been an item in the election pro-
gramme of every prominent Australian politician
so far back as The Bulletin can remember,
and this paper would have felt lonesome with-
out it:—
He promised economy in the finances, and claimed that
since the Kingston Government took office the cost of
administration had been reduced by £120,000 annually.
He hoped to avoid the necessity of fresh taxation, and
undertook, if the finances warranted it, to raise the
exemption from income-tax to £l5O.
What this paper has long been waiting tor, and
is still waiting for, is the advent of some straight
and outspoken politician who will tell this
country the truth in financial matters. Said
truth is that Australia, as a whole, is much
more lightly taxed than it has any reason to
expect, and much more lightly than it ought
to be if it is going to meet its liabilities
honestly, and having stated these facts that
politician will add that he is going to increase
taxation forthwith. Among the Australian
provinces, at least three—New South Wales,
Victoria and South Australia are already
m >re lightly taxed than Great Britain, and
all the seven provinces, except Westralia, are
m >re lightly taxed than many countries of Con-
tinental Europe, where the people are much
less able to bear the burden. But for a long
series of years Australia’s rule-of-thumb finan-
ciers have educated the people to believe, first,
that they are an oppressed, heavily-taxed,
ground-down community (which they are not) ;
second, that they should live in a constant state
of maudlin sympathy with themselves over
their own alleged burdens (which is quite un-
called for) ; and, third, that the first duty of
every Treasurer is to reduce taxation and cheer-
fully dash the consequences. Therefore, the
normal state of things is that in good years,
when there ought to be a large surplus to aid
the public works expenditure, taxation is cut
down and expenditure kept dangerously close
to revenue. In bad years there is a big deficit,
which is passed on, or funded, or held over,
because the Government lacks the courage to
impose new taxation and cover the shortage.
And at all times there is a tendency to shove
every item of public works expenditure —
reproductive, partially reproductive, or wholly
unproductive, expenditure for repairing old
bridges and dredges, cleaning out rivers which
perpetually silt up again, making roads, repair-
ing roads, building gaols, lunatic asylums,
benevolent ditto, court-houses, schools, fences,
wharves, and offices, repairing, altering, and
patching ditto, wood-blocking streets, and the
like—into loan expenditure, and to leave the
bill to posterity, merely that the Treasurer
may boast that he took off the threepenny duty
on tea, or did something equally obscure, which
was no visible benefit to anybody, and which
no one would have noticed if the fact wasn t
kept perpetually bifore him. lhis paper is
waiting p-tientiy for the right kind of Trea-
surer to come along—the one who will raise
£.303,000 a \eir by extra taxation (which
hardly anyone would notice after the first year)
and spend it in building new railways, and
1 mking rivers, and constructing roads and
i ritraiion works, and thereby “absorb surplus
labor,” and “ develope the resources of the
country,” and “open up the inteiior,” and
“ promote the circulation of capital,” and do
all the other beautiful things which our great,
tired country is always hearing about. In
siiu. t, it wants a man who will realise that you
can’t make omelettes without breaking eggs,

The Parsimony of Economics.
Blessed is the fanatic who puts his trust
in an O’M that is beyond the borders
of Proof-and-Disproof whose saving faith
in misunderstandings of the unknown will
carry him serenely on, heedless of scoffs and
sneers. Be his fetish Karma, or Kismet, or
Apostolic Succession, or the Bunyip, or the
newly-invented Boobong—his faith may survive
as faiths survived in the days when the world
was wide. But what is the fate of the fanatic
who pawns his soul to the Manchester School—
who takes Supply for his God, and Demand for
his Devil, and Competition for his Hades—and
who has constantly to forswear his faith to
serve his fetish ? Such a man finds himself
doubting his faith, but without faith in his
doubts. Such a man might find himself con-
templating any portion of the social system—
the educational, for instance—forgetful entirely
of his allegiance to Supply, Demand, and Com-
petitidn. Giving his attention to Melbourne,
he might make the followfing remarks :
At present there is nothing to prevent persons of the
most superficial attainments from opening a “grammar
school” or “ institute” and employing such cheap assist-
ance as best suits their commercial instincts or the length
of their purses. The abundance of such establishments
does not result in supplying the public with a fair article
at the reduced price which conies of competition. It
merely supplies an inferior or positively bad article, which
enters into competition with genuine education, diverts
pupils from the better schools, and thereby cripples the
resources of the latter to such an extent that it becomes
impossible for them to maintain the necessary staff of
really efficient teachers.
This recantation of the eternal verities of
economics is found in the Argus leading-article
of Feb. 22. It is a complete refutation of the
principles to which that journal is pledged—
the freedom of trade and the fundamental
righteousness of competition. Speaking after a
practical enquiry into something that it under-
stands, the Argus declares that unrestricted
competition is a bane and not a blessing. That
saying of John Bright’s, that Adulteration is a
formofeompetitionand oughtnot to beinterfered
with, is now rejected by all but the most fana-
tical of the Manchester school. John Bright
would have been nearer the truth had he said
that competition is a form of adulteration.
Competition implies the survival of the un-
fittest motives, and it necessitates the un-
natural selection of opportunities. Of all faiths
orthodox economics is the one most niggardly
in consolation to its followers—it cramps the
hope and dwarfs the mercy of man. It is sig-
nificant of the parsimony of economics that
the best of its works is a Torv.
Sydney Telegraph in defence of “ private en-
terprise ” :
The State is such a bad business manager that . . it
has no chance against private enterprise. In order to
make our railways yield the best service to the public, it
was found necessary to turn them into a quasi-private
concern by putting them under the control of an inde-
pendent commission, and even in the red-tape manage-
ment of the departmental offices the same system has now
been adopted.
This looks very large and convincing, no doubt ;
but see how it reads the other way up :
Private enterprise is such a bad business manager, &c.
In order to make our insurance societies, &c., yield the
best returns to the shareholders it is found necessary to
take the management out of the hands of the private
owners and entrust it to an elective Government in the
shape of a board of directors. Even in the red-tape
management of the office the private owners have found
it essential to take the business out of their own hands
and entrust the selection of their managers and offlt ers
to a practically irresponsible Government of their own
creation. Also, in the most progressive countries it has
been found necessary to make them, to some extent,
public concerns by State audit, or by insisting on the
publication of accounts, or by compelling them to furnish
returns to the Government or by other means.
It is a poor slab of logic which won't bear being
1 ioked at on both sides.
Any institution of a stipendiary magistrate
system would be ineffective and harmlu! unless
strict provision were made that the said magis-
trates should not be at the beck and call <>f the
Crown law authorities. It is said that in times
of retrenchment these people send out circulars
to the magistrates to the effect that proper costs
should be given to the Crown, &c. The
honorary magistrates can afford to, and often
do, treat these circulars with contempt,
but, as at present constituted, the stipen-
diary magistracy cannot afford to. Would
it not be possible to give them a security of
tenure somewhat similar to the judges, and yet
render it an easier matter for Parliament to
censure or remove them in case of necessity ?
Ihe awe inspired by the Crown Prosecutor in
the average stipendiary is also to be thought of.
Again, the inclination of the stipendiary to
favor the side represented by a lawyer requires
to be taken into consideration. Perhaps, if we
are to have election as the remedy for the
scandals of the honorary magistracy, the election
of stipendiaries (candidates being restricted to
men of ascertained qualification) would also be
a useful innovation.
Whenever a Melb. Metropolitan Board con-
tractor is caught evading the minimum-wage
clause he pleads that the underpaid unfortunate
is not engaged as a workman at all but as a
‘‘ nipper and, as the “nippers’” duties in-
clude everything from running messages to
swinging picks, there is hardly any gainsaying
the statement. The blame, however, rests witli
the Board and its officials in accepting such
pleas; Ihe conditions of contract distinctly
state that no laborers are to be paid less than
6s. a day, certain penalties being provided for
infraction ; and if full-grown able-bodied men
of 20 years ana upwards don’t come under the
heading, what are laborers? The conditions
don’t recognise any such class as nippers, and
neither would the Board if it were in earnest in
its professions of humanity and not a whited
sepulchre of hypocrisy.
1 his time last year, after the outbreak of public
feeling about the 4s. lld.-a-day rate, Melb.
City Council accepted a tender for the supply
of man, horse, and dray at ss. Sd. This tender
has now expired, and at last Council meeting
it was decided not to call for fresh tenders, but
to extend the present contract at Bs. a day,
which Councillor Pigdon remarked was “a
very fair price in view of the present price of
feed, &c.”—“ etcetera ” probably including
men, horses, and wear and tear on drays.
Wear and tear on men, of course, doesn’t
count. It will thus be seen that the Council’s
conscience has smitten it to the extent of 2s. 4d.
per day—rather a big smite as things go—and it
may reasonably be expected that when the
report of the select committee, now inquiring
into the wages question, is published, the
Council will approve of the insertion of some,
if not a very large, minimum wage in the next
conditions three months hence. It moves—
even the hog-like Melb. City Council moves.
Gov. Brassey, at Bendigo, speaking his little,
harmless, necessary piece :
I am glad to learn from the lips of the Mayor that there
is no party—not even the most insignificant in Victoria—
which demands separation from the old country. If there
were such a party, it would be my duty as Governor to
frustrate its knavish pranks.
Whilst H.E. looks to mayors and “ sich ” for
proofs of the existence of an Australian national
party, he can rely upon enjoying his comfort-
able ignorance ; but as to frustrating the aims
of the nationalists, no Governor can do aught
but help on their cause. Tor Governors are
necessarily frothy and flatulent persons, who
feel themselves called upon to bleat with sheep-
like pertinacity dreadful platitudes about ’Ome,
and the Empire, and the gracious Queen, which
are supposed to satisfy the loyal cravings of the
great Australian heart, but which really sicken
men of horse-sense. Attitudinising and plati-
tudinising Governors are largely responsible for
“ that tired feeling” in Australia.
Some really fine distortions of fact came up at
the banquet recently tendered to Premier Reid
at Casino (N.S. W.). “We have tried Protec-
tion and Protectionist Ministries,” said the
chairman of the function, “and found them
both wanting. We now have a Freetrade
Ministry, and if we give it a fair show you
will see in a few years how much better a
state of things will exist than in the past.”
Then Premier Reid arose and asked for “ a fair
trial for the new' fiscal policy, and if, in a few
years, it retarded progress instead of encourag-
ing it, he would be the first to acknowdedge his
mistake.” And no one had the courage, amonsr
all the prevailing slobber, to point out that they
had been trying Reid’s revenue-tariffism for 40
years, and if it hadn’t proved its alleged good
qualities by this time ituiever would. Also, no
one mentioned to the unreliable chairman that
N.S. W. never tried “Protection and Protection-
ist Ministries.” It tried 26 successive Foreign-
trade Cabinets, and then it gave one Protectionist
Ministry a trial for three years, and, because it
couldn’t do in three years what the others had
failed to do in thirty-five, it was thrown out.
The spectacle of the 27th Foreign-trade Ministry
asking for a “ fair trial,” as if its creed hadn’t
been tried and found wanting 26 times already,
is very good. How is revenue-tariffism to pro-
duce “ so much better a state of things than in
the past ” seeing that the past in New South
Wales was practically all the same system?
And why didn’t it create a blessed state of
things while it was there instead of only
intending to do it now ?
A Counterfeit Causerie.
Dear Bulletin, —Many months ago you drew
attention to the fact that an indisputable danger
of the drop in silver to many existing currencies
is the premium now offering to counterfeiters.
Since then, counterfeit silver coins have been
found in four Australian provinces.
* * * *
The first parcel was primarily detected by the
circumstance that it was deposited in bulk with a
Melbourne bank. The Chinese merchant who
made the deposit was undoubtedly ignorant of the
nature of the transaction he was engaged in ; he
received the parcel (,£55 of shillings, all spurious,
except three) from Hongkong, per steamer. If he
had been in the know, and willing to act, he could
have passed off these counterfeits in the ordinary
course of business, without any danger what-
ever. They were not by any means well-struck,
nor were they from good dies, but they
were quite good enough to deceive any
non-expert member of the public. These
counterfeits were of the pre-jubilee design, but
bore dates exclusively used on the jubilee design.
I he lettering was imperfect and somewhat clumsy.
he ettigy of the Queen was a bad copy of the
official effigy. They were made of silver, not
quite up to standard, of a quality usual in Mexican
dollars, and contained a trace of gold. Neverthe-
less, the coins would have passed unquestioned in
ordinary trade, When they were officially
examined the defects were duly catalogued and
announced m the press. Soon afterwards the now
suspicious eyes of the local bankers found some
more coins of a doubtful nature. These proved to
have much fewer defects, the dates were adjusted,
the dies had been more carefully engraved, the
struck in a more workmanlike manner,
and the quality of the silver was nearer the proper
degree of fineness. The counterfeiter (presumably
located m Hongkong or China) was willing
to learn and to profit by his lessons. Next dis-
coveries were made in Tasmania. The Tas-
manian counterfeits were very good work, and
lead to the suspicion that many more remain
m the torpid circulation of that sequestered spot,
oouth Australia, unwilling to be peculiar, sought
out some “ bad ” shillings in its borders ; and then
Kanakaland, last of all, made the discovery that
an Australian province can have more than one
curse, these later counterfeits are the work of
smart men, and are almost as good as if turned out
by the official Mint.
* * * •
The caution of the counterfeiters engaged in
tin s interesting traffic is most exemplary. They
made one mistake—their attempt in Melbourne,
that mistake was engaging a man “not in the
know. Tasmania, South Australia, and Kanaka
land have not discovered large quantities of the
frauds, Ihe “bobs’’came not in battalions but
in single spies. Furthermore, it is always “bobs”
and not sixpences or “half dollars.” They are
practising with the “ bobs ” as far as Australia
(and perhaps England) may be Concerned. When
they have attained the proficiency to be derived
from official criticism, they will extend their opera-
tions to other sections of the currency. With
silver at 31 pence there is only 5'6 pence worth of
silver in a shilling—allowing a good margin of
profit.
* * * *
The Straits Settlements have also had the favor of
attention, and one of the current coins (rix-dollar)
has received the sincerest form of flattery. Some
police proceedings took place at Singapore, but
the offence w T as not sheeted home to anyone in
particular.
* » * *
It is a mathematical certainty that a flourishing
trade in counterfeit silver will ultimately be estab-
lished in one or more dark corners of the earth if
silver remain below 40 pence per ounce, which is
very likely.
'« * * *
At present Australia is not concerned in the
trouble nationally. If the unauthorised coins get
111 to circulation and remain undetected, the only
present difference will be that Australia will be
buying its currency from China instead of from
England. But it is a disagreeable thing for any
individual to be in p issession of coin that no one
else will accept. T iis is the present Australian
danger from the counterfeiter—that he will not do
his work quite well enough. By-and-bye, when
Australia manages its own currency in its entirety,
the counterfeiter will be an ordinary robber of the
State.
* * » *
At the present moment the idea is dawning on
the official mind that something must be done.
The counterfeiter will ultimately be able to profit-
ably produce an exact copy of any given coin from
the present British, U.S.A., French and German
silver currencies— i.e., exact in color, weight, de-
sign and workmanship. It remains to be seen
whether any secret alloy can be introduced into a
wholesale recoinage. A secret alloy is not a very
hopeful stand-by, but it might serve.
* * * *
Gold coinage, under the present dispensation, is
not over-valued. The sovereign is worth a pound in
the melting-pot. Sweating gold coin (hence “sweat-
ing ” labor) is a very old trick. A bag of sovereigns
is shaken violently. The friction of one coin on
another causes wear—the amount of gold worn off
is the profit of the sweater. Or sovereigns are
put to soak in aqua regia, and a thin skin is dis-
solved off each coin. These processes disfigure the
coins, more or less, and the evidence of the
banker’s scales tells the whole story. In America,
gold pieces have been sliced in half, the inside
scooped out and filled with some heavy alloy,
then the slices have been soldered together again ;
this is clever, and may possibly avoid detection, but
it is not sufficiently profitable. In Melbourne, re-
cently, a bank found a sovereign which had
been operated on with a lathe; a fine, con-
tinuous cut was made into the milling all
round the coin ; about 2s. worth of gold was
thus obtained ; the cavity was filled with white-
lead, gilded, and passed on the gentle public.
This trick is clumsy, and easily detected.
* * * *
Of course, bank-notes are forged frequently. In
the States, “ green-goods ” manufacture is a
science, and, at one trial at least, officials refused
to positively swear which of two notes was the
forgery and which genuine. The circumstances
were, however, peculiar, as they related to an
antique pattern of note, without the modern safe-
guards. The Colonial Bank, Melbourne, recently
discovered a fraud on itself from the fact that a
note purported to bear the signatures of two men,
managers of different branches, who had never
seen one another.—Yours, M.
[The illogical part of the above argument is that the
writer assumes the counterfeit-silver trouble to be just
beginning, and the Chinese counterfeiters above-men-
tioned to be the pioneers of the game. Quite possibly
the evil has been going on for years, and half the Austra-
lian silver coin may be bogus, only so well-executed as to
avoid detection. The counterfeiter who was detected was
bowled out, naturally enough, because his coinage wasn’t
good enough. If it had been good enough he wouldn’t
have been bowled out, and no one would have suspected
anything, and if there are other men in the business
whose coinage is good enough they won’t be bowled-out
either. There is a dark suspicion that many thousands
of British shillings are made in Germany, and that vast
quantities of U.S. dollars are manufactured in Mexico,
and that something like 10 or more per cent, of the silver
coinage of the Latin Union is made by private speculators
—but no one knows. Anyone can coin silver with im-
punity if he can only bring the artistic part of the work
to perfection.—Ed. B.]
One Beach, American, who died a few
weeks ago, invented the type-writer, but only
with an idea of doing good to the blind.
If Eight Hours’ celebration committees
had any eye for effect, they would set the men to
march in processions in their working-clothes. A
body of clay-stained sewer-laborers, blue-dungaree-
clad engineers, and blackened stokers would ac-
cord with the eternal fitness of things, while the
spectacle of uncomfortable-looking men, perspiring
in hideously-respectable creased black suits and
tight boots, is only ridiculous. When the shearers,
a few years ago, turned out in bush style, they
attracted more attention than all the gaudy
banners put together. i

Oscar Wilde’s comedy, “ The Importance
of Being Earnest,” opened at Sydney Cri. on
Saturday in the presence of a large audience, and
with the author’s name restored to the bill after a
long absence. It was received with enthusiasm,
and, on the whole, deserved its reception. There
is very little plot about it; Oscar Wilde does not
pile up great masses of mirth after the fashion of
Pinero, or lavish his solid material as that great
artist does. But there is a quaint humor about it
such as no other present-day writer deals in, and
an atmosphere of evening-dress smartness that is
Wilde’s very own. It contains all the bright epi-
grams which the author had left after he wrote
“ Lady Windermere’s Fan ” and one or two other
plays. But in one respect it is better than “ Lady
Windermere.” The characters are not so ineffably
bored among a great deal of expensive furniture ;
they are less suggestive of the worm that dieth
not, with a boiled shirt on, making cynical obser-
vations in a drawing-room ; some of them actually
do things, and don’t look quite dead afterwards
from the exertion. No one could live long in the
atmosphere of Wilde’s comedy without showing
the bad effects of it; the constant struggle to
think of another abnormally smart thing to say
next would produce that agitated drawing-together
of the features in the centre of the face known as
the bicycle-countenance, and would ultimately
tend to iocomotor-ataxy. But it is a very pleasant
atmosphere, none the less, when taken in moderate
quantities. It is so beautifully unreal that a man
goes away from it in a dazed condition and quite
unable to remember his own office address, and
needs to look at the name on his collar to recover
a sense of his own identity. The characters have
no visible means of support save by making
brilliant observations to each other in a richly-
upholstered apartment and trifling affably with a
frivolous cucumber sandwich, but they seem
to get on very comfortably. What The Bul-
letin would like would be to take all the people
out of Oscar Wilde’s comedy, and make them
camp out for three days with swags and large,
indigestible dampers on the banks of a flooded
creek, waiting for a chance to get over to Boggabri
or Bogantungan or Thargomindah or some other
place whose name alone would shock their finer
sensibilities, and see how the dreadful incongruous-
ness of things would pan out. Then it wants to
see these people when they got across, arriving at
Bogantungan by the dirt road and making high-
class remarks as they come. It has no animosity
towards them, of course; it merely wants to try
an experiment.
* * * »
Despite all these loose ravings, ‘ ‘ The
Importance of Being Earnest ” is a good
comedy when not taken too often. It is
bright and cheerful all through, well-con-
structed, with no loose ends hanging out, and
the plot—what there is of it—is clever and effec-
tive. Boucicault, as Algernon, is an artistic gem,
and Cecil Ward is another. Miss Faber, as the
fashion-plate girl in the ruinously-expensive hat,
is one of the most successful society ladies who
has wandered through an impossible comedy this
year, and Miss Hardy gets about the best chance
she has yet had with the B. and B. co., and uses
it admirably. This lady’s pretty face was con-
structed specially by a far-seeing Providence that
she might play the nice, unsophisticated girl under
a large green tree for all the girl and the tree are
worth. Carey, Miss Watt-Tanner and Miss Hill
further assist to let the comedy down gently with
the best results. It is a comedy that requires to
be played in a tender, butterfly spirit; any
attempt to treat it in a vigorous fashion would
produce the same results as if an elephant adopted
a moth and sat on it to keep it warm.
* * * *
“Fedora ” follotv's at the Criterion.
** * *
The “ Broad Arrow,” in five acts, is to be
the new sensation at Her Majesty’s on Saturday.
“Hands Across the Sea” has proved too old to
stand any further revival, even the most enthusi-
astic efforts of the Tank—a capable performer,
though rather cold and unemotional—being unable
to make it any newer, so its career comes to a
well-earned conclusion on Friday night. The
new production is “by arrangement with W. J.
Holloway.” Most new plays in Sydney nowadays
6eem to be more or less by arrangement with Plain
Bill Holloway, which prevents The Bulletin
forgetting that person nearly so much as it vvould
like to do. A society for forgetting Holloway is
much needed.
* * &
“Djin Djin,” W. and M.’s fairy-tale of
old Japan, is bringing in a continuous stream of
bullion to the Firm’s treasury, and so far the first
beginnings of the falling-cff are not visible. Con-
ndering that three Sydney theatres are just now
travelling on spectacle and tank, this is a good
record.
■Jii 5K- * *-
The great bare-knee spectacle gibbers at
Sydney Royal with the same gibber as mentioned
last week, and the bagpipes and the brass band bray
furiously, and the blaze of red kilts and flags and
the sheen and shimmer of the bayonets strike the
eye with a shock like a tomato at election time.
Bland Holt wears tights under his kilts as a sacri-
fice to respectability, but the rank-and-file don’t,
and the hair on their legs stands out conspicuously
as a guarantee of good faith. Possibly the hair
has something to do with the success of the drama,
or possibly it is the great array of petticoats in
general that pleases the public soul, or possibly it
is the artless way in which Mrs. Bland Holt hangs
her other pair of stockings to dry on the end of
Bland’s bayonet—Bland afterwards gets seven
days for being found with them there, or possibly
’t is Mrs. Holt in knickerbockers sitting on Baker s
love-letter. Anyhow, the kilt drama has distinctly
caught on, and that being so it is no good remon-
strating about the accursed bagpipes, and the
stupidity of a garment that needs to have dumb-
bells hung on to its absurdly brief tail to keep it
down in a high wind.
*- * * *
Sydney is supporting three variety shows
just now, which is a considerable strain on a city
that can hardly support itself. There is nothing
specially novel about any of them this week, so
these remarks are merely intended as an expression
of mild surprise that the whole trio remain above
water.
• The first week of “ Trilby,” at Melbourne
Princess’s, appears to have established a boom.
Clearly the play is certain to draw all readers of
Du Maurier’s book, the majority of whom would
have carefully refrained from patronising a feeble
dramatic adaptation. “ Trilby ”is a pretty, sen-
timental show, with bright bits of “Bohemian”
color in the studio scenes, and a fascinating super-
naturalism running through it. The construction
is as novel as the plot, therefore the effect upon
people who come with no definite ideas as to what
they are about to receive will, generally speaking,
be more impressive than they care to admit at the
time. Australians have a habit of protesting
against being surprised, nevertheless the flavor of a
new variety of performance grows upon them
and ultimately tempts them to return for another
dose. Sometimes “ Trilby ”on the stage doesn’t
quite explain itself to the unenlightened. The
mental subjection of poor little Gecko to Svengali
might be more emphasised in the first act, when
the mesmerist reveals to his pupil his musical de-
signs upon the heroine. Du Maurier’s picture of
Gecko is realised by Mr. E. W. Morrison, who
supports Mr. Fax Svengali admirably.
* * * *
Miss Edith Crane, in the name-part, is not
much less convincing than the two musicians. She
puts no force into her death-scene—which couldn’t,
in any case, avoid seeming a tame incident
after Svengali’s gruesome demise; but she walks
through her part in the must natural style possible,
and looks like an angel born to drive the Parisian
Latin Quarter crazy. Miss Jennie Reiffarth, as
Mdme Vinard, is possibly the cleverest “ old
woman ” artist Australia has seen of late years.
Mr. George Trader brings Zou-Zou to the fore,
and the three painters—Taffy, the Laird, and
Little Billee—improve slightly on acquaintance.
Taffy was a fair success from the start. The
Laird labors heavily with his Scotch accent, and
Billee is handicapped by a much larger head and
fully 20 more years than the character ought to
carry. And the three studio companions are
plainly ’Murkan instead of English. Their
national sing-song elocution bewrayeth them.
Also, it is depressing. Why do so many American
actors and actresses chant the same old tune?
Triiby’s song behind the scenes is sung by Miss
Nagel, whose deep contralto declaration that
“Sweet Alice lies under the stone” justifies a
belief that Ada Crossley will have to look to her
laurels presently. Miss Nagel has a great voice.
* * * *
“ Trilby,” it seems, is either expected by
many people to be a study from the nude, or else
they go to catch a glimpse of the heroine’s bare
feet, which, by the way, are not much in evidence.
Anyway, the man who loans out opera-glasses has
done phenomenal biz. up to date. On Easter
Monday and Tuesday, for the first time on record,
he got rid of all his stock and ran around town
trying to borrow some more.
* * *
“The Enemy’s Camp” began to pine
at Melb. Royal after Easter Monday, and the last
nights of this gaudy entertainment are nearly at
an end. A superfluity of cheap plot seems to be
chiefly the matter with Westmacott’s enterprise.
The author was afraid to let his characters ever
stand around quietly and talk on their
merits, because their conversation will not
bear scrutiny. He kept them eternally on the
move, and some of the moves are so unrea-
sonable, or so exactly similar to the doings
of previous people in former dramas, that public
interest fell away from the Royal when the holi-
days ran out. “ The Enemy’s Camp ” has
received handsome treatment from the manage-
ment. The scenery and etceteras wifi be remem-
bered with gratitude, but the author must prepare
to answer for his conduct at some future time,
unless he happens to have died already, in which
case the worm that never dieth is doubtless ad-
ministering a severe reproof. On Saturday next
(18th) “The Work Girl” conies along to the
Royal. She ran for,several weeks in Sydney, and
should repeat the success in Melbourne, where an
ill-conditioned Hupper ’Ouse has been treating a
Factories Bill with contumely.
** * *
At the Alexandra, this week, a hard-work-
ing company is rattling the dry bones of “ Current
Cash ” with much earnestness. Charles Godfrey
and his fire-engine, also the Cosmans, Lentons,
Leslies, Ada Colley, and 20 other stars of various
magnitudes, gleam nightly at the Opera House. A
colossal Eight-hours’ Festival is due at the Exhi-
bition Building on April 21st, and an Easter Fair
is happening upon the same premises!until the
Festival aforesaid takes the flure. Lord Brassey
and suite will appear at the 1 estival.
Though Mistress Grundy frown
And speak with solemn loathing
Of one who did not think it rude
To sit for artists in the nude,
Which means—devoid of clothing ;
Although the matron’s tone
Of indignation shrill be,
For such as seek (but seldom find)
True virtue in the human mind,
The brightest gem of womankind
That ever sparkled will be
Trilby.
She flings her shapely arms
Round other necks than Billee’s,
And thinks no wrong in doing this;
The fragrance of her friendly kiss
Is sweeter than the lilies’.
Long may her bonnie charms
Depicted on a hill be !
We love her ruddy golden hair,
Her white and shapely tootsies bare.
She’s not —no matter, we don’t care,
Our model girl shall still be
Trilby.
A Famous Theatrical Manager.
Janies Cassius Williamson was not born
into the theatrical profession. He waHted mto it
at the ambitious age of 15, and served an arduous
apprenticeship in a company that rushed around
the Western States of ’Murka, seeking what could
be devoured, physically and mentally. Mr. VV U-
liamson has nothing in particular to say of his nrs
acquaintance with speaking parts, except that he,
like other youngsters of the co., had to copy them
out himself from one book of the play provided
by the management, and learn them any time
between midnight and cockcrow. He probably
looks back upon those struggling days with satis-
faction, but doesn’t appear to regret their depar-
ture. Mr. Williamson, indeed, is not an emotional
man off the stage, and for one who has been
(( starred ” in both hemispheres and several ainei-
ent climes he seems about the least self-admirmg
actor on record. He talks of his histrionic career
with difficulty.
* * * *
Youthful J. C. W. came out of the West and
caught the eye of Wnllack, the Wallack, whose old
original theatre was the finest academy of acting
that America has known. Corpedy, ancient and
modern, the Divine Bard, and all sorts of good
work that happened to be then going in England
were produced by Wallack in the best possible
style. He aimed at perfection of detail, and
spared no expense, staging a big show from
London with more thoroughness than the Firm of
W. & M. can take credit for, even nowadays, says
Williamson, who w’orked for seven years as a
member of Wallack’s stock company. Acting,
and plenty of it, was required there. Mr. Wil-
liamson covered a lot of artistic ground during his
seven years’ toiling at the famous New York
house. He became, after a while, the recognised
“dialect comedian,” and was cast for German,
French, Dutch, Yorkshire, or Somersetshire
characters at short notice. He had to interpret
bits of Charles Dickens. When Lotta, in her
prime, played the Marchioness in “ Old Curiosity
Shop,” young Williamson was the Dick Swiveller.
lie padded himself up to the requirements of
Joey Ladle—a well-nigh forgotten person now —in
“No Thoroughfare.” As a Shakespearian comedian
he ranged from Touchstone to Dogberry—a wide
stretch of style. He played Doctor Pangloss, Bob
Acres, and Sir Lucius O’Trigger, and, from the
fact that he dwells half-a-second longer than usual
on those names, one surmises tha J. C. William-
son was particularly happy in old-English comedy.
He went right through the mill at Wallack’s.
After that he betook himself to California with a
reputation, and flourished there for three years.
* * * *
Then the level-headed actor got hold of “ Struck
Oil,” and saw money in it. He suggested altera-
tions in the drama, bought it, married Maggie
Moore, and went on tour with those two valuable
belongings. What Mr. and Mrs. Williamson did
with “Struck Oil” is a tale that has been told
until every experienced kangaroo in the remotest
back-blocks knows it by heart. They first ap-
peared as John and Lizzie Stofel at Salt Lake
City, before a select audience of devout Mormons,
and the blessing of Brigham Young, which passeth
all understanding, descended upon the show like a
dove. The Stofels brought “Struck Oil” to Austra-
lia in 1874, playing fifteen months to unprecedented
business. India was the next country they looted,
after which London. J. C. Williamson and
Maggie Moore presented themselves at the Adel-
phi without much preliminary flourish of trum-
pets, and gave the cockneys 100 consecutive nights
of “Struck Oil.” The play was voted un-Hinglish,
but the “stars,” it was admitted, shone to great
advantage. They became favorites, and on Easter
Monday, 1876, a revival of “ Arrah-na-Pogue ”
was put up for them. The Irish drama ran 100
nights, contrary to the expectation of people who
knew the London market. Chatterton, manager
of the Adelphi, proposed to renew the William-
sons’ engagement for a year, and stage “The
Shaughraun ” without Dion Boucicault’s consent.
The author had somehow failed to get this last of
his Irish dramas copyrighted in England, whereby
it was at the mercy of any conscienceless manager
who might care to use it. Williamson refused to
be a party (a name-party, seeing that he would
have played Conn) to the piratical venture, and the
re-engagement was declined.
* * * *
In 1879, after an absence of about four years,
Mr. Williamson and Miss Maggie Moore came
again to Australia and found the country yearning
fot \ m °re °f them They enjoyed another boom
with Struck Oil. At this time the prosperous
actor was determined to live by his art, and avoid
the worries of management altogether, but cir-
cumstances conquered his resolution. He had
brought out “ Pinafore ” and “The Pirates of
Penzance with him from England, reserving for
himself the parts of Sir Joseph Porter ancf the
Sergeant of Police (Williamson’s humorous render-
ing of the Sergeant is still remembered), and it
came about that John Stofel was forming a comic
opera company—the “ Royal Comic Opera Co
when negotiations with Messrs. Garner and Mus-
grove, who had previously arranged a junction of
w n Va forces, resulted in the triumvirate firm
ol Williamson, Cramer and JVlusgrove,
The senior partner didn’t quite resign himself to
his fate. He dreamed, no doubt, of further
developing his acting powers. A clause in the
partnership deed gave him liberty to plav “ star ”
engagements during six months of the year the
Firm to have first offer. Practically he restricted
actor W llliamson to the service of manager Wil-
liamson and Co. when he signed the bond. To
engage himself elsewhere in Australia would have
been acting against his own managerial interests—
afar°off he He eS Jn°f th ® WOrld ’ il is all situated
atar ott. He took engagements with the Firm
from rime to time “Struck Oil,” followed

But Ins versatility was not plainly shown to the
Australian public. About 10 years ago, or less
