learned his last part—Uncle Matthew Somebody
in “La Cigale in one night. It was a long
part, too.
* * * *
Theatrical management, says J.C.W., is a busi-
ness nowadays. The Firm has made some fine
strokes, of course. “Silver King,” “The Mi-
kado,” and other Gilbert-Sullivan operas; later
“Ma Mie Rosette” and “Djin Djin,” now re-
peating its Melbourne success in Sydney, are
associated in his mind with delightful treasury
returns. But an Australian success is, at the
most, only a matter of a few weeks right away
in Melbourne and Sydney, and the producer
has no sooner shal e 1 hands with himself on the
first night than he must start considering his next
move. So many successes are required to make a
profitable year’s campaign, hence the Firm’s recent
determination to fix its headquarters at Melb.
Princess’s and run all its shows from the one
place. Partner Musgrove, says Williamson, wrote
most despondent letters from England a while ago.
The weary traveller saw nothing going °iri
London that could be expected to please Aus-
tralia — i.e., nothing available in the way of
dramatic stars, no good new’ dramas or comedies,
no comic operas worth a second thought. So he
turned to America for novelties. “ Trilby ” will
be followed by Mrs. Potter and Bellew, and pre-
sently comes “ A Trip to Chinatown,” with Nat
Goodwin, best of American comedians, as central
attraction. Nothing short of the best will do
says J. C. Williamson. He would sigh, possibly’
if such windy inspirations of forced breath were
not unbusinesslike. “You might mention that
the public and the press are rather hypercritical,”
he suggests. But “Trilby”! The advance
booking for “ Trilby ” is very gratifying.
At Poverty Point.
Manager Williamson’s ukase, forbidding Trilby Troupers
wearing natural flowers on the stage, is said to have much
annoyed one actress. Leaves scattered on the Princess
boards have caused more than one accident, and “the
firm ” will not allow Trilby limbs to risk others.
The heart of Melb. femaledom has gone out to George
Trader, the Zou Zou of the “Trilby” co., whilst E. VV.
Morrison, as the aesthetic Gecko, holds a good second
place. Trader is the Boucicault of the co., a thorough
artist —in fact, strongly resembling Dion B. as the
Amazonian Grival. Next to Boucy’s, his is the best Anglo-
French yet heard on the Australian stage.
Veteran Dick Stewart, Nellie’s pa, succeeds George
Tallis as treasurer at Melb. Princess’s, the latter being
promoted to the business-managership, vice Harry Mus-
grove.
Brough and Boucicault’s advt.-hypocrisy in suppressing
Oscar Wilde’s name has come to an end ; Sydney Tele-
graph boldly mentions the excluded author; but S.M.
Herald, in its first notice of “ The Importance of Being
Earnest,” still carefully avoids direct reference to him.
Has anybody ever noticed whether the rev. staff of the
Herald now positively name Judas Iscariot ? or do they
still cautiously refer to him as “ a certain person guilty of
deplorable conduct which every right-thinking person
must condemn ” ?
Dot Boucicault says he is tired of contradicting the
rumor that he is going to London to marry Pattie Browne.
Rumor has engaged the amiable Dot to every unmarried
female member of his co., past or present.
The British Examiner of Plays prohibits the production
of “ Joseph of Canaan ” in the Land of Beer and Podsnap
on the ground of its Biblical origin !
The present seems to be the theatrical mating season.
Bookings for the near future :—Priscilla Verne, to a rich
fashionable; Olive Delroy (of Delroy and Lynne), to a
Melbourne dentist; Cora Bailey, dancer, to Jake Fried-
man ; and Cissy Landford (of Pollard’s Juveniles), to

Three theatrical divorces at next Sydney court.
Edith Crane is one of the seven actresses who a little

States. The critics unanimously acclaimed her the Trilby.

“Dot” Boucicault’s baptismal name is not Dion, but
George Darley Boucicault. The real Dion, jun., was ar
elder brother, who was killed in an English railway acci
dent in ’76. The Edinburgh to London train met with a
bad smash in one of the midland counties, and, managing
to escape with a slightly' injured arm, Boucicault started
to help the more seriously' injured passengers out of the
wreck. While thus humanely engaged, a second train
thundered down into the debris of the first, completing
the fearful destruction and killing Boucicault outright
George Darley then assumed the name of the deceased
brother.

The reign of the Sydney aquariums is over ; 1000 adults
on a holiday is now considered big biz., while £4 or £5
taken on Sunday makes the managerial bosom heave with
pleasure. A few years ago, 5000 admissions on a holiday,
and £3O on Sunday, was only reckoned fair. The fall is
inexplicable.
The Sydney public found out the real use of the
Dornngton last week. When that gentleman's stupend-
ous limbs are encased in the pants of the musical hussar
in “ Dandy Dick,” they serve a great and poetic purpose.
A competition between Dorrington’s legs as the hussar
Baker’s as the lieutenant in “ One of the Best,” and the
legs of Scot Inglis as the captain in “ The Enemy’s Camp,”
ought to draw well. The Bulletin reckons that Dorring-
ton and Baker should run a dead-heat.
The general impression at Sydney Cri. last week seemed
to be that Mrs. Brough’s Second "Mrs. Tanquerav is even
better than it used to be ; that the lengthy Dorrington’s
representation of the beery George Horrid is also improv-
ing with age ; that Miss Watt-Tanner is a rather inferior
substitute for Miss Romer; and that Miss Faber and
Grace Noble, as Saint Ellean, run practically neck and
neck, any advantage being with the former.
Edith Crane, as Trilby, suggests a youthful Nellie
Stewart in many ways, particularly in little tricks of the
voice. In the singing act, with her golden hair a-hanging
down her back and clothed in sumptuous raiment, she is
a large edition of the winsome Nellie in the second act of
“ Ma Mie Rosette.”
Some of the most effective bits in “Trilby” happen off
the stage. To wit, the illusion, so perfect as to deceive
the audience, of Svengali’s piano and Gecko’s violin play-
ing ; the contralto voice that sings “Ben Bolt;” the
vigorous cheering, and then the wild uproar in the
theatre; and, lastly, the play of green limelight on the
Jew’s glittering eye.
Inigo Tyrell, charged with the manslaughter of a bar-
rister in Melbourne, is a man who would have found it
very difficult to get a fair trial in that city a few years
ago. People have mostly forgotten Tyrell’s awful dramas
by this time, and can take a dispassionate view of his
case, but a time was when it would have been necessary
to shift the venue to the mallee country to give Tyrell a
fair run for his liberty.
Reuben Fax—what a stage-name, after Cholmorideley
Montmorency, or even Howard Vernon !
Rignold, booked to open at Melb. Royal on Aug. Ist,
for an extended season, will have difficulty in getting a
decent co. together, as things now are. Westmacott has
now snapped up most of his men, and that aspiring
manager has been so successful and become so popular
that he is not likely to lose them. It is likely that G.R.
will prevail on one or two of the ’Murkan “Trilby”
mummers to linger here awhile. Some of them are
anxious to stay on.
The Wild-Cat Column.
When the Mutual Life of Victoria wanted to
invest £20,000 recently in Vic. Government stock it had
to go all the way to London to get it, because there was
practically none in the local market. Presumably it will
also have to collect the interest in London and "hump it
laboriously back to Melbourne. The chairman of the
Union Trustee Co., at the recent annual meeting, lamented
on the same subject : “We have a great difficulty in
finding safe investments for our trust funds, even at a
low rate of interest. It would be a great convenience and
a great public benefit if the Government would issue con-
sols locally and apply the proceeds to the re-purchase in
Great Britain of an equal amount of debentures current in
the old country.” But then the Government has grovelled
so long to the British money-lender that it really can’t
get out of the habit. Apropos of which, Melbourne Age
advocates a comprehensive scheme for locking the Murray
and the Darling to make them navigable through their
whole length. “ The amount required,” it cheerfully
adds, “ could readily be obtained in the London market
by a loan.” The press has been on this subject so long
that if you found an Australian journalist delirious from
thirst 70 miles from the nearest waterhole, and partially
revived him with a long, cool drink so that his baked
tongue could talk, he would sit up among the glare and
the saltbush and state mechanically that a loan ought to
be floated on the London market.

Some more scraps from the evidence of the City
of Melbourne Bank auditors “ I did not go into details,
but accepted the assurance of Mr. Robertson. I gave a
certificate in favor of an appropriation for £5OOO on
Northey and Biddeley’s account. I could not explain how
interest on t. account was taken into profits, but it was
possible the account was good when it was reduced by
£5OOO. It would not be proper, as a rule, to take interest
to profits on an appropriated account, but there might be
a satisfactory explanation. I could not explain how it
was that interest on W. L. Jackson’s account was taken to
profits after an appropriation for £2OOO had been made.
The proceeding could not be defended without explana-
tion. . . . Mr. Longmuir (the manager) was in a posi-
tion to know more about the affairs of the bank than we
were. We were satisfied with his explanation. We are
not expected to direct the policy of the bank. ... I
did not make close enquiries as to (Turner’s) securities. I
went by what Mr. Longmuir told me. ... If the
directors had granted an overdraft (to another director
on insufficient security) we would be stepping out of our
province if we objected to it. . . . We had to rely on
verbal information to a great extent . . . Auditors
had no control over the appropriations. It would be im-
possible to audit if every book and account had to be ex-
amined. ... An auditor’s certificate is not conclusive
one way or the other. We were always satisfied with the
manager’s explanations as to the securities.”

* * * *
Premier Kingston seems to have altogether
missed the kernel of the State Life Insurance idea, when
ne says that a State office will be started unless the
existing societies treat their clients “liberally.” The
present societies treat the public as liberally as they can
without going bankrupt; there is even a danger that
competition may tempt some of them to be too liberal.
u 8 v ni °re liberality that is wanted, but absolute security,
wmch oniy the State can give. If Kingston means to
rush the private offices into a violent course of further
liberality he is only urging them to recklessness and
possible insolvency.
* * * *
A scheme is on foot in Melbourne to form a
huge assets co. which will take over the ruins of several
large, busted concerns now winding up. The ruins in
each case would be valued as carefully as possible, and
the new co. would issue its own scrip for the amount to
the creditors, who could sell it if they wanted money.
Ihe savmg ought to be very large. There would be one
liquidator and one office and office staff instead of a dozen.
Ihe expenses being thus reduced it is probable that the
interest and rents received from the ruins would pay all
charges and leave a margin of profit, and in that case the
co. could go slow with its realisation and wait for better
times and better prices. And by waiting it would help to
bring about better prices; a great deal of the present
depreciation is caused by the efforts of several dozen com-
panies to rush their property simultaneously on to a sick
market. Still, the prospects of the scheme don’t amount
to much. Nobody has any serious faith in anybody
else s scheme these days.
* * * *
Considering all the lame apologies which have
been offered for the downfall of the Melbourne land and
mortgage co’s, based on the “depreciation of property,”
this fact is worth considering. The depreciation, from the
top of the boom to the very bottom of the depression, as
shown by the municipal valuations of Melbourne and
suburbs, was about 25 per cent. There were sane and re-
liable valuations to be got even in the maddest times of
the boom, else the municipalities couldn’t have got them.
A mortgage co. or land bank or building society which
simply lent out its own funds on city property, up to 60
per cent, of that easily-available valuation, need not have
lost a penny by the smash—the biggest one in Australian
history even if its business had been confined to the
capital. If it had traded in the country, vvhere there was
practically no slump at all, it might have made profits
much as usual. The Conservatives, who keep on asserting
that a State Land Bank must go broke when a collapse
conies, are invited to seriously consider these figures.
* * * *
Perkins’s Brewery Co. (Brisbane) is one of the
few things that are prospering in Kanakaland. The net
profits for 1895 were £11,783, and after paying 10 percent,
div. there remains £5658, making the balance to credit of
profit and loss £18,593.
» * * *
The secretary of the Melbourne Farmers’ Mutual
Loan and Building Society explains that his institution is
constructed on the terminating principle (a fact not set
forth in the published prospectus), which removes the
principal objections alluded to in last week’s issue. The
chief objection which remains is that Victoria wants a
State-managed, or at least State-audited, Land Bank with
about £10,000,000 of capital to lend at low rates ; and a
private concern with £300,0. 0, though a distinct move in
the right direction, isn’t large enough to make much im-
pression on the mortgage evil. Still, the Farmers' Mutual
is a move in the right direction.
* * * »
The balance-sheet of the Mutual Assurance
Society of Victoria appears in this issue. The Bulletin
has already commented on this document (in its issue of
March 21) as a highly satisfactory statement. Deprecia-
tion has been written off remorselessly, and every effort
made to put the securities on a bedrock valuation, which
is the first thing to be considered. Safety, not glare and
big bonuses, is the prime consideration in life insurance.
* * * *
The interesting spectacle of J. Bull writhing
under a tax on absentees’ incomes from local investments
was provided at Tas. Supreme Court the other day, when
the Emu Bay and Waratah Railway Co. tried hard to
wriggle out of paying tax on its debenture-interest. The
co. is a London co., and has floated debentures in London,
and its net receipts aren’t enough to pay the guaranteed
interest to the debenture-holders. Still, it pays the
debenture-holders a little, and on this little the Govt,
demanded income-tax. The co. fought, apparently, to
bring home to the minds of its debenture-holders the
astounding fact that Tasmania really wanted their
shekels, for last year’s Act left it no leg, or even a
pretence of a leg, to stand upon. So the Chief Justice
easily decided ; and the co. went away and wept its cash
into the hands of Treas. Fysh.

When S.A. State Export Department was
initiated the leading Tory legislators condemned the pro-
ject as an interference with private enterprise, and the
idea of a Govt. Dept, getting better prices than private

London double the current Adelaide price for a shipment
of lambs on behalf of a leading member of the local
National Ass!

* * « »
Jumping has had another set-back in N.S.W. —
this time at Cobar, where an effort was made to secure a
part of the mining property known as the Peak by in-
forming the Govt, of some small informality on the part
of the 'owners. The warden dismissed the application,
and the jumpers were hooted. The public also expressed
approval of the result in a street demonstration. The
low-«Tade mines of Cobar are attracting attention in the
mining market, and people realise that nothing would
discourage investment quicker than jumping, whether
successful or not. In this instance Barrass and Conley,
by their wonderful discovery, have opened up a part of
Cobar which had been abandoned for years. They have
had about 39000 z. gold and divided nearly £10,090, and
are now running 10 heads on what would be called mul-
lock if it did not go from 2oz. to 3oz. to the ton. If this
mine is as good as it looks, it is one of the mineral wonders
of the world, but what it is really worth remains for time
and work to prove.

Quite a sensation in Melbourne last week,
created by exhibition of the Bingara (N.S.W.) diamonds
just returned cut from London. Shares (£lOO each)
enquired for at £2lO. No div. has been paid. No water
was the first excuse. Too much water is the present
lament. It would be interesting to know how much hard
cash the 1200 £lOO shares (fully paid) realty represent.
Mt. Drysdale mine (N.S.W.) advertising for a
manager. This show paid £28,000 in divs. (went in debt
£lOOO to pay the last), spent £lO,OOO to get the gold out
of 1400 tons of stone, and erected a mill when the rich
streak had almost disappeared. Rig returns from a
pocket and bigger newspaper gush boosted the shares up
to £BOO, and the sorrow of the men who bought at big
prices is indescribable.
* « * •
In J. B. Jaquet the N.S.W. Mines Dept, has
at any rate one man unafraid to venture an opinion in his
reports. Big things have recently been said of the rich-
ness of Surface Hill, Wattle Flat, N.S.W. Says Jaquet:
“ I am of opinion that the chances are against any of
these pyrites veins being worked profitably at a depth.
Their great richness at the surface is without doubt due
to a natural process of concentration. The hill was
originally much higher than we see it to-day. Under
the ordinary processes of denudation the rock, of
which it is composed, and the auriferous pyrites veins
ramifying through it, would be slowly worn away. The
pyrites veins would succumb before the country rock, and
the greater portion of the gold as it was set free would
fall downwards and be retained within the walls of the
veins.”

* « * •
.James Thomson, formerly of Melb. Standard,
tells in a Melb. paper of the early glories of the Mur-
chison (W.A.): —“One of the first sensations is Ains-
worth’s Last Chance. They had not been working long
before they struck stone that was so rich that it returned
2000 oz. to the ton by the primitive means of dollying.
There were five partners in the find, and they decided to
erect a small three-head battery. It was an extraordinary
thing, but the machinery had to be stopped on more than
one occasion owing to the gold working up between the
stampers and clogging portions of the battery On
one of the many occasions that I have been to this mine I
brought up the ladders in my own hands a small quantity
of quartz which I dollied myself for a return of 440 z. of
pure gold to the value of £4 Is. per ounce. It is one of
the show places of the Murchison, and generally regarded
as one of the crack mines. Further on there is
another show-mine, the Goleonda, which was originally
worked in a primitive fashion in the earlier days
of its existence. I have seen the owners dis-
tributing among themselves the week’s proceeds,
which were pickle-bottles full of dollied gold. A few
pennyweights more or less in one of them did not signify.
In fact, tiie Murchison has had sensational proceedings
as well as Coolgardie. but no one was booming the Mur-
chison, and consequently little was heard of it.” And yet
the total value of all the gold produced from the great
Murchison field, according to the official returns, is not
much over 100,000 oz 150,0000 z. all told. Seventy-
five tons of stone from J. Thomson’s wonderful mine
would have accounted for it all, leaving the several hun-
dred other mines to produce nothing among them.
Westralia is a weird land where 72 mines, yielding from
lOoz. to lOOoz. to the ton are discovered every week, and
bucketfuls of pure gold are hauled out of shafts and
served round in pickle-bottles—and yet the net result,
when it gets into cold print, shows that, barring S.A.
only, Westralia is, so far, the very poorest gold province,
in proportion to its size, in Australasia.
W. A. gold output for March is declared to be
11,0840 z. On this “auspicious occasion” the Murchison
beats Coolgardie, the respective returns being 45940 z. and
46430 z. Dundas comes along with its 210 z., and even
beats Ashburton, with 9oz. No wonder the W.A. boom
in London is dead. A large increase is promised in the
near future. It has been promised for two years, and the
same old wheeze—want of water, machinery, proper gold-
saving processes, railway communication, &c., are doing
duty still. The writer admits the probability of an in-
creased output shortly. It may be more than double this
month’s return. It would want to be 10 times the quan-
tity to pay the cost of its getting, and to disprove The
Bulletin’s contention that the W.A. gold-boom will be
known as the great fraud of the century.

Fulton’s 20-head stamp mill, erected on the
Murchison for public crushing, having proved a dead
letter in that respect, has been sold for removal. Its
failure is excused on the ground that the syndicate own-
ing it had no working capital. True, it takes money to
run a public battery at Cue.

These mistakes always occur one way.

Cue wire : “Another shoot of ore has been
struck in the Mainland Consols, and several bags of rich
stone have been secured.” How consoling to the British
shareholders, who bought this mine at 10 times its value,
and have since spent on it far more than it is worth.
“ Several bags of rich stone ” well describes it. A manager
who came from England to look after it was so amazed at
the mine that he left it. His report is :An occasional
bagful of good ore, but the bulk too poor to crush.

Monzies wire: “The new find reported from
Mt. Margaret district, a couple of days ago, proves to be
a very big thing.” We are then told of a few rich slugs,
and that the find has been called the Golden Cliff, no
doubt because gold “can be traced all over the hill.”
Reported only two days, and yet proved to be a very big
thing'. It may be —but all the same this is the sort of
stuff from W.A. that has made the Australian public tired
and at last has disgusted J. Bull, Esq., himself. What
has become of all the other Mt. Margaret pocket-finds
na le over a year ago ? The Queen of the Earth, to wit,
whose gold-shoot could be covered with a man’s hat?

starting of the Menzies’ line fills Kalgoorlie with inarticu-
late wrath.

Stated that there is a move in London to amalga-
mate Barley's and Bavley’s South with a view of finding
£50,000 working capital. The first has a capital of £480,000,
but none to work on. It is a great object-lesson of the public
“ haves” committed in the name of mining. Where are
the waste dumps which experts and every metropolitan
newspaper in Australia described as worth 2 to 3oz. to the
ton? Unmoved and uncrushed because they are worth-
less. Where are the rich finds made in every level of the
mine according to public wires? Echo replies that they
were never made. Eor months the mine lived on accumu-
lated gold got out of the mine many months before. Its
richness went out at less than 100 ft., and could have been
covered in a hop, step and a jump in length. Its barren-
ness was palpable to every man who could distinguish a
mine from a lime-kiln, but the newspapers, partly
through the dense ignorance of their representatives,
fostered this gigantic boost, and have never had the com-
mon decency to apologise.

of England mine, W.A., is spread over the news columns

Madame Berry, 197th 3s. div. Will easily reach
the 200th, and put up an Australian record.
* * * *
On tonnage average, Elaine (Vic.) stands on top
of the other Australian fields, barring Charters Towers.
Equipped with good pumping-plants, several of the an-
cient good yielders are to test the country below water-
level, where development stopped many years back on
payable stone. Elaine mining is singular for the absence
of crosscuts; beyond working the lodes no attempts
have been made to see what the outside country con-
tained. Thoroughly opened up, the field may yet take a
good position.

The Queensland leper, who was practically
suspended during the elections, is now being dis-
covered almost daily. The latest one comes from
Warwick, where he dealt in fruit—which is a
pleasant thing for Warwick to reflect upon.
Further lepers are daily expected.
Hospital Saturday, throughout N.S.W.,
has been fixed for May 2, on which occasion all the
beauty of the province will turn out in its nicest
frock and beguile the passer-by of his loose change
in the sacred cause of charity. This paper trusts
that it will be even more successful than in pre-
vious years, and that the passer-by will part with
his change in a cheerful spirit.

Thus the Townsville Chinese in an address
to Gov. Lamington : —“ That your Excellency and
the flower of your house (Lady Lamington), who,
on account of her beauty, must have been born in
Su-chow, may enjoy every blessing, is the wish
of”—Yee Woh Loong, Chum Wall Y ee, etc. Is
Lady Lamington’s beauty then of the Mongolian
type V Born at Su-chow ! Great Scott ! what a
compliment!

“ His Excellency was afterwards escorted
to the refreshment-room ” —(stereotyped line in

* * * *
The homeless woman found dying of con-
sumption the other day on a doorstep at Carlton
(Melb.), was named Blanche Alexandra Francis.
The name suggests the heroine who lounges in a
gilded boudoir in the London Journal. But how
sadly out of place it sounds on a damp doorstep in
a great, cold, busted city. Blanche Alexandra
couldn’t even get into the hospital, for it was
crowded with typhoid patients, so a kindly widow
took her in and cared for her till she died.
* » * *
According to Northern papers, the mis-
sionary ship John Williams is manned by a
Polynesian crew, the officers finding them just
as efficient and obedient as Europeans. The
Bulletin would suggest that the missionary ship
be also manned by Polynesian missionaries, instead
of Europeans. Logic is logic.

Baby Brassey and
baby Freeman-Thomas—
uncle and nephew—take
morning airings in Melb.
Botanic Gardens in
paniers on each side of a
mule led by a gorgeously-
caparisoned “Jeames.”
Most of the ride they
fight vigorously across
the mule’s spine, one “ on
the point ” meaning a
descent into the panier s
depths. At present,
honors are about even,
say regular spectators
who act as referees on the
quiet.

A slice of freshly-
cut cactus-leaf, applied
to a black eye within a
few hours of the “acci-
dent,” will, it is said,
in 24 hours effect a cure.
The propagator at Syd-
ney Botanical Gardens
says that he has many
applications for cactus,
‘ ‘ especially after holi-
days.”
The cleverest bit of acting in the per-
formance of the “Birds”of Aristophanes, at Melb.
University, the other day, was that of the Greek
priest who begins the sacrifice to the bird-gods.
It was a capital caricature of the sanctimonious
drawl of a high-church curate reciting the Litany,
and was so well done that the clergymen present
were by no means pleased.

Iri the window of a city shop during
Easter week there was exhibited a large crude
painting representing a young girl in a bedroom
in a state of nudity, being urged by a figure
in the orthodox form of the devil in the direc-
tion of the bed. Underneath this bed a serpent
was coiled, while a ticket in bad handwriting
beneath the picture bore the one word, “Sin.”
A footnote stated that the painting was the handi-
work of a rev. gentleman, and that another picture
of the same kind, entitled, “ The Result of Sin,”
by the same artist, might be seen inside on pay-
Photos, by A. B, Clinton, Townsville.]

The wretched N.S.W. woman, convicted
the other day of poisoning her husband with
strychnine, will possibly not be hanged, she hav-
ing, it is said, been previously charged at the
local court with insanity. Ihe history of the
crime boiled down : There was no baby, and, at
the mature age of 45, she avenged her grievance
with the deadly alkaloid. The feature m the
case was that, though she was possessed of LI ,
she declined to engage counsel, but insisted on
defending herself despite th“ T ''dge s warning.

Editor Bulletin, — You have a lot to say
about Jay Pees just now, but let me assure you
that the stipendiary magistrates of the small
country towns are not all immaculate. The out-
side suitor usually comes off second best urdess the
case is very dry and plain. Also, the lawyers of
the small towns won’t push cases against local big
wigs who can put plenty biz. in their way. When
you have a law-suit in, say, Gerabundamine, if
you are a Uitlander you will, if you have sense,
bring an outside lawyer to fight your case —one
who has no business connections in Gerabunda-
mine. An expensive plan, truly, but the only way
to keep the local trap and beak in order. I talk
from experience.—H.
Westralia is just now a happy hunting-
ground for the journalistic blackmailer. Those
engineering shady concerns are always good game
while honest men, if worked judiciously, will
generally come to terms on the score of expe-
diency, accepting blackmailing as one of the con-
ditions of the country—just like the sand and flies
and the absence of water. The “ publicity ”of a
great many journals is a marketable commodity,
exchanged sometimes for cash, sometimes for ad-
vertising. Some much-advertising Westralian
visitors could speak feelingly on the subject. On
hearing that a certain young “ pusher ” was on his
way to the country, a much-professing journal
began gathering together all the nasty comment
on the youth which was available. And published
it on his arrival, of course, so that brazen empti-
ness might be confounded ? Not a bit of it
Showed it to him. The young man shelled out.
Gov. Buxton, from one point of view, lias
proved a failure. Since his arrival in S.A. lie has
given Adelaide society absolutely nothing to talk
about. Apparently H.E. goes to bed at 8.30 p.m.
and never goes round the town o’ nights in a
hansom.

In a recent M.L.
divorce-suit. She was
ordered to give the three
children over to the hus-
band, who had secured a
decree. Then she opened
up on the Judge, and said
that neither he nor his
law could tear the young-
sters from their mother —
‘‘ I will rather tie them
round my waist and
jump into the harbor.”
Here the Bench raised
its eye-brows, but the
poor stricken woman con-
tinued : “ Possession is
nine points of the law
—well, He will have to
prove they are his child-
ren, and it’s a wise father
who knows his own
child.” Exactly and
that is why the law of
most countries has up to
now provided that adul-
tery alone shall be, a
ground for divorce in the
case of the woman and
not of the man.
A member of the financial squad, released
from gaol after serving a long term, was lately
married at a suburban, church with considerable
pomp and circumstance. The engagement was
made previous to the investigation of the gentle-
man s accounts —an investigation which caused
delay m the consummation of the happy event.

LOVE, IN THREE ASPECTS.
(FROM HEINE.).
Awake, sad lyre, the old, old song
That ruled thee once with empire strong,
All other themes above ;
The angels, they call it Heaven’s joy,
The devils, they call it Hell’s annoy,
And men, they call it Love !
Christchurch, M.L.
J. LIDDELL KELLY.
A BIRD-SONG.
A mission'ry's calling I try to combine
With the trade of a Melbourne J.P.,
Which is mighty obliging of one with a fine
Flow’ry genealogical tree ;
For I claim descent true from (Treat Brian Boru,
And from Shakespeare and Caesar and Pym,
And Kong-foo-tsee and old Mo of Judee,
And the rest of the Scriptural swim.
For I’m Dicky Bird, Dicky Bird, tweak, tweak
tweak,
The poetry flows when I speak, speak, speak ;
I’m a sort of impossible freak, freak, freak,
A modern immaculate beak, beak, beak.
It s always been one of my hobbies to seek
Each feminine golden-haired sinner,
And start by a method considered unique
To develope the good that is in her ;
So why should they cavil if into some park
Of an evening we happen to stray ?
For the best of development’s done in the dark-
bo the expert photographers say.
For I’m Dicky Bird, &c.
[Several other verses of this deleted by order.]
FOR BETTER OR FOR WORSE.
My Amy, on her good days,
Is the sweetest creature going ;
We gaily chat of this or that
The while she does some sewing;
From time to time I pinch, or pat,
Her cheeks with pleasure glowing.
On Amy’s very good days
No wrathful storm I could, raise
To vex her with its blowing;
I’m “ Dear old boy ! my cup of joy
Is full to overflowing.
But Amy, on her bad days,
Has “ tantrums ” past all bearing ;
We snarl and snap, until, mayhap,
I quit her presence swearing ;
Her face, which well deserves a slap,
A scornful smile is wearing.
On Amy’s very bad days
She drives me into mad ways,
Dislike for me declaring.
Her parting scoff is “ Break it off ! ”
I totter home despairing.
She has her inbetween days,
When (though I’ve known her kinder)
I call her ‘ ‘ pet, ” and much regret
The swear-words that maligned her;
She tells me not to feel upset,
And says I “ mustn’t mind her.”
On Amy’s inbetween days,
Bewitched by her serene gaze,
My blind love groweth blinder ;
I kiss her brow and make a vow
To take her as I find her.
Melb.

The very newest Vic. craze is to be the
breeding of dachshunds. Lady Brassey and
Govt. House have contracted a consuming love
for them, and “ society ” is now “ putting on dog ”
Gov. Brassey has several highly-bred dachshunds
in quarantine ; about the only other pure-bred
ones in Victoria (also in quarantine) being four
owned by two well-known footballing Melb.
brothers, who have received over 200 offers for a
litter shortly expected.

A FABLE.
A lam stood on a stony rise with very little grass,
When by that way a kangaroo did per adventure pass.
“ Oh ! pretty lam,” the old man crys, “ why do you feed
on sand,
When over yonder desert lys the rich of richest land.”
And, seizing up the lam, he guyed, nor did he stop or tire
Till on the other blooming side he found tha’d bin a fire.
They warn’t half a blessed blade or drop of water round,
The ashes what the tussicks made was all the feed they
found.
“ Ah ! better bare the hills we have,” the lam soliliqide,
“ Than fly to others known not of,” then turned it up and
dide.
Melb. “Herald lately quoted a Yankee
description of Copenhagen, wherein it was de-
scribed as the dullest city on earth —no murders
had occurred there for 20 years, and social scandals
were unheard of. The following day editor Reay
was besieged by a passageful of indignant Danes,
one of whom managed to get a letter inserted
denying the ’Murkan’s statements point blank.
“ What rubbish to talk of one murder in 20
years,” writes this white-haired patriot: “It is
more likely to be 20 in one year, and, as for social
scandals, the place is full of them !” Hang a man
who won’t stick up for his country !
Dear Bulletin,— We identification.
Here is a curious (and literally true) experi
ence. Strolling up Bourke-street, Melbourne,
one night. X was overtaken by a woman, who
requested me to pay for the pie which 1 had
just abstracted from her stock. Utterly be-
wildered, I went back with her to her pastry-
cook’s shop, and was promptly identified by
the boy behind the counter as the person who
had just eloped with a pie. The thing was
ridiculous, but the woman was firm. Dither
payment for the pie or- —a policeman 1 I
wouldn’t pay, and was marched off accord-
ingly to the station. But the sergeant in
charge, who happened to know me pretty
well, dropped on to his subordinate
pretty hard. “ Get back to your
me man ; you’re a janius,
an’ no mistake ! Meet me to-
morrow at 2, Mr. X, an’ we’ll see
if we can’t clear this up.” Next
day we went to the shop, the boy
was produced, and to him the
sergeant, in a voice of thunder:
‘•Tell the truth, ye young divil, or
I’ll take ye away wid me ! Didn’t
ye ate that pie yerself, an’ then, for fear twould be
missed, ye pinted out the first man passm as
havin’ tuck it ? ” And the boy, blubbering, owned
up.—X. [The correspondent happens to be a
newspaper-man. The policeman, therefore, had
against him a strong priina facie case of pie-
stealing.—Ed. B.]
• • * *
A weird, strangely-ruinous Chow was run
down, the other day, in Sydney. For 18 months
past this specimen has been known to be in active
decomposition, but though his hands and .feet and
the other visible parts of him were m a state too
awful for description he was allowed to supply the
infected vegetable to Belmore markets without
interference until one day last week. Ihen the
intelligent constable overhauled him and took him
to Little Bay with much difficulty, the ancient
and fish-like adversary making a desperate resist-
ance. It is officially announced that tin case is
not leprosy but only psoriasis which is about as
scaly as the other thing though not quite so viru-
lent This fact shows that the public should not
rush too hastily to conclusions about its leper, and
should sort him out carefully to avoid
standings, and that when it finds the microbe
dreaming on the dinner-cabbage it should inspect
it in a dispassionate spirit and not jump to a
decision without sufficient evidence.

The Melb. burglar must feel the hard
times keenly. Two of the trade lately burgled an
undertaker’s shop at North Brighton, and were
preparing to load their spring-cart with coffins,
and coffin-plates, and various materials for up-
holstering caskets and beautifying corpses, when
the undertaker’s daughter, bursting upon the
scene. “ went for ” the marauders with a coffin-lid,
SCENE IN OXFORD STREET, SYDNEY.
and routed them with bloodshed. The burglars
were arrested in their wild flight by various con-
stables, and put away to cool. This attempted
enterprise has created an impression that the
Melb. boomers have been moved late in the day to
bury their own dead.

It is suggested that the recurrence of
heated coal cargoes at Newcastle, N.S.W., may
cast some light on the ghastly disappearance rate
of vessels leaving that port. But when a ship is
destroyed by fire, some vestige of her is usually
left. And she burns slowly : the sailors have
generally ample time to take to the boats, and to
cast overboard a message telling their fate. It is
the sinister peculiarity of vessels disappearing
from Newcastle that the ocean swallows them up
absolutely. Crews, boats, wreckage, messages—
nothing is left behind. Such a constancy of utter
destruction is only explicable on the theory that
shifting cargo founders the ship almost in an

Dear Bulletin, —-
Not only in the towns,
but in the bush, John
Chinaman looks after
his creature comforts.
When a mob of Chows
take even a three
months’ contract, they
at once erect a break-
wind and dig a small
garden. With plants
from the nearest garden
and salading seeds, in
four weeks they have
pak-rhoi, or Chinese
cabbage, lettuces, and
radishes. In summer,
if the contract lasts six
months, they have all
sorts of greens. Sam-
shui, Chinese brandy,
is always in the camp;
very often a bottle of
spirits to entertain
whites with. Never
short of a smoke, they
do not cadge, and they
don’t quarrel. It is
their comfort (as they
want it and acknow-
ledge it) that is the
danger of their resid-
ence. I cannot per-
ceive any diminution
in hired Ch inkies since
the LlOO poll-tax. An-
nual poll-tax or license
to Chow employers is
apparently the only way
to shunt them. —Scotty
the Wrinkler.

* * * *
Some intelligent Newcastle (N.S.W.) col-
liery owners, as well as miners, are said to think
that a strike just now would not be such a bad
thing, for the reason that, as a consequence of
former blundering strikes, men and masters were
glad to resume work on any terms. The latter
entered into low contracts which permit of neither
good wages nor profits. If the men laid down
their tools for a week, such contracts would be
cancelled.
* * * *
In the new mission steamer, John Wil-
liams, the crew’s quarters are placed right aft, and
the berth for the native teachers and their wives
forward—this owing to experience gained on the
old mission boat in which the quarters adjoined.
It was found that this proximity did not chime in
with the seventh commandment—for religion
among the islanders by no means signifies
asceticism.
The duties of civil servants. The statiou-
master at Southern Cross got a letter the other
day from a stray passenger thus “Left my port-
manteau in luggage-room. It is unlocked—you
can’t mistake it. Kindly open it and air and dry
my clothes. Then re-pack them and forward to
me.”
“IT’S A POOR RULE,” &c.
She was a fair, frail, timid thing,
With wild, wide eyes, and he a plain
And portly person slumbering
With strident snores that shook the train ;
And, while he slept, at stations passed
Two dames had left, but nothing broke
His dreams of home, until at last
A jolt severe contused his ear,
And he expanded and awoke,
And found, with tremors in each limb,
That timid girl alone with him.
She crouched, and from her corner eyed
The gentle citizen with dread,
And his perturbed face implied
A wish that he was safely dead.
They neither spoke, but sorely vexed
With grim suspicions watched to see
What awful thing would happen next.
The lovely maid at length displayed
A mad desire to rise and flee.
She’d plainly lost her few poor wits,
And warnings gave of coming fits.
The man arose, the maiden screamed.
He looked out on the flying dales,
Then spoke a prayer (or so it seemed)
And flung himself upon the rails.
The people yelled. They stopped the train,
And to the nearest station bore—
Now crippled, and convulsed with pain—
The portly man, and all the van
Was stained and stippled with his gore.
“ At least,” they say, who tell the tale,
“ Poor chap, he didn’t die in gaol ! ”
Melb.

wasn't half as good, with the brilliant result that Major

Sydney, April 14, 1896.
Mr Dear Moorabinda—
The Agricultural Society’s maiden ball at Sydney
Town Hall was a great social success and a bril-
liant spectacle, with lavish decorations of waving
palms, coats-of-arins, and a pair of floral shields
with Lady Hampden on one side and Sir T. E.
Buxton on the other—the S. A. Gov.’s shield being
rather originally punctuated with a soul-stirring
full-stop after the “Sir.” Otherwise all was well
in a decorative sense. Sustenance was pictur-
esquely spread in the basement, floor and music
were admirable, and the only hitch was the tardy
arrival of the vice-regalities, who long kept the
committee cooling its heels on the marble steps,
and lengthened the w’nite-and-gold card of twenty
dances by several vicarious extras. There was not
a very surging attendance at the Show ball, but
as only a limited number of tickets had been
issued the committee was no doubt well-satisfied
with the turn-out, which was more like a club than
a public ball.
Lady Hampden carried the lovely posy pre-
sented by the Society, which harmonised with her
gown of pale bronze satin with puff ball sleeves
and folds of violet velvet —a coronet of purple
velvet surmounted by three diamond stars and
diamond brooches and necklace being the Y.R.
etceteras. Her Excellency’s gyrations in the
official square were —well, somewhat informal. I
never saw anyone more hopelessly at sea in the
third figure than Lady H. (dancing with the
president of the Agricultural Society) who moved
as stiffly as a ramrod and wouldn’t sanction the
“ hands-across ” movement or hold any lady by
more than I 'the button of h;r love. And Gov.
Hampden standing up with Mrs. John See (in
flesh-pink to the finger-tips and pretty diamonds)
had to be shown the way by Gov. Buxton, who
wears his star like a teething-ring and seems to
possess a very light and fantastic toe indeed.
Lively Mrs. Freeman-Thomas, in cream brocade
and a pink rose or so, created a diversion by
cutting a sort of flip-flap in the second vice-regal
set; Miss Buxton, who always looks as if she has
a lolly in her mouth, but who, like all the vice-
regal damsels, has the art of conversation, wore a
white satin skirt-shaped like a three-cornered tart
and pink trimmings on the bodice ; Miss Brand
wore pink satin veiled in white chiffon ; her sister,
all white; Mrs. Rupert Carington, pink and
white—indeed, pink appeared on everybody, in-
cluding the wife of ex-Premier Parkes in pink
silk and chiffon, who sat beside her rococo
bridegroom and was introduced to the contents of
the dais by a well-meaning aide. The handsome
wife of the vice-president adorned the dais in
cream brocade trimmed with old lace, bands of
Trilby velvet in pansy shades and a snappy dia-
mond necklaoe—her daughter making her debut in
white siljc and lilies of the valley. Another suc-
cessful debutante was a daughter of the Presi-
dent’s, while the pretty daughters of a very active
member of the ball committee looked like a couple
of snow-flakes in white silk and satin gowns.
St. Vincent’s ball-committee made a huge suc-
cess of its ball, at Sydney Town Hall, on Wednes-
day, and set a good example for the coming charity-
ball season, besides providing an object-lesson m
unswerving energy in a singularly good cause.
The fine decorations had been handed down by
the Ag. ball-committee, and an excellent supper
downstairs, as w,ell as a perennial buffet in the
vestibule, gave people plenty to dance on. Two
new waltzes were played—Harry Hyndes’s musi-
cianly “ Mindaribba,” and “The Wisteria,” by a
Victorienne, both of which found favor at once
with waltzers, and the programme of dances was
judiciously arranged. Also, a novelty was’.a
f>almist, who told fortunes to the dense two-shil-
ing crowd in the three galleries, and who came
down to the ballroom later on.
The vice-regal party, sans Lady Hampden,
arrived by fits and starts —first of all the ladies,
headed-by Lady Brassey in amatronly gown of black
satin brocade glistening like a beetle, and enlivened
by pink roses on the corsage and jewels sparkling
here and there. Mrs. Freeman Thomas appeared
in pink and silver; Miss Buxton in pompadour
silk ; and the Misses Brand in white silk and blue
and white striped silk respectively, looking rather
more dashing than usual, Miss Alice Brand carry-
ing a very special committee posy that added
S’eatly to her ensemble. Gov. Hampden, with
aron Brassey and Gov. Buxton, arrived late, hot
from the A. J.C. dinner. These have been busy
times with the Great, who have gone faithfully
through the giddy Easter programme more like
machines than men. By the way, that report
from ’Ome about Lady H. being the better Gover-
nor of the two isn’t being verified in Sydney. Gov.
Hampden is a model Gov. as far as keeping his
engagements is concerned, while the occasions on
which her Ex. has defaulted already run into
dozens.
At St. Vincent’s ball, which was “ plain and
fancy,” the dressing went from the sublime to the
ridiculous. The large feminine committee was
carefully combed up. Mrs. Prendergast, for in-
stance, wore a white silk gown veiled in silvery
net, a cluster of red-and-black poppies on the
shoulder, and magnificent diamonds; Mrs. Slattery
(hon. treas.) was m pink and white chine silk and
lace and diamonds ; Miss Doyle (hon. sec.) in a
smart white silk gown with glittering crystal net
draping the bodice, and a snappy diamond star ;
Mrs. M. H. Stephen (vice-president) wore a good-
looking mixture of pansy brocade and velvet; and
a number of other nice frocks helped to soften the
effect of a colleen in pea-green cap-k-pie, and a
damsel in orange who looked rather out of place
at St. Vincent’s ball. Then a short, substantial
lady in a high - waisted white christening-
robe had evidently come as a baby, and the
ill-fated Marie Stuart (so-called, I suppose, because
of the people who stalk around at fancy balls in a
copy of her duds) did the ball-room with the last
mosquito-net of summer pinned to her back hair.
There was a good percentage of naval youths pre-
sent whom the Admiral had graciously allowed to
sport their tinsel—unlike a previous occasion when
they were all ordered forth in their plain clothes,
though the Ad. himself glittered all over like a
gold-stopped tooth. By the way, I can’t
say I liked the nautical slops of a great
Erench commander. Epaulettes and medals and
gold buttons I can forgive, but when it comes to
little gold anchors in each corner of the hero’s
coat-tails, the whole thing suggests a bon-bon.
Now that women have made the experiment of
attending the yearling-sales at Randwick, these
highly interesting functions will probably become
the fashionable thing to “do.” At the sale of
Duckenfield Park yearlings last week Mrs. ‘ ‘ Jack ”
Eales and a party of ladies were in evidence, being,
so far as I know, the first women to venture out to
Randwick under the circs., although it’s a regular
thing at ’Ome for fashionable women of a sporting
turn of mind to attend the Newmarket sales. And
no wonder. There are few things more exciting
than to hear the figures climbing over one another
while a baby thoroughbred goes romping round
the ring—and more especially when the hammer
is wielded by such a punctilious gentleman as the
A.J.C. secretary. A man who ought to know
says that the presence of ladies at these sales
would liven up the bidding—on the principle, I
suppose, that men like to look big when women
have their eyes on ’em ?
On dit that Lord Brassey for all his bonhammie
is, like the valet to the King of Barataria, a
rathdr touchy man. When the yachting M.L.C.
mistook the newly-painted Sunbeam “ for a col-
lier, by dad,” and said so at the club, it unfor-
tunately happened that Gov. Brassey was just
behind and heard all. Instead of diplomatically
passing it over and accepting the proffered ex-
planation, Gov. B. took it rather huffily, and rela-
tions were for a little while awkwardly strained
between His Ex. and the Sydney yachtsman.
Evidently the Sunbeam is Gov. Brassey’s tender
point.
The complimentary regatta to Gov. Brassey, on
Saturday, was a success to onlookers, for it was a
bright day on the water, even if the howling gale
played the fool with the race-card, and made it
impossible for whole fleets of sailing craft to enter
an appearance. But everybody who owned a
brass button went gadding about the harbor,
which really did look beautiful, with foam on
every wave, and as blue as a hilltop-novel. The
scene round the starting-point was very festive —
all the yachts with an overhead-gear of bunting,
and the trim-looking flagship apparently one mass
of people. Gov. Brassey, in a white, somewhat
cookified cap and a hard-bitten serge suit, was the
most simply-dressed yachter of them all, and paid
frequent visits to the genial commodore of the
P.A.Y.C., on board the trim White Star, in a
“ puffing Billy ” manned by businesslike seamen
in oilskins. Gov. Brassey’s water equipage is
meant to be taken very seriously indeed. There
was a vast amount of visiting between the big
yachts. On the Sunbeam, Lady Brassey (in
creamy serge) was “at home ” by invitation, and
had a long list of callers.
The Commodore’s steam yacht White Star was
in man-o’-war order from end to end, and easily
accommodated a large party, including a globe-
trotting baronet double-banked by a fox-terrier,
the Bishop of Sydney and his sister, and several
Melbournians. Miss Cowper (sister-in-law of the
host) was hostess, and wore a black silk gown with
fichu of cream chiffon and bonnet en suite, while
the Commodore sported the smart blue and gold
uniform of the P. A. Y.C. Sydney yachtsmen cer-
tainly do dress the part—there is absolutely no
fault to find with them from a wardrobey point of
view : and if the brass-button industry ever goes
under it won’t be the fault of the Yachting Club
rules and regulations. Lady Hampden, in a work-
manlike suit of black serge and a fawn felt infantry
hat and feather, lavender gloves, black-spot veil
and an ecru parasol with a polka dot, paid a visit
to the Commodore and took tea on board, going on
afterwards to the Sunbeam “at home” with a
party from the White Star. All the afternoon
there was nothing but visiting—the Bronzewing,
the Ena, the Iduna, and the Era, all having large
parties aboard.
The regatta itself was only half a regatta—but
it was infinitely more amusing than a postpone-
ment. There were plenty rowing events, and
those funny little copper punts, rigged to resemble
the warships they scrape the sides of on a work-
ing day, were the Eltons of the programme, and
made all the fun in the unavoidable absence of
the tub-race. By the way, I must compliment
yachting femininity on the way it turned out for
the Brassey regatta. The dressing was very neat.
One damsel in navy-blue, with a mate’s cap and
no petticoats on, cut a very attractive summer-
periodical-looking figure as she blew down the man-
o’-war-steps with the wind well behind her. Per-
haps the two pluckiest women at the regatta wore
navy blue serge, and adorned the yacht of a

“ The Importance of Being Earnest went
up, to the delight of a large society audience, at
Sydney Criterion on Saturday, and got home a
the round green olive gets home in the midclie
stages of a feast. There was very dressing
in the trivial comedy. Miss Faber played Gwen
doline in two frocks, the chiefest of which vvas a
ribbon brocade in cream, blue and pink, with pale
blue silk sleeves to a dodgy bodme, and big black
hat with peeps of blue. Miss Faber was not
frothy enough to be convincing as the posm 0 , im-
possible Gwendoline who talks of the vibrations
in the name of Ernest, with clasped hands, and
is full of transcendental tommy-rot to the top
curl. Miss Faber would always be thrown away
in a part absolutely without sympathy or sincerity.
Pretty Miss Hardy was “lively m a grey
frock and white chiffon bib. Her song, home-
body told me so,” was very feelingly sung and
gained the encore it deserved. It was the one
touch of natural emotion in three acts, and the
audience, steeped in delightful and entertaining
cynicisms, grabbed at it and hung on to it as the
Rev. Chasuble said he would hang on the lips oi
Miss Prism. t
Jennie Watt-Tanner, as “ Lady Bracknell,
came on at first with some of the buttons of her
gorgeous purple silk and velvet frock undone at
the back —but buttons are a detail in the trivial
comedy, which is full of the brightest and best
pessimism in the modern market. One now and
then suspects the brilliant author of merely stand-
ing well-known sayingson thciroldbaldheads and
even then, if they become infinitely amusing that
way, and if nobody ever dreamt of reversing them
before, it must be accounted for originality on the
part of the man who first got the idea. It doesn t
seem a very clever thing, now, to eat a potato, but
the early man who stopped people from boiling
the tops and throwing the bulb away was
liant genius in his foggy, uncertain day. ‘‘ r lh e
Importance of Being Earnest ” is full of Wude-
isms, the smartest of which have been widely
quoted and so seem familiar; but some lovely
fresh sallies occur in the quarrel-scene between the
women, where the false gush of the Society female
over another of her kind quickly turns to its
native gall when the inevitable man comes along.
Gwen, who has had the best of the battle, rails at
the country : “I should be bored to death.’
“Yes,” retorts Cicely, the simple village maid;
“ that’s what the papers call agricultural depres-
sion.” So honors are easy.
The Governmentouse ball on Thursday may be
counted in as the usual brilliant spectacle which
it seems difficult for any vice-regal snivoo to avoid
being. Cosmeas were the mainstay of the hand-
some V.R. decorations, which is a fortunate
fashion for Guvmentouse to set, since the humble
but attractive flower grows like a weed for the
mere asking, and every woman who goes to
Guvmentouse and adopts its decorations can-
not always afford to have roses out of season
to trim her victuals with. I hear that, at the
Y.R. ball —where the hostess looked good form in
black and white and diamonds, and Lady Brassey
was a study in blue, with court plumes and glisten-
ing ornaments —a good many of the bedizened
guests tore themselves away before 11.30, so as
to catch last trams and things like that to their
distant happy homes. What sort of a chance
has poor cabby of keeping hoof-and-hide together
when people get home from gubernatorial halls of
dazzling light in twopenny tram-cars ?
Sydney Polo Club, under the patronage of the
Governor, holds one of its intensely interesting
sports meetings at the pretty Kensington course
on 28th inst. (Saturday). The fixture, which will
be attended by vice-royalty, is sure to attract a
fashionable gathering. By the way, Gov. Hamp-
den is a tremendous golfer. He goes forth in a
becoming soft felt hat and hits a ball over the flat
country between Bondi and Rose Bay. Fortu-
nately the uninitiated never suspect a golf course.
They think the red flags are surveyors’ marks, or
something to do with a sewer.
The enterprising Sydney photo, man, Eden,
indefatigable organiser of popular concerts, got up
a vast shilling concert at Sydney Town Hall on
Saturday to enable the majority to hear the culti-
vated warblingsateveof debonnair tenor Newbury
and Madame Spada and the members of their
concert co. The bill was fourteen items long, and
included “The Death of Nelsdn,” “Sing Sweet
Bird,” “Sunshine and Ram,” “Come into the
Garden, Maud.” You couldn’t have had a better
programme for a “pop.” Apropos of concerts,
clever little Maud M‘Carthy will visit Australia
next year, and give a series of 30 concerts.
Writes my Melb. correspondent
“ From the crown of her head to her ankles the
American impersonatress of Trilby is a bewilder-
ingly pretty girl. Miss Crane’s arms, wrists, and
hands have caused quite a little sensation on their
own account; and when, in the third act, the
actress appears gowned in a classic white silk robe
embroidered with gold, with her red-gold hair a-
hanging down her back almost to her knees, one
understands the infatuation of the whole of the
male cast of the play. In the book, Trilby is
given five admirers; in the play, she has all the
principals, with the supers, thrown in.

very much d la her favorite Marguerite Gautier
in a trailing peignoir of white silk, with wing
sleeves of white fine lace. She wears with this
gown a bunch of crimson roses, and works in a
snappy little effect at the death by spilling the
rose-leaves all over herself. On the dead white

“ Melb. Society rolled up in bare-busted splen-
dour to the performance of the ‘ Birds ’ of Aristo-
phanes at Ormond College. It was all in Greek ;
but as Society doesn t go to the play to under-
stand anything, that didn’t matter. However,
everyone "made desperate efforts to look learned,
and watched those who undoubtedly did, or were
supposed to know Greek; laughing when these
laughed, and applauding when they applauded.
Society at a Greek play is like a Protestant at a
Catholic mass—it takes its cue from the ways of
the majority. The consequence was that there
was more laughter and applause over the ‘ Birds ’
than at the Princess’s over, say, ‘ The Amazons’ or
‘ The Passport. ’
“ A calico ball is being organised in Melb. for
19th May in aid of the Austin Hospital for In-
curables. The committee has struck a brilliant
idea as regards the calico part of the entertain-
ment. Instead of requesting the guests to array
themselves in cotton garments, each is asked to
bring with her or him not less than two yards of
calico, to be used afterwards by the hospital
inmates. ”
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
Re the “one down, t’other come up’’perform
ances of Newhaven and Coil: these crack two-year-olds
are merely repeating the in-and-out form of many prede-
cessors tliat could be mentioned ; nevertheless, an expla-
nation is due from their respective owners, trainers, and
jockeys, also the public has a right to a full state-
ment of the explanation when it comes. In short, the
public ought to be informed that the starting of Newhaven
and Coil for any race is no guarantee of their fitness to
run, and that either horse is likely to prove 211b. better
than the other on any particular day. Assuming that
they are both of them honestly raced on their variable
merits -and Tiie Bulletin does assume it—there is no
other way of accounting for the unsatisfactory meetings
of Coil and Newhaven, as revealed by the calendar. The
explanation aforesaid would be officially accepted as
“ satisfactory,” inasmuch as there is no written turf law
forbidding an owner to start a popular favorite when the
animal doesn’t appear likely to gallop its best, nor any
regulation compelling a trainer to thoroughly understand
his business.
It makes no difference to cursing punters
whether the trainers of Coil and Newhaven don’t under-
stand their business and don’t know when to expect their
equine charges to run “ up to form,” or whether the
owners of Coil and Newhaven, being possessed always of
reliable information (as far as horseflesh can be reliable)
concerning the fitness or unfitness of their champions,
race them without the least regard for the Turf’s best in-
terests. Ikey Mo gets there all the same, whichever of
these two satisfactory explanations may be the right one.
Yet the public would be spared a lot of unnecessary grief
if a definite understanding of the case were arrived at.
The sport of horse-racing, as we have it in Australia,
comes from England, and The Bulletin can’t be too per-
sistent in mentioning that a Coil-ctt/n-Newhaven state of
affairs wouldn’t appear satisfactory to reputable owners
in England, where crack two-year old form is proverbially
true and consistent. Out here it seems to follow the rule
of see saw, and some light on the subject would give the
Gentiles a chance to see the folly of putting faith in the
“ book.”
* * * *
The A. J.C. Easter Stakes running was so inex-
plicable that it is no wonder that inquiry was called for.
Coil and Newhaven don't appear to be a pair of in-and-
outers, but that something was radically wrong was
evident. Newhaven, with 9.7, won in the commonest of
canters in 3.16—hot time for a canter—while Coil, his
Champagne victor, finished ignobly in the rear. It was a
notorious reversal in both cases, but the public, who
knew they were picking up money laying odds on New-
haven in the Champagne, viewed it from this standpoint
only, and hooted accordingly.
Coil and Newhaven each appear to have certain
days off from turf duty. Coil won V.R.C. Sires Produce
Stakes, but let Newhaven have a turn in Ascot Vale
Stakes. In the A. J.C. Champagne it was Coil’s day, and
in the Easter Stakes it was Newhaveu’s “shift.” An
extraordinary pair, truly. In all their races both have
won easily. Now, which is the better horse?
Courallie’s owners were not over-satisfied with
J. A. Delaney’s riding in the All Aged. He was kept too
far back to catch Delaware in the home run. But what
price the despised M.L. youngster Tire, who dashed past
the pair and won with ease in 1.40-1 —half a second under
the record. That brilliant run from the beaten ruck
confirms the writer’s idea that he will blossom into another
Tirailleur. This horse downed all opposition in M.L..
but was heavily weighted in Australia until Glenloth’s
year. That race was a certainty for him on track-form
with Glenloth, but he got killed.
The pride of St. Albans’ is essentially a “gallery ”
horse, winning or dead-heating in the last few strides
He makes hard work of all his races, and in Cumberland
Stakes only managed victory by a neck. Like Cabin
Boy, the improved Valiant made him go all he knew in
the straight, and his backers weren’t comfortable until he
got alongside the son of Antaeus, who shaped much
better than in S. Cup.
Gough says he has to be in perfect condition to
get Wallace home in his hard tussles. The sluggard won’t
gallop unless he’s thrashed. “ The only way to make him
gee would be to have an electric battery attached to the
spurs.”
A peculiar thing about the All-aged Mile was
that Courallie carried 51b. less than in the Doncaster.
the time was the same, and he could only just place him
self. Even under the most favorable circs, it is probable
that he couldn’t have beaten Tird, who passed him as
though standing still. Delaware, too, is a brilliant miler,
be it remembered, and won the Doncaster last year.
The Harvester ran a most respectable third in
the Cumberland, and would have been much closer hail
he not lost his position at the sheds. He is by no meani
a back-number yet, but isn’t well-conditioned. Toreador
didn’t run up to form. In fact, nothing appeared to run
up to form during the day, bar Wallace.
It was a mistaken notion keeping Cabin Boy in
the rear on the waiting principle in Cumberland Stakes
He tired himself struggling for his head, and when the
field moved he couldn’t go faster. Pace-making is the
youngster’s forte, and he can pace, too. Even if sent
along to the front, he couldn’t have shaped worse than hf
did—last.
The public ought to show profound respect for
Gough’s whip, which has pulled them out of the mud od
many occasions. If ever he happens to drop that whip in
a race, they’ll have to part to the ring, for Wallace will
never win under mere hand and heel persuasion.

given second money, and Favor was somewhere down the
bottom of the straight.

At Mackay (Q.) election 235 votes, which
were expected to change the result of the poll by
ousting a Govt, candidate, were thrown out as in-
formal, because the returning-officer initialled the
front of the ballot-papers instead of the back,
a he possible uses of a system like this are obvious.
The first blow in the Maoriland election
Oftinpaign was struck a few days ago, when an
enthusiastic elector rang the bell of ex-M.L.A.
Henry .Smith h ish, and landed him an awful
whack in the eye as soon as he came out. The
first nose-blood of the battle is, therefore, on
Fish s doorstep.
Minister Gould, on his own account, is
using his spare time codifying the N.S.W. jury
laws ; Premier Reid is going carefully through the
Criminal Code ; and other Ministers are nursing
little things of their own. Hence Chief Justice
parley’s retirement and the general cave-in of the
Law Consolidation Commission.
At an early stage, Mr. “Tom” Slattery’s
in-and-out and round-about reasoning re the
validity of the existing N.S.W. Land and Income-
tax assessment measure stirred several would-be
interveners, but a remembrance of Colin M‘Kay
Smith s relators hip in the expensive payment-of-
members question pulled them back into a state of
holy quiet.
The problem with which the N.S.W. bush
settler is now wrestling is something like this : If
a man buys land from the Govt, at £1 an acre, and
expends labor equal to £2 an acre on the necessary
improvement of clearing, and lays out, say, 10s.
an acre on fencing, and his crops are a dead
failure, and his cow is dead, and his fence is burnt
by bush fires, and the tank, which cost =£3o worth
of hard work to excavate, is dry, and the selling
price of the whole place is now only 18s. an acre -
what is the unimproved value?

H. H. Champion, it is alleged, is going to
contest the Villiers and Heytesbury seat in Vic.
Assembly. Pity he doesn’t save himself for next
September, and run against Sargood for the
Council. The Assembly is reasonably Demo-
cratic already, and it is only wasting a good
Democrat to put him there when the Council
needs him so badly.
Henry Lucy, S.M. Herald’s London corres-
pondent, has changed his opinion about Chamber-
lain since the Jameson episode. Just after the
elections he foresaw that Birmingham Joe would
ride the high horse to his ruin; his permanent
acceptance by the Tories was impossible. Now,
‘ Dizzy’s ” career may open to him. Lucy is a
better Liberal partisan than he is a political critic.
Cabled that American cruisers are assem-
bling in Turkish waters. The English, with 300
odd of the best fighting ships afloat, have been
unable to coerce Turkey, except at the prospective
cost of a general European war. It remains to be
seen what Turkey and her protector Russia will
say to the danger of obliteration by the U.S. and
her formidable fleet of 45 cruisers, gunboats, &c.,
all told.
The Bulletin begins to dream of a time
when there will be an Age-of-Consent party in
Australian politics, in addition to the Tory, Demo-
cratic, Labor, Single-Tax, Land-Tax, Retrench-
ment, Cold Tea, Freetrade, Protectionist, Revenue-
tariff, Bible-in-Schools, Yellow Pup, Holy Roman,
and National Ass parties. And the Age-of-Consent
party promises to be the most unpleasant of the
lot.
The Clarion, on the franchise in Vic-
toria :
The colony ol Victoria has adopted the principle of
“one adult one vote,” and the women of Victoria will
henceforth vote in parliamentary elections.
Victoria “ has adopted the principle,” but the
House of Rich Landlords blocks the way. No
doubt, too, the women of Victoria will “ hence-
forth. ” But whenforth ?
Some prominent S.A. National Ass candi-
dates, when closely questioned as to the meaning
of the words “ employing suitable labor ” in the
Northern Territory, included in the Ass policy,
have explained that “suitable labor” does not
necessarily mean other than white labor. This, of
course, is but a clumsy attempt to deliberately
deceive. What the Ass wants is cheap alien labor
and nothing else. If it meant white labor it would
say so, and not needlessly alienate voters.
Under the original Federation Bill, as pre-
sented to Vic. Parliament, after the Federal Con-
stitution was agreed upon it was to be submitted
to a popular vote, when a majority would decide
whether Victoria should federate or not, and even
if there was a majority in favor the vote was to be
null and void unless 50,000 electors were in the
affirmative. The Upper House gently changed
the 50,000 to 75,000, and now it is a matter for
serious thought whether 75,000 electors can be
induced to worry about the matter one way or
another. Vic. Council evidently thinks not,
which accounts for the fluid deposited inside the
cocoa-nut.

Melb. Argus disgustedly points out that
Kingston and his colleagues in S.A. have not had
to face a single want-of-confidence motion m the
House. Truly a degrading state of things. When
it is admitted'that Ministries go into power merely
to conduct business, and not to figure m a series of
wild and useless “scraps” with the other fellows,
all the light and color and interest will be wiped
out of Parliamentary life, and gentlemen of con-
servative instincts will prefer to give their patron-
age to the p.r. or the rat-pit.
The absentee-tax, carried by the Kingston
Govt, to the accompaniment of Tory howls and
prophesies of national disaster, realised only
d;3600 last year ! According to opposition press
fables, hundreds of thousands of pounds were
“ withdrawn ” from S.A. owing to this iniquitous
tax !

JACK: “ Well, blow me tight if that ain’t the smallest funeral
ever I see’d!”
Gem of S.A. Tory eloquence :
Mr. Kingston was always associated with humbug, in-
tolerance, and autocracy,' and the skin of his carcase hid
one of the worst livers ever known. He also committed
bigamy by marrying every reform that came along, then
murdered them by this forcible espousal, and cemented
them under the hearth of his policy to be unearthed by
the Labor party. Therefore, Mr. Kingston must not ob-
ject to being referred to as the “ political Deeming.”
“ Murdered them by this forcible espousal ” is
good. Even Deeming waited a bit.
Sydney Telegraph: “The Labor-party
. . . stood solidly behind Mr. Reid in his en-
deavor to take some of the burdens off the working
masses, and make wealth and privilege contribute
its share to the public revenue.” Which means,
The Bulletin presumes, that the Labor-party
stood solidly behind Mr. Reid in taking the duties
off pianos, Brussels carpet, jewellery, tapestry art
squares, precious stones, four and six button
gloves, surah silk, marble clocks, American bottled
fruits ’and tinned turkey. This is what is really
meant by “taking the burdens off the working
masses.” 11 Aint it sickenin ?
During the last two or three weeks the
N.S.W. Govt, has steadily eaten and drunk its
way right through Northern N.S.W. Pity that
the daily press, which said so much about the
hard eating and drinking of the Dibbs Ministry,
doesn’t start to remonstrate. Its fetish, who is
short and stout and takes no exercise, and is too
much addicted to sweet stuff, can’t stand this sort
of thing. Dibbs was long and lathy and always
in fighting condition, and could digest a tenpenny
nail on the shortest notice, and banquets only
made him appoint his friends to good billets more
vigorously &ncl damn a littlo harder, but Cr. xi.
Reid is built of softer material, and a few more
banquets will leave him a green and bilious wreck.
The Income-tax promises to prove a
ghastly disappointment so far as concerns the
N S.W. country districts-if one case Ihe Bulle-
tin has heard of is anything like a fair sample.
In a certain rather prosperous township the local
lawyer has, for some time, averaged ,£4O a week
for filling up Land and Income-tax schedules, and,
through much ingenuity and a patient study of the
Act he reckons that he has done the Guv ment
out of =£9oo taxation for that district alone.
Also he reckons that all his schedules will hold
water, and that his clients-most of whom cant
be bowled out by their books for they haven t got
any—will stand any examination if they only re-
member his instructions. One of his inventions
is a new and complete method of proving that the
land thereabouts is practically worthless. Said
lawyer is now refreshing himself with drink after
his labors, which is how some of these things have
leaked out.
A sign of the times in the direction of the
practical abolition of one form of sweating is the
proposal at the M.L. Trades and Labor confer-
ence for the establishment of a State tailoring
workshop. As a proposal equally worthy of atten-
tion, the establishment of State bakeries might be
added.

Q. electors mostly treat
their contingent votes as
strange beasts to which it is
better to give a wide berth.
In the S. Brisbane election,
for example, out of a total of
4,600 votes polled, only 86
were contingent. The system
has therefore had no influence
whatever on the results of the
first batch of elections. It was
originally devised to oust the
horrid Labor man who slipped
in between two Govt, candi-
dates, and applies the basic
principle unjustly enough. So
far, its only achievement has
been the dismissal of ex-Minea
Minister Ilodgkinson to float
co’s in Westralia.
If the Liberals have their
way at next election, there will
be in Maoriland no Japanese,
no private friendly societies, no
distraint for rent, no exorbi-
tant rate of interest, no C.D.
Act, no (legally) unprotected
young girls, no starvation
wage, no excessive boy or girl
labor, no unsupervised clubs,
and (if the people so decide) no liquor. Then there
are to be workmen’s villages, State fire-insurance,
and the audit of public companies’ accounts.
The number of sheep in N.S.W., which
reached the maximum of 61,831,000 in 1891, was
at the end of 1895 only 47,333,000. The decrease
during the 1895 drought is set down at a little
over 10,000,000. The awful luck of ex-Premier ■
Dibbs glares forth conspicuously in these figures.
Dibbs’ protective tariff lasted from the end of ’9l
to the end of ’95. There were four consecutive
good seasons up to the end of ’9l, and in these
four years the increase was over 15,000,000 sheep.
Then, in the four years covered by the Dibbs
tariff, there were two droughts and a decrease of
14,000,000 sheep, so that the protective era came
out 29,000,000 sheep worse than the previous four
years of Foreign-trade. People judge a policy by
the good or bad luck which goes with it, and if it
doesn’t rain in N.S.W. under a protective tariff
then that tariff is bound to go down.
Premier Reid kindly assures the N.S.W.
sugar-planters that when their industry is de-
stroyed by Freetrade they can “ take to something
else,” and suggests dairying. In other words,
they are to root out their cane crops, and sow
grasses, and wait a year or two for them to grow,
and buy cows, and build creameries, and get the
money to do all these things from no place in par-
ticular, and live on nothing in the meantime. The
advice is so affably heartless that The Bulletin
is filled with a great yearning to see G. Reid try-
ing something else also. It would like to throw
open the legal profession to all comets who could
pass an examination, in which case it would be so
swamped that G.H.R. wouldn’t make salt at it.
And then it would tell the distressed old man of
vast circumference to take to something else —
watchmaking or electrical engineering, for in-
stance, or perhaps navigation ; any small thing
that an old man who knew nothing about it could
naturally make a living at right off without the
smallest difficulty.
When Federation arrives, and there are
no border tariffs, and railways and roads are built
to carry produce to the nearest port, wherever it
may be, instead of to the ports of one particular
province, however remote and inconvenient they
may be, Port Augusta should start on a career
that will ultimately make it almost, if not quite,
the greatest seaport in Australia. It lies at the
head of a gulf which extends nearly 200 miles in-
land ; therefore it is so much nearer to the centre
of the continent than such coastal cities as Sydney.
It is the nearest port for all S.A., for all N.S.
Wales west of the Darling and the Paroo, and for
a great part of S.W. Queensland —only the traffio
from these places has been carefully kept away
from it by the want of connecting railways and
good roads, and diverted to Sydney and Brisbane.
Also, it is 1000 miles nearer Europe by sea than
either of them. Port Augusta, with its 757 people
and its one newspaper and its lonesome gaol, is the
natural outlet for a larger extent of territory than
any other place on the Australian coast, and its
depressed state is a lamentable example of how
provincial legislators have insisted on dragging
trade to their own cities, regardless of distance
and expense and all other considerations.

has a ten-year-old daughter nearly as tall as her
pretty mother. The young lady is of strongly
artistic instincts.

cate, states that he has been a Freetrader all his
