Re the yarn about Bishop Julius, of
Christchurch, M.L., saying that the man who put
bad threepenny-bits in the collection-plate wanted
the grace of God very badly —or three months’
hard. When Mr. Julius was archdeacon at Bal-
larat (Vic.), he gained a reputation for saying
smart things, but once fell in badly—at a Bungaree
agricultural show. Reaching a reaper and binder
in company with a party of claqueurs, his rever-
The Late T. P. PUGH.
Photo, by Poulsen, Brisbane.
ence gravely asked the attendant, “ My good man,
is this the celebrated sheep-shearing machine we
have heard so much about ? ” The man looked
for a moment, without replying; then, noticing the
clerical aspect of the questioner, demanded, “ Are
you a farmer, sir?” “No,” replied the fiend-
scarer, “ I’m a shepherd.” “Is that so ? ” queried
the reaper-agent; “ how often do you shear your
flock ? ”
J. N. Gilmour, of Bathurst (N.S.W.), who
died recently, had some “fads.” At the local
show he annually gave a cash prize for the best-
ironed gentleman’s shirt, the work of a woman
who earned her living at the tub, and cash prizes
for the male and female servants showing the
longest record of continuous service in the dis-
trict. He owned much local bricks and mortar.
American papers give more of the inside
about the Ballington Booth ruction, with copious
details. Even a minor thing like this shows the
disadvantage of not having a Pacific cable, so that
everything is filtered through England. Ballmg-
ton complains that, after dismissing him, his
brother Herbert and the rest of the English
deputation informed the public he had resigned.

One imported high N.S.W. railway official
doesn’t yet know everything. As he stalked
abroad at dinner hour on a recent day he saw
several employes demolishing a water - melon.
The new-chum paused aghast. “ Good gracious,
Mr. X.,” said he, turning round to the official
next in rank, who hung faithfully in the rear—
“good gracious ! do these men eat raw pumpkin
for dinner ? ”

The poet Neild, in his recent platform on-
slaught upon rival bards and the newspaper critics,
was understood to infer that some paper or other
had indicated readiness to take =£2o in exchange
for a “ good notice ”of his book. Mr. Neild, if he

terms.
Queen Victoria’s letter, on the death of
Prince Battenberg, reprinted in all colonial
Gazettes, concludes with thanks “for the appre-
ciation manifested of the dear and excellent prince
who lay down his life in the service of his
country.” Confusion of the parts of the verbs “to
lay ” and “ to lie ” is a royal weakness, as witness
the historical advice given by the Prince of Wales
to Lady Mordaunt to lay up when she had the
measles.
The flowers which Mrs. Goodenough, by
the hands of Admiral Bridge, laid on her husband’s
grave in North Shore cemetery last week, recall
the fact that it is now over 20 years since the
Commodore died from lockjaw supervening on
wounds received from South Sea natives. By a
coincidence, the Naval Home, erected to his honor
in Sydney, was never so crowded with “ tars ” as
during the past fortnight, fully 350 occupying the
place on some nights.
T. P. Pugh, of Pugh’s Almanac, died at
Nanango, Q., the other day, aged 04. He was one
of the oldest journalists in Australia, and supplied
Moreton Bay correspondence to Parkes’s Empire
in ’55. He edited in turn the Moreton Bay Free
Press, Moreton Bay Courier (now Brisbane
Courier), and Brisbane Telegraph ; and was a
foremost figure in the agitation for the separation
of the Northern province from N.S. Wales. From
’63 to ’6B he sat in Parliament as a representative
of North Brisbane ; and during the 15 years ended
1870 did valuable work in moulding opinion and
shaping institutions. Then came money embar-
rassments and health failure, and he gradually
faded out of notice. A minor police-magistrate’s
office was provided for him, which he held with
credit till his death. Few Australian pioneers of
thought have done better service in their day and
generation.

A good many people have been waiting to
hear of the summary discharge of Alfred Austin
from the Laureateship, for his alleged poem in
which he glorified the buccaneering Jameson for
making war on a friendly State, and practically
sooled on other people to go and do likewise. The
Laureate is a semi-official person, with a salary
paid out of the public revenue, and the spectacle
of Austin being paid by the Government to glorify
law-breaking and filibustering, while the same
Government is prosecuting the law-breaker at
Bow-street, is a fine piece of hypocrisy.
Maoriland law, the other day, tried in a
slow and feeble way to set a great wrong partly
right by the release of Nils Jacobsen. Jacobsen
was found guilty in 1887 of a murder committed at
Little River, being convicted almost entirely on
the evidence of one Anders Nyman. The murder
was proved by abundance of independent evi-
dence, including the corpse, but only Jacobsen,
Nyman and the deceased were present when it
•was committed—if Jacobsen was really present,
which is doubtful—and Nyman apparently got his
swear in first. Jacobsen was sentenced to penal
servitude for life. Then Nyman, by a curious
accident, was nearly being hanged in New York
for a crime he didn’t commit, and in an outbreak
of conscience or hysterics he swore an affidavit
that he was the real author of the Little River
murder. After that Maoriland justice enquired into
the matter gradually for five years, and having
proved the confession to be authentic it released
Jacobsen when it got fully rested.
There enlisted in the first Vie. Artillery
Corps, in the early seventies, a magnificent picture
of physical perfection—one Terence O’Dwyer,
fresh from the County Clare, 6ft. sin. in his stock-
ings, and as shapely as a Greek god. On the oc-
casion of Terence’s first guard at Govt. House, the
great white-elephant-eyesore on the Domain wag
unfinished, and “ the Bowens ” were temporarily
fixed at Bishopcourt, E. Melb. The corporal of
the guard had posted his new sentry, and carefully
read over to him and explained the routine orders
from the board in the sentry-box. Then came a
verbal injunction: “There’s a tame wallaby in
the grounds ; be careful he doesn’t nip out through
the gates —he sometimes tries.” What a wallaby
was, Terence speculated as he marched briskly to
and fro in the broiling sun. It was the last of four
consecutive days of intense heat, every door and
window was wide open in expectation of a change
—which suddenly came, like a very fury, accom-
panied by clouds of dust. The wild south wind tore
into Bishopcourt, bangingblindsand window-sashes
and whirling flimsy draperies about the rooms. In
the drawing-room a light cushion-covering of deli-
cate lace was violently whisked around the apart-
ment, and finally it flew out of the window. Lady
Bowen, who had witnessed this, called excitedly
to the placid A.D.C., “Oh, Major Pitt, do try
and recover that antimacassar ; it was my mother’s
own work.” The gallant major hurried out and
vainly searched the grounds. It occurred to him
to question the sentry. As he approached, Ter-
ence, with the assistance of his carbine, had
assumed a paralytic attitude, which might have
been recognised as a caricature of a “ present ”
(arms). “ Haw ! sentry,” drawled the A.D.C.,
“did you see an antimacassar fly through the
gate?” Terence’s face was lit up with an apolo-
getic and deprecatory smile as he replied, “ Oh,
the ‘Macasser.’ Shure the divil himself wouldn’t
shtop thim ‘Macassers.’ He wint past me wit
tree leps, and away into the gyardens foreninst
before I could shtur a shtep to shtop’im.” Ter-
ence, has since learnt the difference between a
wallaby and an antimacassar.
There was once in N.S.W. a judge noto-
riously fond of the fair sex. A case was tried
before him in which the plaintiff was an exceed-
ingly pretty woman—so pretty that defendant’s
counsel hardly attempted to directly win the case,
but contented himself with reserving numerous
points for appeal. However, he did not get his
appeal; for, when the judge’s notes were seen,
they were found to contain nothing more than the
name of the pretty plaintiff—and her address.
Rev. Zillman, a German-Australian parson
located in Western Victoria, and four years ago
one of the chaplains at the Tombs prison, New
York, proposes to start a labor-church m Mel-
bourne, on the lines of the new English labor-
church. Mr. Zillman is a tall, clean-shaven, grey-
haired, much-married cleric who is generally at
loggerheads with his official superiors, and has had
quite a Bohemian career. He has dabbled much
in printers’ ink, and for a short time edited
Toowoomba (Q.) Gazette.
According to the report of the Lormer
Board, the enthusiastic teetotaller who instituted
the proceedings comes under the legal definition
of a lunatic. The law recognises as insane any-
body who can’t distinguish right from wrong.
Judge Williams and Co. now state that Lormer
had a distinct bias against the liquor-trade,
and though he adjudicated with such bias, he
did not know he possessed it, and considered he
was acting rightly. Which is another way of say-
ing that Lormer did not know right from wrong
and that he is a legal lunatic.
An Australian lawyer-politician, who had
enjoyed himself in the amorous East, was, on his
return, consulted by a well-known cleric concern-
ing Japan —what sort of a place, he asked, was it
to spend a holiday in ? For answer he received a
glowing description of the wonders and delights of
the land of the Mikado. “But tell me,” inter-
rupted the clergyman, “is it the sort of place I
could take my wife to?” “ Well,” replied the big
man, “I suppose you could take your wife there—
if you wanted to. But, look here, you wouldn’t
take a ham-sandwich to a Lord Mayor’s banquet,
would you ? ”
Ballarat East’s chief exhibit is “ Bill ”
Green, Vic.’s oldest identity, who has spent 58 out
of his 80 years in that province. This hale old
party anchored in Port Phillip, per Royal George,
m ’3B, when Sewerville counted lialf-a-dozen pubs,
and churches and a stray theatre (J. T. Smith’s).
Green was in the rally of Western district station-
hands that ‘ ‘ dispersed ” 300 Mount Shadwell
blacks for spearing sheep. For this the station
managers were indicted, but acquitted. Green
recollects the stocks that from ’4l to ’45 stood on
the site of Melb.’s Custom-house, and he passed
over Ballarat in ’39, when the Golden City was
unthought of.
Vic. Justice Hodges, the other day, gave a
young man four years for stealing two umbrellas.
Prisoner had three larceny sentences already re-
corded against him. Those in court were of
opinion that perhaps half of this extraordinary
penalty was due, though no doubt his Honor was
quite unconscious of the fact, to the circumstance
that this youth corrected the Judge on a point of
logic. “You have a very serious record,” said
Mr. Hodges. “ This is the fourth time you have
been found guilty of larceny.” “Yes,” came
from the dock, “and I have suffered for that.”
Then the Judge jerked out “Four years,” and
the prisoner was hustled from the dock.
Mr. J. C. WILLIAMSON.
Photo, by Talma, Melbourne,
Purves, Q.C., is not likely to conduct the
prosecution of another Melbourne journal in a
sporting-libel case, or any other sort of libel-case
wherein the defendant-layman fights personally
for his own hand. The legal theory that Cham-
pion didn’t know enough law to withstand the
furious onslaught of plaintiff O’Halloran and his
counsel was made to look very foolish when the
pair of professionals retired with their tails between
their legs. Inasmuch as Champion wasn’t a lawyer,
and didn’t care what he said about the men who
were posing as specimens of turf-purity, he had a
soft thing on. He couldn’t have got any hired
counsel to strike terror into the hearts of two mem-
bers of the shark-persuasion. Not much. Dog
sometimes bites dog, but it rarely or never eats
dog.
The first Australian burying-ground was
on the site of the present Sydney Town Hall. A
Sydney Gazette of 1804 contained a proposition
to fence in the ground, in consequence of the pigs
rooting up the earth and graves.
The other day N.S.W. Solicitor-General
Reid actually filed a bill against a couple of men
who had given a bill-of-sale over a crop which
had not actually then been sown and who had
sold that crop when it was procured. As was
pointed out by Crown-Prosecutor Wise, the Act
made no provisions for lien over anything which
did not exist. '
Three poets, in one age of glory born,
Our Jumpingkangarooland did adorn :
The first in writing horsey verse surpassed,
The next in footy verse—in both the last :
The force of Nature could no further go—
To make the third, she joined the other two !

The new business for 1895 was greater than that for 1894 by 121 policies, assuring £28,740 ; and

114 claims occurred during the year, under 117 policies, to the amount of £32,275, which is

The claims have decreased year by year since 1890, when they amounted to £45,371 11s. lid.
The total income for the year was £155,387 4s. 7d.
The interest earned for the year was more than sufficient to pay all claims and bonuses.
The expenses show an increase of only £ll2 18s. Bd. over those for 1894, although the new business,
which is responsible for the greater portion of the total, produces, as above mentioned, an addition of
£B6O 7s. 2d. to the income.
As the fifth quinquennial investigation, as at the close of the year, was in progress, the directors
made a special investigation of the assets. The society’s premises were revalued on the basis of present
net rentals, and in Melbourne, Sydney, Brisbane, Launceston, and Auckland, it was decided to write
off £56,242 12s. r >d. In Adelaide and Dunedin no change was made.
The properties which had fallen into the society’s hands and had either been foreclosed upon oi
were simply in the society’s possession as mortgagee were subjected to a most careful scrutiny, both at
head office and at branches. With few exceptions they also were valued on a neb rental basis, and
=£22,522 14s. sd. decided to be written off. Out of the profits of the quinquennium, a further sum will
be transferred to the credit of the investment fluctuation fund account to provide for any possible
depreciation in the whole of the securities, as specified in clause 76 of the articles of association.
The funds at the close of the year amounted to =£865,872 7s. Id., as compared with £662,761 Is. 6d.
at the close of 1890, showing an increase for the five years of £203,111 ss. 7d.
The directors wish to point out that the average rate of interest earned on the funds, invested and
uninvested, was £5 Is. lOd. per cent. ; that when a security falls into the society’s hands, or is believed
to be doubtful, no interest nor rent is taken credit for unless actually received ; and that the small
amount of outstanding interest in the balance-sheet, the greater portion of which has since been paid,
is an evidence of the soundness of the securities. Attention is drawn to the auditor’s certificate in
this respect.
The usual actuarial investigation of the liabilities of the society has been made, and the results are
reported to you separately.
The two directors who retire at this meeting, in terms of the articles of association, are the Hon.
Thomas Loader and Dr. Alexander Morrison. They are eligible for re-election, and have given notice
of their intention to offer themselves accordingly. (Note. —Since the report was issued, the Hon.
Thomas Loader has withdrawn his candidature, and will not offer himself for re-election.)
The directors have to report with deep regret the death of Mr. Caleb Peacock, chairman of the
Adelaide local board, and of Mr. D. B. Cruicltshank, of Auckland, both of whom were appointed at
the opening of these branches in 1879 and 1883 respectively, and have rendered valuable service to the
society. Neither vacancy has been filled.
The auditors, Messrs. A. W. Cleveland and C. M. Holmes, also retire in terms of the articles of
association, and have given notice of their intention to offer themselves for re-election.
JOHN B. GILLISON, Actuary and General Manager.
WILLIAM SIDDELEY, Chairman.

Judges Tindal and Littledale, in London,
tried similar bigamy cases the same day. Says
one: “I gave the scoundrel seven years.” Says

A Russian Finn, accused of chewing off a
fellow-sailor’s ear, pleaded guilty to Melb. Bench
last week, but asked for leniency on the ground
that he was starving.
One of the objects of the lately-appointed
N.S.W. Board of Exports is, according to the
Board, “ the appointment of an expert in London
to keep the manufacturing classes there in touch
with our timber and to teach them its uses.”
Considering that N.S.W. spends about £300,000
a year on imported timber, isn’t the first duty of
the Board to appoint an expert to keep the country
in touch with its own timber and to teach the
Cornstalk the uses thereof ?

Australian Centenarian.
101 YEARS OF AGE.
HOW SHE PROLONGED HER YEARS.
We doubt if but few care to grow old. There is a
dread about losing one’s strength, eyesight and
hearing, and a fear of becoming a burden to one’s
friends.
One of the oldest—if not the oldest—Australians
has an important secret to divulge which illus-
trates that the trials of old age can be delayed, so
that when the years do creep on apace, one can
grow old gracefully, and as long as life endures
have a fair measure of.physical strength.
Mrs. O’Rjgan, of 4.(1 Rippon Street, Ballarat,
writes us :—“That her Mother” —now living in
Windsor, N.3.W.,—“ is 101 years old ; well and
active, and that it is through the use of Warner’s
Safe Cure that her life, has been prolonged.” She
was able to keep the blood pure ; kidneys active,
and the bowels regular by its use. If these im-
portant organs are strengthened and regulated
occasionally in this manner, the so-called “Terrors
of old age ” prove to be myths, and advancing
years but a kindly winter. *
DON’T GO TO SEA WITHOUT A BOTTLE
OF ENO’S “ FRUIT SALT.” “ From a town in
British Guiana, South America. —J. C. Eno, Esq.,
London. —Sir,—After two years’ trial of your
excellent ‘ FRUIT SALT, ’ I can safely say that
it has saved me much misery from colonial fevers,
indigestion, and impaired appetite, to which I
have been subject during eleven years’ residence
in the tropics. It is invaluable to travellers as a
preventive of sea-sickness and a relief from the
other ailments of life aboard ship; and, for myself,
I would as soon think of going a voyage without
my tooth-brush as my bottle of ‘ FRUIT SALT.’
With ordinary care it does not get hard and caked
as other effervescent preparations do in warm and
humid climates, and this is greatly in its favor. —I
am, Sir, yours respectfully, W.J.8.” Sold by all
chemists and stores.
Public Notices.
jVTOTICE is hereby given that it is the intention of
BENJAMIN CRISPIN SIMPSON, of Sydney, in the
colony of New South Wales, Member of the Institute of
Civil Engineers, Esquire, to apply to the Parliament of
New South Wales during the next session for a Bill to
authorise the said BENJAMIN CRISPIN SIMPSON, or
his Assigns, to construct a Bridge and Approaches thereto
between the South and North Shores of Port Jackson, the
said Bridge and Approaches to commence at a point in
Cumberland-street, in the city of Sydney, about thirty-
three chains fifty links north from the intersection of
Cumberland-street with Charlotte-place, in the city of
Sydney to Dawes Point, thence to and crossing the Waters
of Port Jackson to Milson’s Point, North Sydney, thence
to and terminating at a point in Alfred-street, about ten
chains fifty links north of the intersection of Dind-street
and the said Alfred-street, in the borough of North
Sydney ; to authorise the building over and diversion of
streets or roads; to authorise the appropriation or use of
certain lands required for or in connection with the carry-
ing out of the said work ; to make provision for compen-
sating the owners of and others interested in such lands ;
to authorise the imposition of tolls on persons using the
said bridge ; to confer powers for the making and enforce-
ment of bylaws ; and for all other purposes which may be
incidental thereto. Dated at Sydney this 13th day of
April, 1896.
LEONARD GIBSON, 92 Pitt-street, Sydney, Solicitor
or the Bill.

Though none of the yearlings, sold the other
day, fetched IOOOgs., as Coil did last year, breeders were
quite satisfied with results. Mr. W. Bailey secured three
plums in the Carbine—Marchesa colt(ssogs.); Abercorn —
Moonstone colt (300g5.), and the Gossoon —Spice gelding
(300g5.). Etraweenie’s brother, a fine youngster by
Trenton —Nellie, brought over from Vic., went to Mr. W.
R. Wilson at 350g5., and Mr. Ike Foulsham gave 285 gs. for
the Splendor—Esperance colt. Dan. O’Brien, whose luck
seems to have gone on a holiday, only secured the
Autonomy—Bangle colt (255g5.) after spirited bidding.
He meant to have him, and anything Dan. fancies he
generally gets. In M.L. once, though, he luckily met his
master. After bidding up to 2000 gs. for a young three-
quarter brother'to Trenton, he knocked off. S. H. Gollan
capped him with 25gs. and got the “bargain.” It never
won a race.
m * * *
Manifold’s dangerous Camperdown stable won
the Cup at Onkaparinga, S.A., with Veronica, who beat
Preston ; also the Steeple with the hard-pulling Dungan,
and the Hurdles with Geometry. This stable will have a
hot “ corner ”in jumpers this winter. The speedy Pivot
is said to be a promising G.N. candidate. It was on a
trial with him that Veronica went over to beat Preston.
* * * *
Hordern’s stable scored three seconds at Rand-
wick, up to The Skipper’s easy win in the City Stakes.
On past form he was fairly thrown in, and backers seemed
to know it. Ancient Robinson Crusoe has got some good
’uns in his day, and The Skipper is by no means the worst
of the crowd.
* » * *
Music, last in a field of 16 in City Stakes, with
7.12, tells its own tale. What a show he had in last Aus.
Cup, 24 miles, with 8.10 !
* * * *
Newhaven, an original 120 gs. purchase, cost
W. T. Jones and W. Cooper 2000 gs. before the Champagne
Stakes. His new owners are said to have backed him
so heavily that the Easter Stakes win will only just about
compensate.
* * » *
Thirteen of the Hobartville yearlings auctioned
last week were geldings ; no colts. Kirkham sent up nine
geldings, but none of the Tocal lot were unsexed.
» » * »
An idea seems to exist that had Trentham been
vigorously ridden out, instead of letting his stable mate
up, he would have won the Cup. He certainly “ came
again ” when Toreador rushed up, and secured third place.
Like many others, he had his admirers, whose remarks
over the business weren’t very angelic. In France the
pari-mutuel pays out on losers if stable companions win.
* » * *
Poor jockey Mulry, buried last week, was a
native of Ireland, and a trusted rider, whose fate appeared
to be connected with Rosehill. A year ago he sustained
concussion of the brain. Kelso was averse to him riding
Thor in Rosehill Steeple, but Mulry was confident. The
accident occurred through a common cause —Thor didn’t
see the jump owing to horses being immediately in front
of him.
• * * *
While the excited crowd was yelling itself hoarse
over the popular idol after Sydney Cup, a badly-struck
bookie quietly disappeared in the confusion, leaving
behind his capacious bag, chalked “ Gone, but not
forgotten.”
* * * *
Scratching Wallace for the All-Aged mile was
wise. Taking the time as a criterion, he would have had
to break the record to beat Tir6, who shot out like a ball
at a target ; the chestnut’s slow, ground-gaining plod
would have been of no avail. He was beaten in this event
last year.
* « * *
In the case of a sluggish horse like Wallace, the
advantage of being paced by a stable companion is very
obvious. Mr. Wilson’s second string won the Sydney
Cup for his champion, probably.
* * * *
For the first time in the present writer’s recol-
lection “ books ” laid longer odds than the Tote. Dungan,
3 and 4 to 1 Onkaparinga Steeple ; Tote odds, 5 to 2.
* * * *
The hooting at Newhaven last Thursday was
described by the Argus reporter as an “unseemly demon-
stration.” The uproar of depositors at a busted-bank
meeting is also unseemly. No display of indignation
ought to be permitted in Australia.
* * * *
Wallace at the two big Autumn meetings was
Carbine over again—a bit short of condition at Fleming-
ton and cherry-ripe when he got to Randwick. Both
horses ran second for the Australian Cup.
* * * «
Demise, in Adelaide : Filgate, the worthy, if
rather fossiliferous, Melb. turfite, an astounding mine of
horsey yarns. He carries to the grave, according to his
own account, the true story of Newminster’s nobbling,
when trainer Dakin slept in a nightcap of steel and
dagger-proof blankets.
Filgate once entered a horse for a race at Rand-
wick, but the A.J.C. altered the date. Filgate refused to
run his horse, would not pay forfeit, and was “ posted.”
He sued in the Supreme Court, but was beaten on a
technical point. His argument was that the club had no
right to alter racing fixtures while horses were in training,
and, still refusing to pay up, he was placed “ beyond the
oale.” He had declared, however, that, whichever way
the suit went, he would never run a horse again in N.S.
Wales, and many Vic. sportsmen of the day followed his
example.
* * •
“ On ever-deceiving l form, Trentham, with only
1.4, looks like annexing Adelaide Cup next month, unless
Bradford is the stable choice.
* * * *
In five events at Randwick backers had to lay
odds on the favorite. The tote never asks for odds on. It
takes your money and pays a div.
* » * *
The Harvester, not thoroughly, wound up when
ne left Melb., was practically raced into form at Rand-
wick. He is a confirmed stayer, and won, as a three-year-
old, V.R.C. Champion in 31jsec. better time than the
Plate was run on Saturday.
* ♦ * *
The Steeplechase and Plate, both three miles,
were won in the same time, 6.17. Think of it! Had old
Mikado 11. or a first-class mule started in the Plate, either
.night have got a place.
* * * *
Estimated that the ring lost <£Bo,ooo over the
first two Randwick days. Only the Income-tax people
oould believe this. Where would Ikey get eighty thou. ?
Bad times, cronk practices, and the ridiculous odds
offered have brought heavy betting down to bedrock,
live-pound wagerers of years ago now talk in a half-
crown strain.
» * * *
Such a rising crowd as Coil, Tir6, Newhaven,
Resolute, Cydnus, The Officer, Gozoczar, and possibly
Huret, threaten to turn next season’s classic events and
handicaps into prize-riddle competitions. And there’s a
chance, too, of another Harvester being foisted on an un-
suspecting public at the last moment, or a second Water-
fall creeping into prominence when the crowd isn’t
looking.
* * * *
Wilson’s galloping goldmine didn’t have a
Trentham to bust the field for him in A.J.C. Plate, and
was beaten fairly and squarely, not only by The Har-
vester, but by Fort, at even weights. The “ race” was a
monotonously slow affair. When the field did wake up,
Wallace wasn’t fast enough. That’s his trouble. Gough
should have come away long before the finish, and made
his own pace. The slow time, 6.17, shows that this could
have been done with advantage. When The Harvester
put steam on the rest looked like a lot of crocks. Care-
fully nursed throughout, he put in one dash that ad-
mitted of no serious opposition.
The only excuse advanced for Wallace s defeat
is that his previous races had a non-beneficial effect on
his highly-strung frame. He presented a tired, world-
weary appearance before the start, and had an unpleasant-
looking bruised heel, but his connections thought it was
only throwing away a stake not to start him.
* * * •
The slow, exercise canter at which the field went
in the Plate caused people to wonder why the club hadn’t
put a trot on the programmes.
* * * *
The only way to ensure a good, sound race over
a long course is to adopt the time limit. This is the
V.R.C.’s rule in connection with the Champion. The
Harvester was half a second over the time when he won
his Champion, and only got half the stake.
* * * *
The pile distributed on Harvester before the
three-mile start foreboded a big surprise. Sheet Anchor’s
son is at the top of the tree, and apparently equal to
downing Wilson’s pet again if thev meet in the spring.
* * *
Randwick Second Steeple was a blundering,
wearisome spectacle. The top-weight—Mikado 11., a real
good ’un —won his first race since up from a spell: ancient
Bushman, who has had a lengthy and varied career, fol-
lowing home. The other seven either turned tail or
turned turtle. A couple of beauties who fell seemed to
“chance” their jumps. When a horse does that, it
generally means a job for the undertaker.
* * * *
Harold Bickford, winner of the Druids’ Wheel
Race (£200), at Meib., is perhaps the most likely of all the
present Australian riders to develop into a first-flight
man. There are about half-a-dozen Australasian men
who take it in turns to beat each other. Among horse-
racing men this is significant. “ Too near each other for
any one of them to be a wonder,” say the equine wise-
acres, and this applies with equal force to cyclists. One
Melb. cycling journal (nicknamed Parsons’s Weekly)
styles that modest young man the champion. Now, the
champion has not won one Australasian championship
this season, and has gone down before Megson pretty con-
sistently.
* * * «
Kux, the German rider who lowered the
Adelaide-to-Melb. record by 6 hours, had the assistance of
all the German-Australian wheelists available to pace him
on his journey. Bergstrom started him at Adelaide,
Seiper picked him up at Kingston, and, after several youths
ending in “ berg ” and “ heimer ” had taken him as far as
Williamstown, Miss Lucy Beyer led him the last, ten miles
to Melb. Town Hall. Miss Beyer is the 20-year-old
daughter of the late Peter Beyer, the Teutonic gymnastic
teacher of the majority of Melb. schools 10 years ago, and
has been reared in an atmosphere of dumb-bells and
ponderous clubs. She is now eleven stone of shapely
muscle capable of turning off a mile in 2.30, or sending
the average man “out” for the required lOsecs. in one
hit; nevertheless, she is a remarkably pretty girl, and one
of the few women who look well on a machine.
Kux possesses abnormal lung capacity, his dif-
ference in chest circumference between inspiration and
expiration being 4*4in. He pedalled the first 40 hours
without sleep. Then he slept for four hours, and travelled
for another 24 before taking an hour’s nap. The whole
distance was done at an average speed of 9 J miles an hour.
* * * *
By the way, how is it that so many foreign
names appear among racing cyclists and so few Irish ?
The account of a Melb. football-match reads like the
charge-sheet of a Dublin riot, yet last Austral’s programme
gave only Huurigan and Hogan to defend the honor of
ould Oireland.
* * * »
At latest dates the owner of Mystery was pre-
pared to back his trotting mare for another go against
cyclist Harris, on the condition that they start in oppo-
site directions, and travel strictly on their own to the
winning-post. The quadruped’s legs versus the biped’s
ditto, and devil take all pace-making. A fair proposal.
• * • •
“ Hee ” Donahoo, sole survivor of the Melb.
yachting accident, is a well-known Vic. cricketer and
footballer, being a brother of the brilliant interprovincial
bat of that ilk, and a nephew of “Jack” Conway, who
engineered the first Australian Eleven. He is a big,
strapping young fellow, but not much of a swim-
mer ; and how he kept afloat for 15min. after getting a
nasty bruise on his back, and with his trousers hanging
about his feet, is a mystery to those who know his
abilities in the water. “ Alf.” Terry, the unfortunate
owner of the yacht, who was one of the three drowned,
was a son of Terry, of Terry’s brewery, and though but
23 years old, was himself brewer at Lathams.
« » * «
If Parsons has got “a little bundle” he left it at
home. on the occasion of Porta’s recent ben. at Melbourne.
He won his heat of the 2-Mile Handicap in the fastest
time of the day, and just lost the final of the same race
through M'Lean falling in front of him about 30yds. from
home. He beat Porta in the 4-Mile and 2-Mile match and
lost in the Mile. He won his heat of the J Mile Invitation
and was just beaten by Harris in the final for the same
race.
* * * •
English rowing authorities have upheld N.S.W.
in its squabble with Vic. about Dawson. Which doesn’t
alter the position one jot. The practice of sending ’Ome
for decisions on athletic disputes of all kinds is ridiculous.
It flatters the English know-alls, and manifests gross
ignorance of the principles of the pastimes in which
“ colonials” are supposed to excel.
* * * *
J. L. Sullivan, apropos of a second talking-
match between Corbett and Fitzsimmons : “ Yes, Fitz.
can beat Corbett. It would be a long fight, but the Aus-
tralian would win. Corbett should have been a preacher.
He was a good man once, but isn’t now.”
♦ * » *
Sydneyites who remember Bob Fitzsimmons
faking at Foley’s old hall, can imagine the red-headed one
slouching round Maher and hitting all out of distance
until he got his man in position, and then like a flash out
came his right on the “ spot.” Fitz. said there were only
two blows struck during the fight. When his right
reached Maher’s jaw, and when the back of Maher’s head
reached the stage.
‘ ‘ Men feel with their brains, and women
think with their hearts.”
The last batch of Q. elections fixes the
composition of the new Assembly as 41 Ministeria-
lists, 20 Labor, 6 Opposition, and 5 Independent.
Jack Hamilton’s seat for Oook is still in doubt.
“Steel-rail” Phillips, a Govt, toady, has been
deservedly ousted by a Labor man for Carpen-
taria ; and storekeeper Newell replaces Labor
Rawlings for Woothakata. Excluding Speaker
Cowley, the Govt, party musters 40 against 31
possible opponents. All the Independents, how-
ever, are not reliable Oppositionists ; and means
could doubtless be found to make some of them
“ vote right ” or stay away on a critical division.
The net result of the election is a nominal increase
of two in the total possible Opposition vote, and
a gain of three seats by the Labor-party. Prac-
tically, if the Govt, party can hold together, con-
ditions are almost “ as they were.”
Gunnedah Land Board has finished one
section of the “ Mercadool Land Case,” an
apparently interminable suit dragged out to deter-
mine whether the selections on Mercadool run, in
Walgett (N.S.W.) district, were genuine selections
taken up by people who really yearned to “go on
the land,” or were merely dummied in the good
old fashion for the benefit of the station-owners
or mortgagees. After 47 days’ struggle,
with the help of expensive counsel, and of
many station - managers, boss cookies, and
other witnesses, the Board has decided that,
so far as the selections of one James
Wilson are concerned, James was “ a mere
counter, a mere dummy,” at a salary of JR per
week, with a bonus of <£lso when the selections
were duly transferred. There are only 14 other
cases to be considered, in which similar questions
are raised, and, when the Board has wrestled with
them all, the Lands Minister thinks that “no
doubt there will be an appeal ” to the Supreme
Court—and thence to the Privy Council. The
“ Mercadool case ” bids fair to extend far into the
20th century.
Out of nine new magistrates gazetted in
the Barwon (N.S.W.) electorate, three are dead
(one nearly a year), and one left in December to
reside permanently in England.
The vacancy caused by the death of H. F.
Manning. Clerk of Tas. Assembly, is being eagerly
sought after by every local M.P.’s distant relative.
The appointment being practically in the gift of
the Speaker, quite an army of billet-hunters are
trying to deposit salt on the tail of Mr. btanora
Bird. Rumored that the latter desires the billet
himself.


REMINGTON.
1874. 1895.
In This my Twenty-first Year I Have Ascertained Just
to What Extent the Pioneer of All Type Writers Out
Numbers the Combined Forces of Competition in New
York City—the Home and Headquarters of Type Writers.
For the Past 16 Years Attempt After Attempt Has Been
Made to Displace Me in the Estimation of the Public,
Each Succeeding- Year Producing More Than Its Pre-
decessors of Would Be Rivals. Many Have Fallen by
the Wayside. Of the Survivors No Less Than 26 Different
Makes Are To-day Represented in the New York Market.
A Complete Census of Writing Machines in Use in New
York City is Impracticable, But in View of the Prevailing
Tendency to Concentration of Business in the Large Office
Buildings, I. Now Account for the Type Writers in Use in
33 of Those Buildings.

VALIDS
The English Prison Board has decided to
abolish the treadmill, crank, and oakum-picking.
Darling Downs Gazette (Q.) on reform
and the need for a State Drink Department:—
Firstly, reform in whisky. Queensland’s whisky, as
provided from the Tweed to Cape York, is in greater need
of reformation than anything I know, unless it be its poli-
ticians. Made principally of old boots, kerosene, tobacco,
and white spirit, it is universally drunk—by the universal
drunk. It has filled our asylums, our hospitals, our gaols
with wrecks. Let the whole liquor-trade he under Govern-
ment control. Let us have Government pubs. The
advantage would be astonishing. Our politicians, who
live on whisky, would he pervaded with a purer spirit.
The democratic suburb of Collingwood
(Melb.) supports 30 bakers—29 of them non-
unionists. The difference between union and
non-union wages represents something over |d. a
loaf, so the sweater can sell his bread Id. cheaper
and still make a larger profit. Therefore, the
local working man whoops for union principles
and alleges, if any question arises, that his wife
bought from the non-union sweater without his
knowledge. It is now proposed to put a large,
glaring brand on all bread made in union shops,
so that the house-father can’t pretend not to know
what kind of bread he is eating.
The Transvaal cloud again lowers. Ap-
parently Chamberlain has taken advantage of his
kudos to press the Outlanders’ claims further than
Kruger is prepared to yield. There is absolutely
no reason, from the Boer point of view, why
Kruger should yield one inch. Twice the Boers
have been driven by the English from territory to
which they had at least as much right as anybody
else : it is time they turned at bay. Their methods
of life may be barbarous, their homes may be
dirty ; but, such as they are, there is no reason
why they should be sacrificed to the greed of
British or Jew syndicates.
In Maoriland the Tory Opposition has the
solid beer and whisky vote of the country, and it
is now imploring the Cold Tea party to join it.
“It might reasonably be expected,” says the
Otago Daily Times, “that a Temperance party
worthy of the name, instead of isolating itself
with a single idea, would make common cause
with the Opposition in order to bring about a
purer state of matters.” Meanwhile, the Cold
Tea crowd is helping the Tory faction quite
enough. It managed to split the Liberal vote at
Christchurch so accurately that over 8000 Liberals
are disfranchised, and the Beer and Big Loan
candidate got in as the representative of only 5000
voters.
The German socialistic newspaper, Yor-
waekts, is said to have been established on a
sound financial basis by the very Government
authorities who wanted a chance to suppress it.
Vokwaerts was bringing itself out with much
difficulty, and the death mark seemed to be coming
on its young brow when Emperor William gave
the word that it was a dangerous publication
which all loyal officials must follow with a watch-
ful eye. So thousands of officials suddenly bought
V orwaerts in order to keep an eye on it, and the
circulation of the beastly disloyal rag having gone
up like a rocket, remained up. Radical journalism
all the world over owes a good deal to its fervent
enemies.

Mr. Button writes later: —“The Pills I had
from you were of great benefit to me and others.
I n one case, when three hunters and trappers
were travelling about the ranges near my place on

A Wonderful Cure.
Herr Rassmussen. August, 1894.
Dear Sir, —In the year of 1871 I was eight days
in the water sheep-washing, and contracted rheu-
matism and stiffness in the joints, and have tried
all advertised medicines. When I came to Sydney
I had a great difficulty in walking, and went to
the Turkish baths, which did no good. Passing
your window I saw a testimonial from Mr. E.
W. O’Sullivan, M.P., with the same complaint as
I had, which led me to try your Remedies. On
Thursday before Good Eriday I took the first of
your Pills ; since then I have used 4 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Vitality Pills, 3 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Rheumatic Pills, with Liver
and Kidney Pills, and free use of your Alfaline
Eucalyptus Oil, and I am now, with the blessing
of God, as well in health as I was 23 years ago.
I can with great confidence recommend your
Remedies to anyone suffering from the same com-
plaint. May you long be spared under God to
be a blessing to suffering humanity.
I remain, dear sir, yours very gratefully,
JOHN KEAY.
Witness : Edward Hardy, proprietor Town
Hall Coffee Palace, 521 George-street.

Victoria has just discovered that the
naming act passed by the Gillies-Deakin Govern-
ment tied things so artistically in a hard knot that
coal-measures in private lands sold by the State
prior to tL can’t be worked either by the State or
tne private owner or anybody else until the law is
amended. The State didn’t either retain the coal
eposits itself or part with them to the purchaser
oi the land, so they don’t definitely belong to
anybody. The Parliament which built this act
consisted largely of lawyers. .

Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
ot the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block m Gollins-street, Melb., a distance of 060 ft.,
f'.uu contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Hoods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; 9,

17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long.*
South Melb. Council has decided that the
local Joss house (the Chow Cathedral) does not
come under the definition of “a building used solely
for religious purposes,” and is not exempt from
rates. Consequently John Chow is requested to
dump up <£2o arrears. John is going to fight it,
and should win if there is either horse-sense or
equity in Vic. law. The Joss house is quite as
sacred an edifice as the average allegedly Christian
establishment, and is, in reality, a house of god,
for Joss is there in person, rivalling Solomon in all
his glory; whilst in the church of the pious
Briton the reigning Deity is too often only repre-
sented by the few loose threepennies in the pants-
pocket of the Rev. Deuteronomy Snufflebust.

Dear Bulletin. —The N.S.W. Bill to give
relief to selectors doesn’t go far enough. What is
needed is a Benevolent Asylum for selectors only,
where the men who have spent all their capital
and the best part of their lives in improving
Crown lands, could find a quiet retreat where land-
boards cease from troubling, and the mortgage
takes a rest. The inmates could employ the time
between meals in drafting a Land Act that would
allow bona fide selectors to earn almost as comfor-
table a living as the squatters’ perjured “ dum-
mies.” No doubt in such an asylum “Steele
Rudd ” would be able to finally dispose of old
“Dad,” and that fabulous example of his class
could amuse the rest of the inmates with practical
illustrations of how he ploughed his 40 acres with
a broken shear-blade, and harrowed it per dog
harnessed to a currycomb, and how, when the
‘ ‘ crop ” was promising 90 bushels to the acre, a
neighbour’s cow sat down on it so hard that it
never rose again, and the storekeeper seized his
farming implements for his grocery-bill. Of
course, a necessary addition to the asylum would
be a House of Detention where any young men who
developed symptoms of settling on the land could
be kept under observation. To effectually restore
these unfortunates to reason old “ Dad ” should
be engaged to visit them daily with a two hours’
lecture on agriculture as it is practised on “Our
Selection.” If any failed to recover their reason
under this treatment, they should be removed to
Callan Park without further waste of time.
There is no other way.- — Cripps Clark.

Railway-carriage doors with noiseless,
automatic opening and closing action are to be
placed on some of the Melb. suburban lines this
month. In connection with this invention there
is an ingenious device by which the name of the
approaching station is shown in each carriage.
The inventor is a Melb. bank clerk, named Fraser,
who has already been offered a handsome sum for
his invention by a big English railway co.

An inevitable result of the Lormer Board
report was the interviewing of sundry Vic. poli-
ticians by an obsequious emissary from Melb.
’Erald. Each M.L.A. in turn was begged to
state his views on the situation, and each of these
oracles replied, with the air of one who is reveal-
ing an unexpected truth, that magistrates Rapi-
port, Baxter, and Bird deserved to be wiped off
the bench. There is nothing sadder in modern
journalism than the custom of utilising passing
events for the glorification of dull platitudinarians.
If typhoid fever is more prevalent than usual in
Smellbonrne, a reporter waits upon members of
Parliament for information as to what ought to
be done, and when these famous persons have
duly stated that the drainage system needs im-
proving, the stale chestnut is hurled at the public
by their evenin’ journal as though it were a
thunderbolt from Heaven.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
23-of this issue.
“I expect so much with my wife that I
cannot expect money. ”
J. Hotsou and Co.'s attempt to get further
Government assistance for their Melb. meat-
freezing business almost deserved to succeed for
the sake of its splendid audacity. Mr. Hotson,
who seems to have started his Newport works with
more enterprise than capital, got a 14 years’ con-
ditional lease from the Railway Dept. The busi-
ness flourished, nevertheless the conditions of the
lease re the amount to be expended by the tenants
on buildings and machinery were not fulfilled.
After a while, Hotson obtained, at one fell swoop,
50 per cent, extension of his original lease term
and a large reduction in the amount he had con-
tracted to put into buildings and plant. Another
year or two rolled on —the business flourishing
more than ever—and then Hotson came along
with a proposal that Government should fix
up new premises, leaving him to supply
machinery only. At this stage of the case a little
growling from opposition meat-freezers was heard
in the distance. The Western and WimmeraCo.,
which has put about «£20,000 (including the cost
of a new jetty) into its business on the north shore
of Corio Bay, began to talk of taking action to
secure Government concessions, either in the form
of ‘ ‘ facilities ” such as Hotson enjoyed, or a money
equivalent. Whereupon Hotson dropped his
latest proposal, and suggested an alternative to
the effect that “ his firm should make their own
improvements at the works, provided that the Go-
vernment deducted from the rental annually a
sum which, if capitalised for 19 years, would be
equal to the present necessary expenditure on the
works.” Premier Turner, warned by those growls
in the distance, turned a deaf ear to the voice of
the charmer, and for the present Hotson is bound
down to his lease, and meat-freezing in Melbourne
and elsewhere will have to be conducted on private
commercial principles.

Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “Allen and
Ginter’s Straight-cut No. 1,” “Cameo” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “Pure Gold ” and “ Dixon’s No. 1,” have given us official notice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
Ct TZHUR-ZEIE CASTLES” and C-AIPS TLATsT. 75
We have declined to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the nterest of
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co.. Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Wills’ “Three Castles’’ and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
“ Three Castles” and “Capstan.” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.

Aboriginal Names.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Prof. Morris, of Melbourne, has started to ex-
plain the origin of some of our aboriginal words
and, as a matter of course, will drift into the usual
grotesque blunders incidental to information col-
lected promiscuously from miscellaneous sources
probably few or none of which are acknowledged’.
If his explanation of many other words is similar
to that of “wallaby,” “ wallaroo. ” and “ yabba ”
as lately recorded in The Bulletin, his dictionary
will need to be followed at once by a new and re
vised edition.
My object here is to fix the authentic origin of a
few well-known Australian words, and place them
beyond all further dispute. The first blacks with
whom the whites associated were those of Sydney
and Botany Bay, followed by those of the Hawkes-
bury, Hunter, and Twofold Bay. To the dialects
of those places we naturally turn for the earliest
aboriginal words in use among the white men.
_ The most interesting vocabulary in my posses-
sion is one taken down from the lips of Bennilong,
one of the two Sydney blacks who went to Eng-
land with Gov. Phillip in 1792. Attached to this
is another taken from a George’s River black named
Walwarra, in 1803, by Thomas Jarret Ives, who
lived with the blacks and spoke their language.
Both vocabularies were printed at the Sydney
Gazette office in 1809. In the Botany dialect I
find the first record of the word “myall ” the
name given to a stranger. This word will ’be re-
ferred to hereafter. The Botany dialect gave the
word ‘kobbera” for head. At Sydney it was
cabboora. It gave us “ mundowie,” the word
for leg, though widely used for the foot by the
whites. From Botany came the words wombat,
wommera, yarraman, wonga-wonga, gunya, waddy,
wollaba (wallaby), wallaroo, heelamin, budjerie’
corobberie, baal, and curriejung. Yarraman was
yeeraniahn,” from yeera, the teeth, and mahn,
long -literally “ long teeth,” a name for the horse.
The negative was “ beeal ” (baal), and the affir-
mative yobeen. ’ From Botany came the once-
familiar words, “ paialla ” to tell, “ durellie ” to
fight, “ coolahra ” angry, “ jerran ” frightened,
and “ goori ” for fat. Curriejung was the Hibiscus
Heterophylla, and the name was also used for
fishing-lines made from the bark of that plant.
From Botany comes the word boomerang. Wal-
warra called it “ booniering ” and “bummering.”
The Botany tribe called the native bear “ coolah,”
the same as one of the bear-names at Morton Bay.
All these words were scattered by the pioneer
timber-getters, settlers, or squatters, from Sydney
to the Gulf of Carpentaria. The “ wallaby ” of
Botany was the paddymella of the scrubs. The
grey kangaroo was “ bobroo,” the red one “ gob-
reea.” In the Sydney dialect “ coojee ” was an
oyster, and “ Bondi ” the heavy nulla. The word
“ nulla-nulla,” the light club, came from Twofold
Bay. “Euroka,” the sun, belonged to Botany
and Sydney.
Leaving Sydney, we pass over the “Wodi”
dialect of Shoalhaven and Illawarra, and arrive at
the “Awaba” dialect of Newcastle and Port
Macquarie. A vocabulary of this dialect was
obtained by Threlkeld, the missionary who went
there in 1826, and who has left us the most com-
plete and valuable work yet written on any Aus-
tralian dialect. In “ Awaba ” the boomerang has
become / turrama,” and the “ merree ” for dog at
?tm a cllled ‘ y °° k . i , ” \ the slut - however, bfing
“ warrt*l ” T- 66 ’ • A tame dog and slut are
is still unchanged. Tht “ W ° rd “ Wommara ”
Crossmg t he range we enter the two great and
widespread dialects of Kamilroi and “ Wired
tedF:° noU^ d Kahm ’lry and Wiradderie, or
“ ' h a ' ie - Here we have the generic word
comes 1- tl i nalr T ) ,M r al , l blacks - From the Kamil
( mes the word kookaburra” for jackass, and
buralga (boorallga) for the native companion.
TheW°r f ßa /w IS ord becomes “ burallgum.”
hst of Wiradhuri words was collected by
rchdeacon Gunther at Wellington Vale in 1836
in Kamil the boomerang has become “ barman. ”
11ns is also the name at Morton Bay.
oil 16 w ° r d <l jumbuck ” for sheep appears origin-
ally as jimba, joinbock, dombock, and dumbog.
n each case it meant the white mist preceding
a shower, to which a flock of sheep bore I
strong resemblance. It seemed the only thing
the aboriginal imagination could compare it to!
From the “ Kamil ” and “ Wirri ” (the negatives)
dialects comes coolabah ” or “ coolibar, ” for the
box-gums.
From Wirri comes “billa,” a river or creek.
Attached to the Morton Bay word “bong” for
dead, it became billabong, a dry or dead water-
course.
There, too, is the word “dilly,” fine wood dust
hence dillybag, the small bag in which the gins
carried the sticks for making fire, and fine stuff
for igniting.
Here we first find the word “gidya,” the name
ot one of the acacias. Also the word ‘ ‘ gibba, ” the
vhite quartz crystal used at the bora cemetery.
Hence gibbar, or kippar, the young man initiated :
also gibbir, used recklessly by the whites for
any kind of stone.
The wommara ’ has now become “ womarr.”
Here a stranger is still mayol, or myall, and all
strange blacks were described as wild blacks by
any tribe. Hence myall, a wild black.
In Wirri the dingo is warrigal. The word dingo,
pronounced “ deeng-go,” appears in Bennilong’s
h s f as the dog’s name in the Sydney tribe.
Mopoke ” (a nightjar) is the “mobock” of the
Sydney blacks.
Moorabinda” will learn that her pen-name in
the Wirri dialect means “sound asleep,” not an
appropriate cognomen for a fair writer who is ex-
tremely wide awake. But consolation may be
found in the fact that in a northern dialect “ Moo-
rabinda ” means “stately emu.”
In the “ Peecumbill ” dialect of the Weir and
Macintyre, meeal, or myall, is the generic term for
a man. In no tribe is it the name of the acacia
pendula. That error arose as follows. The coast
blacks traded with the inland stranger or myall
blacks for myall or brigalow spears. They called
the spear myall, or myall spear, because they got
it from myall or strange blacks. A brigalow
spear, especially black brigalow, smells exactly
like myall, and was called myall by the blacks for
the same reason. The coast blackboys, with
pioneer squatters, when they reached myall
country, recognised the wood and called it myall,
after the spear name. Myall was first seen by Sir
Thomas Mitchell on the Peel River in 1836.
Finally this acacia became known as myall to
blacks and whites from the Peel River to the
Peak Downs. At Morton Bay the brigalow
acacia was “Bonnoroo.”
When the bushman refers to his “ hard yakker ”
he is using the word “ yacca” (work of any kind)
from the old Turrabool dialect of the Brisbane
blacks. The word first appears in 1838, iu the
convict period of Morton Bay. The word
jackeroo, a station new-chum, comes also from
the old Brisbane blacks, who called the pied crow
shrike ( Stripera graculina) ‘ ‘ tehaceroo, ” a gabbling 1
and garrulous bird. They called the German
missionaries of 1838 “jackeroo,” a gabbler, be-
cause they were always talking. Afterwards they
applied it to all white men. The Russell River
blacks call the same bird “ jowa-jowa,” from jowa
the mouth, literally “all mouth.”
The origin of the word kangaroo was published
ivT ln T ? S j X ycars Captain Cook got it from
the Endeavor River blacks, who pronounce it
to-day exactly as it is spelled in the great navi-
gator s journal, but they use it now only for the
big toe.
Either the blacks in Cook’s time called the
kangaroo “big toe” for a nick-name, as the
American Indians speak of the “big horn,” or the
man who asked the name of the animal was hold-
ing it by the hind foot, and got the name of the
long toe, the black believing that was the part to
which the question referred.
On the east coast of Australia a widely-distri-
buted word for an old man kangaroo is “ goora-
min. ’ It extended from Dubbo, in N.S. Wales,
to Rockhampton.
All the native tribes of Australia spoke varia-
tions of only one language. A river or range of
mountains often separated two distinct dialects.
In 1861, when Walker went out with two white
men and six blacks to look for Burke and Wills,
he met one tribe on the Gulf of Carpentaria
speaking a dialect unintelligible to all his black
troopers except one from Deniliquin, in N.S.
Wales. This black spoke “Kamilroy,” a dialect
which Ridley, in 1855, found intelligible over a
distance of 800 miles.
Another extensive dialect was the “Yoocum-
Yoocum ” of the Clarence, Richmond and Tweed
Rivers, and part of New England.
One remarkable fact in conclusion. The Botany
Bay blacks called rain “banna,” and a little girl
rnyee (the name given by Lady Carington to
her Sydney-born baby). These words I found
again 1500 miles north of Botany among the
Russell River blacks, with the same meaning, and
occurring nowhere in the intermediate dialects.
In Botany Bay there is a river called “ Woronora,”
originally “ Wooroono6ra.”
In the Russell dialect the centre peak of Bellen-
den-Ker is “ Wooroonodr&n.”
(( There is a curious coincidence in the fact that
banna, ’ the Botany blacks’ name for rain, was
one of the Irish words for water, and that the
Irish word “boreen,” a pathway, was identical
with the Morton Bay “boreen ” for the small
pathway from the large to the small bora circles.
If we take isolated words, we might connect the
Australian dialects with half the languages of the
human race.
At Rockhampton, fire is “wee,” and Stanley
fot the same word for fire in Central Africa. In
forth Queensland I found the word “ alba ” used
for white, the same as the Latin.
In The Bulletin of Jan. 20, one of your bards
has a poem headed “ Tareel-Dool. ” In the Kamil-
roy dialect it means a place of “brown reeds”—
from “ tareel ” reeds, and “dool” brown. It was
in old times, and may be still, the name of a reed-
encircled lagoon on the Namoi. In Ridley’s vo-
cabulary of 1851 it is spelled “Tarildul,” pre-
sumably the “ Drilldool ” of the modern ortho-
graphical Goth who turned Yarr into “Yass,”
Walgeer into “ Walgett,” Narrungdeerie into
“ Narandera,” and committed a few other similar
outrages.
Brisbane.
A. MESTON.
One Shepparton, Vic., orchardist this
season sent out 6000 cases of apricots, besides other
fruit. m
The heaviest sheep yet killed in Maoriland
was on show in Christchurch the other day—
1901b. dressed.
The deepest spot of all the oceans was
probably found by the Penguin in her recent
surveying cruise, south of the Friendly Islands.
Ihe wire snapped at a depth of 29,400 feet—bed of
the ocean not reached. This is nearly 1500 ft.
deeper than the greatest depth hitherto attained
in the North Pacific.
At Port Macquarie, N.S.W., parrots of a
kind apparently new to the district have been rava-
ging the grape-vines, and will not be driven away,
although they are shot in hundreds. Several
vineyards have been abandoned by their owners
to these pests.
A terrible warning to amateur fire-brigades
is cabled from Lille (France). Six firemen went
prowling around for a drink at the local hospital
after extinguishing a conflagration there, and
found a bottle of clear spirit which they took to
be gin. The stuff happened to be poison, and the
half-dozen thirsty heroes died immediately.
“ It may be taken for granted that every
intelligent person has read ‘Trilby,’” cackles a
Melbourne press goose. What a strange concep-
tion of human intelligence is expressed in such a
remark! Every eager devourer of fashionable
modern novels and novelettes has read Du Mau-
rier’s yarn.
Stead’s latest ad captandum dodge is a
“ Penny Hymnal,” as a preliminary ad. for which
he has been inquiring of all sorts of prominent
persons what hymn has “ helped ” each most.
“Tummy’s ” vote was for “ Lead, kindly Light ! ”
and strong indeed must be the spiritual rapport
between that podgy little sybarite and the ethere-
ally ascetic Newman. Herbert Spencer is the
only man of the lot who says, in effect, “ D—n
hymns ! —I hate ’em ! ” Who can imagine a
Spencer being “helped” by a hymn !
On his Horn Central Australian trip Prof.
Baldwin Spencer, of Melb. University, got two
new species of marsupial “mice’’with specially
fat tails, and conjectures that during droughts
they live on their fat, as camels and zebras do on
their hump-fat. Also, he met burrowing marsu-
pial “ rats,’ - blind like moles, and living on ants
and ant-eggs. Likewise, caught the “ parenthic ”
(varantus (jitjantcus), an Bft. land-lizard ; frogs
which, just before a drought, balloon themselves
out with water, go to sleep in the mud, and only
wake up when it rains ; spiders which build 15ft.
wide webs ; and spiders which whistle.
Melbourne Harbor Trust will go broke
shortly, unless Providence or the Government
intervenes. Its average expenditure is £25,000 a
year more than its income, and the borrowed
money on which it has been living is nearly ex-
hausted. Thomas Loader, of the City of Mel-
bourne Bank, is one of the valuable old men who
has helped to run the Trust into its present awful
condition, and said condition is another proof of
the wisdom of J. Service’s contention that the
companies law shouldn’t be too strict, else Valu-
able men like Loader will go out of the directorial
business. - Considering the C.O.M. Bank and the
Melbourne Harbor Trust, for both of which he
was partly responsible, the necessity of not driving
men like Loader out of the business is quite
apparent.


NOTICE.
BY virtue of authorities, powers and privileges set up
and established by a Charter of Rights and Con-
stitution of the Women’s Institute, Wellington, duly
attested, signed and sealed, vested and reposed in me
for the time being, the first and only President and
Chief Executive Officer thereof, and to enforce all
obedience thereto, and, in consequence of the re-
sult of desertion by the officers and members of the
said Women’s Institute against one or all of the
said authorities, powers and privileges so vested in
me, and against my. commands duly issued and made
known to said officers and members assembled on an oc-
casion of an attempt to open an ordinary meeting of the
said Institute at a place known as the Women’s Club,
Wellington, and at the hour of 8 p.m. on Monday, March
16, 1896, and continuing during that hour till on or about
9 p.m. the same time, occasion, and place. Now, I hereby
proclaim and declare that the Women’s Institute of Wel-
lington is and did become defunct and ceased to exist at
the hour, time, place, and occasion afore and herein men-
tioned.
As witness my hand this 18th day of March, 1896.
SARAH JONES.
Witness to signature—
E. F. Jones, a Justice of the Peace.
The J.P. here so obedient a witness to this amus-
ingly pompous jargon is the hubby of Sarah Jones
aforesaid.

Spicer’s Courtship.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Spicer was a selector; why he chose to be a selec-
tor rather than enjoy comparative ease and
affluence as a corporation day-laborer or a wharf-
hand or navvy is inexplicable. He had taken to
the wilderness, built his smart bark hut in the
centre of an apparently impenetrable forest, and
was now actively engaged eating his way out
again. Along-the bank of the trickling creek he
had cleared an acre or so where a few fruit trees
flourished, and a methodical little vegetable-
garden looked green and encouraging. Dick
Spicer was a methodical man; what he did he did
well, and he was always doing. Dick was small,
and he looked puny lifting his pigmy axe to those
mighty gums, and patiently hewing splinters out
of the eompact bush. Having little or nothing to
say to his scattered neighbors, he exchanged small
talk with his hens, and favored Griffin, the low-
comedy dog-of-all-work, with his opinion of things.
Mr. Spicer was a bachelor approaching 50, wiry,
leathery, deliberative, and very diffident in com-
pany. But, despite his apparent uneasiness when
chance threw him into the society of females, Dick
was looking about for a wife. The stillness of the
long evenings and the solitary Sundays implanted
a great yearning for the companionship of a good
wife in his lonely heart. In looking about, the
selector’s view was very limited. There was not
an unmarried woman of suitable years within a
radius of 12 miles. Of all the approachable
females, he admired Mrs. Clinton the most, and
his only hope lay in the fact that Clinton was in
feeble health, and reported to be sustaining life
precariously with one lung.
Clinton held a block about a mile up the creek,
and Spicer paid him occasional abrupt and un-
ceremonious visits there. Sometimes he would
lean against a door-jamb, with not more than his
head inside, and pass a few remarks relative to
nothing in particular, in an irresponsible sort of
way; but more frequently he just stood about
outside, and criticised the poultry in audible
soliloquy, or reflected aloud upon Clinton’s ridicu-
lous notions about dairy-work and vegetable-
growing. However, he always displayed a proper
neighborly concern in inquiring after Clinton’s
health before leaving.
“ Y’ain’t feelin’ no better, I s’pose ?” he would
ask, with an appearance of anxious interest that
quite touched the sick man. Clinton was always
feeling “pretty bad.” He said as much in his
dull, heavy manner, and Dick would go off to in-
dulge in contemplation, and consult his dog.
i Spicer did not wish Clinton to die, he did not
want to hurry him up ; he was a patient, dispas-
ionate man, and the possibility of his neigh-
bor’s early demise entered into his calculations
merely as a probable circumstance which, however
regrettable, could not reasonably be overlooked.
Clinton substantiated predictions, and obligingly
died within a reasonable time, and Dick rfide
solemnly in the funeral cortege, behind the drays,
on a lame cart-horse borrowed from Canty for the
occasion.
After the funeral he looked in upon the widow,
and, feeling inspired to say something conso-
latory and encouraging, expressed his belief
that she wouldn’t mourn much about Peter.
“’Tain’t worth while,” he said, Dick’s com-
mand of language was only sufficient to en-
able him to say. the thing he meant once in
a. dozen tries, and on this occasion he was con-
scious the moment he had spoken that the sen-
timent expressed was hardly appropriate to the
occasion. Before he could frame an apology the
disconsolate widow attacked him with a spear-
grass broom, and stormed him out of the house.
He walked home thoughtfully, afflicted with a
nettle-rash and a vague idea that perhaps he had
not made an altogether satisfactory beginning.
But Spicer was not cast down ; he had resolved
upon a plan of courtship, and the object of his
first manoeuvre was to break his intentions gently
to the widow. This he thought to accomplish by
hanging round the house a good deal. He would
haunt her selection in the cool of the evening, or,
in his more audacious moments, perch himself on
the chock-an’-log fence running by the side of the
house, and whistle an unmelodious and windy jig
which was intended to convey some idea of his
airy nonchalance and great peace of mind.
It was a long time before Dick progressed from
the fence to the wood-heap, and meanwhile the
widow had not seemed to pay any particular atten-
tion to his movements. He sometimes addressed
her with a portentous truth bearing upon the
dieting of laying hens, or the proper handling of
cows, or the medical treatment of ailing chickens ;
but usually satisfied himself with a significant
grin and a queer twist of the head that was his
idea of sheer playfulness and waggery. The
neighbors came to notice him over-looking the
selection or perched on the fence supervising the
weather and things generally, and predicted that
there would be “amarryin”’ up the creek pre-
sently. Presently ! Spicer did nothing hastily,
nothing to lead anybody to believe that he had not
all eternity to come and go on. He never con-
sidered the flight of time, and had made many
calculations that carried him on to the end of the
next century, without discovering any incon-
gruity.
He did arrive at the wood-heap eventually,
though. Mrs. Clinton’s boy John was too young
to wield an axe with any effect, and one afternoon
Dick lounged over to the logs, took up the axe,
and examined it with an air of abstraction. He
weighed it carefully in his hand, and satisfied his
curiosity by trying it on a log. When he had
chopped about half-a-ton of wood he appeared
satisfied that it was a pretty good axe. That
evening he chuckled all the way up the creek, and
all the time it took to prepare his tea, and towards
bedtime confided to Griffin, with more chuckles,
his opinion that it was “ ’bout’s good’s done.”
“She can’t go back o n that,” he said with
assurance.
But Spicer lingered at this stage for a long
time ; he cut all the wood the widow needed, and
did other little things about the selection, and
often sat on the fence, as usual, and gradually
grew to be quite at home there. The widow
accepted his services now as a matter of course,
and though she was often betrayed into expres-
sions of great impatience, Dick remained oblivious,
and worked out his courtship in his own ponderous
way.
His next step towards strengthening his position
was when he took it upon himself to put several
new palings on the roof of Mrs. Clinton’s house.
This was a decided advance, and when the buxom.
little woman thanked him, his odd screw of the
face and sidelong nod clearly conveyed the im-
pression that he was beginning to regard himself
as a “ perfect devil amongst the women.” There
was more chuckling that evening, and further con-
fidences for the dog. After this Spicer ceased
working seriously on his own selection, and slowly
extended his sphere at the widow’s. He did some
gardening, and repaired the fences and dictated
improvements, but it was not till eighteen months
after Clinton’s death that he made his great stroke.
It was on Sunday afternoon that Dick discovered
Mrs. Clinton in hot pursuit of the boy John
with one shoe in her hand and one on her toot.
John was in active rebellion, and yelling his con-
tempt for the maternal authority. Spicer rose to
the occasion. He secured boy John, took off ms
belt, and proceeded to strap the unfilial youth—to
give him a grave, judicious, and fatherly larruping
—under the eye of his mother. Then the selector
drew off to consider and weigh the important step
lie had taken, with the result that, half-an-hour
after, he hung his head in at the kitchen door, and
said abruptly :
“ Treaser, when’s it to be ? .
“ Meanin’ which ? ” asked the unconscious widow.
“ Meanin’marryin’.” .
The widow thought for a moment, and said, just
as if she were completing the sale of a few eggs .
“ This day month ’ll suit me.”
“ Done,” said Spicer. , ,
Then he felt called upon to make some kind ot a
demonstration, and edged up to Mrs. Clinton in a
fidgeting sort of way, and when near enough made
as if to kiss her, paused half-way in doubt, and
then didn’t.
“ The man’s a fool,” said the stout little widow
composedly. .
They were married though, under conditions ot
great secrecy, at the parson’s house in the town-
ship, with the blinds down. It was with great
difficulty Dick was convinced of the necessity of
witnesses.
EDWARD DYSON.
All kinds get married on mining-fields.
Not long ago a Coolgardie clergyman, who had
gone to an outlying district to tie-up a couple,
found the bride-elect at the wash-tub. Wiping the
suds off her hands and arms, she faced him and
announced that she was ready. The cleric wasn’t;
he said he would call in two hours. He called,
and, to his surprise, found her still at the tub.
Somebody whistled down the adjoining shaft for
“ Bill,” and the marriage took place. A week
later, a witness of the ceremony met the cleric and
told him in a casual way that “ Bill hides his wife
awful.”
A most important, though intricate, point
has been raised in Vic. criminal court, with
reference to a prisoner’s giving evidence against
another prisoner arraigned with him. The argu-
ment is that, since such evidence would be worth-
less against the other under the old system, and,
since the object of the new system is only to allow
a man to give evidence on his own behalf, and to
prevent certain incriminating questions being
asked him at all, it is against the policy of the law
to allow such evidence to affect in any way the
interests of a co-prisoner. It is not settled yet.
Westralian Telegraph Dept, expected to
have to pay anything between ,£15,000 and
,£20,000 for the mere carriage from coast to
destination of the material for the Dundas-Eucla
section of the new overland telegraph-line to
S.A. The section will contain 525 miles of line
passing mostly through absolutely waterless and'
timberless country. The material, which includes
iron poles for the whole distance, will have to be
carried by camels. The firm of Faiz and Tagh
Mahomet was prepared to put 500 camels and 200
men —mostly Afghans—on the work, and wanted
six months in which to complete it. The material
for the section would weigh less than 900 tons.

Mr. Joseph Bosisto writes re the statement
of a Bulletin correspondent that, “ to the shame
of Australians, the finest quality of eucalyptus oil
for chemical purposes in these provinces is being
imported from California and Algeria. The euca-
lyptus grown in California and Algeria, says Mr.
Bosisto, is of one species—viz., the blue gum (glo-
bulus)— and does not contain the chemical body
termed eucalyptol—the essential medicinal agent-
in such abundance as some other species, such as
oleosa, dumosa, cnerifolia, and incrassata. Aus-
tralia ’exports tons weight of these oils annually ;
and the ‘ ‘ shame ” is a chimera.
The 40th annual demonstration of Mel-
bourne’s “Eight Hours” League, which has
hitherto confined itself to the Friendly Societies’
Gardens, will this year adopt the A.N.A. idea of
a festival at the Exhibition Building. Sports and
joys of all descriptions are promised by the manage-
ment, also the inevitable Art Union is to be thrown
in free of charge. Each shilling-admission ticket
offers a chance of winning one of a hundred, or so,
prizes, ranging from .£1000 to a fruit-dish. A
rush for these attractive investments has already
set in. The Demonstration is dated April 21st.,
and the person fated to win £ 1000 at one fell
swoop can prepare to receive -the cash on May
2nd.
